Section 1: Introduction to General Surgery (Q1–Q5)

1. Swelling not moving in all direction, this means that the swelling originating from:
  • A. Muscle
  • B. Nerve
  • C. Bone
  • D. Skin
Diagnosis:
  • Rigid structure (bone) swellings → Completely fixed to skeletal system
    • Physical palpation → Immobile in any plane or direction
  • Exclusions:
    • Skin (D) swellings → Move in all directions with skin
    • Muscle (A) swellings → Move transversely when relaxed, fixed upon contraction
    • Nerves (B) → Move perpendicular to axis, immobile along length
2. Swelling in the right anticupital fossa, it is important to ask about:
  • A. Diarrhea
  • B. Dyspnea
  • C. Dementia
  • D. Hand movement.
Anatomy & Clinical Evaluation:
  • Antecubital fossa → Contains brachial artery, median nerve, radial nerve
    • Local swelling → Compression of neurovascular structures
    • Assessment → Evaluation of motor/sensory status (hand movement)
  • Exclusions:
    • Diarrhea (A), Dyspnea (B), Dementia (C) → Systemic conditions unrelated to local upper extremity lesions
3. The possible associated symptom with abdominal pain:
  • A. Claudication
  • B. Tremors
  • C. Hematuria
  • D. Headache
Diagnosis:
  • Abdominal pain → Potential urological origin
    • Nephrolithiasis or urinary tract infection → Elicit flank/loin pain
    • Associated sign → Hematuria (blood in urine)
  • Exclusions:
    • Claudication (A) → Lower extremity ischemia during exertion
    • Tremors (B), Headache (D) → Neurological manifestations unrelated to acute visceral abdominal pathways
4. The possible symptom of brain metastases:
  • A. Jaundice
  • B. Dyspnea
  • C. Hematemesis
  • D. Blurring of vision
Diagnosis:
  • Brain metastases → Elevate intracranial pressure or compress visual pathways
    • Clinical manifestation → Blurring of vision
  • Exclusions:
    • Jaundice (A) → Hepatic/biliary dysfunction
    • Dyspnea (B) → Cardiopulmonary pathology
    • Hematemesis (C) → Upper gastrointestinal bleeding
5. Abscess on palpation is
  • A. Red
  • B. Tender
  • C. Painful
  • D. Yellow
Clinical Signs:
  • Abscess palpation → Localized exquisite tenderness
    • High internal pressure of pus + inflammatory mediators → Tender to touch
  • Exclusions:
    • Red (A), Yellow (D) → Inspection findings, not palpation
    • Painful (C) → Subjective symptom reported by patient, not physical sign elicited by examiner

Section 2: Wounds, Tissue Repair & Wound Care (Q6–Q60)

6. Incised wounds:
  • a. Heal faster than lacerated wounds.
  • b. Are more likely to injure deeper structures than missile wounds.
  • c. The accompanying shock waves produce extensive local damage.
  • d. Treatment is by hemostasis and leaving the wound open to drain.
Pathophysiology:
  • Incised wound → Clean, regular margins with minimal tissue devitalization
    • Healing → Primary intention → Fast rate
  • Exclusions:
    • Missile wounds (b) → Higher kinetic energy → Severe deep structure damage
    • Shock waves (c) → High-velocity missile injuries, not cold steel blades
    • Treatment (d) → Primary closure, not left open to drain
7. Regarding the causative traumatic agent, the following statements are correct, except:
  • a. The extent of tissue damage by a missile depends upon its weight.
  • b. The extent of tissue damage by a missile is proportionate to its velocity.
  • c. Stab wounds are inflicted by knives and may injure viscera.
  • d. Stab wounds usually fracture bones and thus add to tissue damage.
Pathophysiology:
  • Stab wound → Low-energy penetrating trauma by sharp object
    • Physical effect → Rarely fractures bone
  • Exclusions:
    • Missile weight (a) & velocity (b) → Directly proportional to tissue damage (KE = 0.5 * m * v^2)
    • Stab wounds (c) → Knife injury → Can easily penetrate deep viscera
8. The injuries that are induced by high velocity missiles are attributed to all the following, except:
  • a. Passage of the missile in its track through the tissues.
  • b. Sonic waves.
  • c. Cavitation effect.
  • d. Neatly slice the tissues.
Pathophysiology:
  • High-velocity missile → Severe tearing, crushing, bursting forces
    • Physical effect → Irregular massive tissue damage, never neat slicing
  • Exclusions:
    • Missile track (a), Sonic waves (b), Cavitation (c) → Primary physical mechanisms of high-velocity projectile trauma
9. The peak number of fibroblasts in a healing wound occurs:
  • A. 2 days after injury
  • B. 6 days after injury
  • C. 15 days after injury
  • D. 60 days after injury
Pathophysiology:
  • Fibroblastic phase → Proliferative stage of tissue repair
    • Fibroblast migration → Peak population at day 6 post-injury
  • Exclusions:
    • Day 2 (A) → Acute inflammatory phase
    • Day 15 (C), Day 60 (D) → Maturation/remodeling phase; cellularity decreases
10. Macrophages are present in the wound starting on the 4th day after injury until the wound is completely healed. The primary function of macrophages in wound healing is:
  • A. Intracellular killing of bacteria
  • B. Collagen production
  • C. Activation of cell proliferation
  • D. Modulation of the wound environment
Pathophysiology:
  • Macrophages in healing → Primary orchestrators of transition to proliferative phase
    • Function → Release cytokines/growth factors (TGF-b, PDGF) → Activation of cell proliferation
  • Exclusions:
    • Intracellular killing (A) → Primary role of early PMNs
    • Collagen production (B) → Synthesized directly by fibroblasts
    • Environment modulation (D) → General function, secondary to cell activation coordination
11. The first cells to migrate into a wound are:
  • A. Macrophages
  • B. T lymphocytes
  • C. PMNs
  • D. Fibroblasts
Pathophysiology:
  • Early wound healing → Immediate chemotactic response
    • First cellular wave → PMNs → Peak at 24–48 hours
  • Exclusions:
    • Macrophages (A) → Arrive later, peak at 48–72 hours
    • T lymphocytes (B), Fibroblasts (D) → Migrate during proliferative phase
12. There are 18 types of collagens in the human body. Which two are the most important in wound healing?
  • A. Type I and III
  • B. Type III and VIII
  • C. Type II and X
  • D. Type VI and XII
Pathophysiology:
  • Collagen in tissue repair:
    • Early granulation phase → Rapid deposition of Type III collagen
    • Maturation phase → Replaced by stronger Type I collagen
    • Healing outcome → Driven primarily by Type I and Type III collagens
  • Exclusions:
    • Type II, VI, VIII, X, XII (B, C, D) → Specialized structural collagens, minimal role in general wound repair
13. The tensile strength of a completely healed wound approaches the strength of uninjured tissue:
  • A. 2 weeks after injury
  • B. 3 months after injury
  • C. 12 months after injury
  • D. Never
Pathophysiology:
  • Scar tissue maturation:
    • Healed wound tensile strength → Maximum ~80% of uninjured tissue
    • Ultimate strength recovery → Never approaches 100% of intact tissue
  • Exclusions:
    • 2 weeks (A), 3 months (B), 12 months (C) → Wound strength is sub-maximal, never fully recovers to baseline
14. How does re-epithelization take in a well-approximated surgical wound?
  • A. 2 days
  • B. 1 week
  • C. 2 weeks
  • D. 1 month
Pathophysiology:
  • Well-approximated wound → Clean, minimal distance between margins
    • Epithelial bridge → Rapid migration of keratinocytes across gap → Completed in 2 days
  • Exclusions:
    • 1 week (B), 2 weeks (C), 1 month (D) → Overly prolonged timelines, indicating complications or secondary healing
15. About healing by secondary intention, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. Wound infection leads to healing by secondary intention.
  • B. It is a slow process.
  • C. It ultimately produces a neat scar.
  • D. Wound contraction is involved in this type of healing.
Pathophysiology:
  • Secondary intention healing → Wound left open to heal by granulation and contraction
    • Consequence → Promotes large, irregular, unsightly scar
  • Exclusions:
    • Wound infection (A) → Prevents primary closure, forces secondary intention
    • Healing speed (B) → Highly prolonged process due to tissue volume gap
    • Wound contraction (D) → Mandatory physical mechanism to reduce open area
16. Which of the following cells or blood elements play a role in the initial phases of wound healing?
  • A. Polymorphonuclear leukocytes (PMNs)
  • B. Macrophages.
  • C. Lymphocytes
  • D. All the above.
Pathophysiology:
  • Initial healing phase → Inflammatory cascade
    • Cellular participants → PMNs, macrophages, lymphocytes → Coordinate immune response & debris clearance
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Individual cells that are all essential parts of early healing, making "All the above" (D) correct
17. The following statements regarding the role of macrophages in the wound healing process are true EXCEPT:
  • A. Macrophages are the dominant cell type during the inflammatory phase of wound healing.
  • B. Macrophages are essential for wound healing
  • C. The macrophage role in wound healing is limited to phagocytosis
  • D. macrophages are a source of several humoral factors essential for wound healing
Pathophysiology:
  • Macrophages → Highly active, regulatory secretory cells
    • Role in healing → Not limited to phagocytosis → Crucial source of growth factors/cytokines
  • Exclusions:
    • Statement A → Dominant cell of late inflammatory phase
    • Statement B → Absolutely essential for survival/coordination of healing
    • Statement D → Key source of humoral repair factors
18. Concerning the remodeling phase of wound healing choose the correct answer:
  • A. Total collagen content increases steadily through this phase.
  • B. The normal adult ratio of collagen is approximately 4: 1 of type I to type III collagen is restored.
  • C. Eventually a scar will achieve the strength of unwounded skin.
  • D. The normal adult ratio of collagen is approximately 4: 1 of type III to type I collagen is restored.
Pathophysiology:
  • Remodeling phase:
    • Remodeling mechanism → Degradation of Type III collagen → Replaced by Type I
    • Physiological target → Restores normal adult ratio of 4:1 (Type I to Type III)
  • Exclusions:
    • Total collagen (A) → Does not increase steadily; plateaus or decreases
    • Strength recovery (C) → Never equals 100% of intact skin
    • Choice D → Falsely reverses ratio (stating 4:1 Type III to Type I)
19. Which of the following is FALSE regarding polymorphonuclear neutrophils (PMNs) and their role in wound healing?
  • A. PMNs release proteases that degrade ground substance within the wound site.
  • B. Neutrophils use fibrin clot generated at the wound site as scaffolding for migration into the wound.
  • C. Neutrophil migration is stimulated by local prostaglandins, complement factors, interleukin-1 (IL-l), tumor necrosis factor-a (TNF-a), transforming growth factor-b (TGF-b),platelet factor 4, or bacterial products.
  • D. PMNs are the first cells to infiltrate the wound, peaking at 24 to 48 hours.
  • E. Neutrophils release cytokines that later assist with collagen deposition and epithelial closure.
Pathophysiology:
  • PMNs (Neutrophils) → Acute inflammatory responders
    • False role → Do not secrete cytokines for collagen deposition or epithelial closure (role of macrophages)
  • Exclusions:
    • Statement A → Release MMPs to degrade necrotic tissue
    • Statement B → Migrate along fibrin clot scaffolding
    • Statement C → Recruited by local chemotactic factors
    • Statement D → First cells to infiltrate, peaking at 24–48 hours
20. The proliferative phase of wound healing occurs how long after the injury?
  • A. Day 1
  • B. Day 2
  • C. Day 7
  • D. Day 14
Pathophysiology:
  • Proliferative phase:
    • Timeline → Starts around day 3-4, peaking around day 7 post-injury with active granulation and collagen synthesis
  • Exclusions:
    • Day 1 (A), Day 2 (B) → Dominated by acute hemostasis and early inflammatory phase
    • Day 14 (D) → Late stage of proliferation transitioning into early maturation/remodeling
21. Which of the following is true regarding the fibroblastic phase of wound healing?
  • A. Early during wound healing, the predominant composition of the matrix is fibronectin and type II collagen.
  • B. After complete replacement of the scar with type III collagen, the mechanical strength will equal that of uninjured tissue approximately 6 to 12 months postinjury.
  • C. Even though the tensile strength of a wound reaches a plateau after several weeks, the tensile strength will increase over another 6 to 12 months due to fibril formation and cross-linking.
  • D. As the scar matures, matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) break down type I collagen and replace it with type III collagen.
Pathophysiology:
  • Fibroblastic/Remodeling phase:
    • Long-term strength gains → Collagen fibril reorganization and enzymatic cross-linking → Continues over 6–12 months
  • Exclusions:
    • Early matrix (A) → Composed of fibronectin and Type III collagen, not Type II
    • Strength recovery (B) → Never equals uninjured tissue
    • Mature scar (D) → MMPs replace Type III with Type I, not Type I with Type III
22. Which of the following is FALSE regarding healing of full thickness injuries of the GI tract?
  • A. Serosal healing is essential to form a water-tight barrier to the lumen of the bowel.
  • B. Extraperitoneal segments of bowel that lack serosa have higher rates of anastomotic failure.
  • C. There is an early decrease in marginal strength due to an imbalance of greater collagen lysis versus collagen synthesis.
  • D. Collagen synthesis is done by fibroblast and smooth muscle cells.
  • E. The greatest tensile strength of the GI tract is provided by the serosa.
Gastrointestinal Pathophysiology:
  • GI tract mechanical strength:
    • Layer of greatest tensile strength → Submucosa (dense collagen network), not serosa
  • Exclusions:
    • Serosa (A) → Critical for rapid water-tight peritoneal seal
    • Extraperitoneal segments (B) → Lack serosa (e.g., lower rectum) → Higher leak rate
    • Early phase (C) → Collagen lysis > collagen synthesis → Temporary drop in strength
    • Collagen synthesis (D) → Fibroblasts and smooth muscle cells are key producers
23. Which type of collagen is most important in wound healing?
  • A. Type III
  • B. Type V
  • C. Type VII
  • D. Type XI
Pathophysiology:
  • Early soft tissue repair:
    • Predominant collagen laid down by fibroblasts → Type III collagen (found in granulation tissue)
  • Exclusions:
    • Type V (B), Type VII (C), Type XI (D) → Minor anchoring/specialized collagens with minimal roles in acute soft tissue union
24. Healing by first intention means:
  • A. Using catgut.
  • B. Union between two edges of an incision without subsequent breakdown.
  • C. Subsequent breakdown.
  • D. The immediate use of a plastic dressing
Clinical Definition:
  • First intention healing → Primary wound closure
    • Mechanism → Immediate approximation of clean edges → Direct union without subsequent breakdown
  • Exclusions:
    • Catgut (A) → Specific suture material, not part of definition
    • Subsequent breakdown (C) → Clinically represents wound dehiscence
    • Plastic dressing (D) → Supportive dressing choice, not a biological healing definition
25. When a long bone fracture is repaired by internal fixation with plates and screws:
  • a. Callus at the fracture site forms more rapidly.
  • b. Delayed union is prevented.
  • c. Direct bone-to-bone healing occurs without soft callus formation.
  • d. Endochondral ossification is more complete.
Orthopedic Pathophysiology:
  • Rigid internal fixation (plates and screws):
    • Bone healing pathway → Complete elimination of micro-motion → Primary/direct bone healing without soft callus formation
  • Exclusions:
    • Callus formation (a), Endochondral ossification (d) → Suppressed by absolute stability, occurs only in secondary healing
    • Delayed union (b) → Can still occur if vascularity or surgical technique is poor
26. What type of nerve injury involves disruption of axonal continuity with preserved Schwann cell basal lamina?
  • a. Neurapraxia
  • b. Axonotmesis
  • c. Neurotmesis
  • d. Axon lysis
Pathophysiology:
  • Nerve injury classification:
    • Axonotmesis → Disruption of axon/myelin with preserved connective tissue framework (Schwann cell basal lamina)
  • Exclusions:
    • Neurapraxia (a) → Temporary conduction block without anatomical damage
    • Neurotmesis (c) → Complete transection of axon and all supporting connective tissue layers
27. What is FALSE regarding healing of cartilage?
  • a. Cartilage is avascular and depends on diffusion of nutrients.
  • b. Superficial cartilage wounds are not associated with an inflammatory response.
  • c. Cartilage injuries often heal slowly and result in permanent structural defects.
  • d. A major source of nutrients to cartilage is from nearby periosteum.
Pathophysiology:
  • Cartilage biology:
    • Nutritional source → Avascular tissue dependent on diffusion from perichondrium, not periosteum
  • Exclusions:
    • Statement a → True; lacks direct blood vessels
    • Statement b → True; superficial defects do not touch blood supply, triggering no inflammation
    • Statement c → True; poor vascularity leads to slow healing and permanent defects
28. Which phase of healing is most affected by exogenous corticosteroids?
  • A. Initial phase of cell migration and angiogenesis
  • B. Proliferative phase
  • C. Maturation
  • D. Scar remodeling
Pharmacology & Pathophysiology:
  • Exogenous corticosteroids:
    • Primary target in wound healing → Suppression of early inflammatory response, monocyte/PMN migration, and early angiogenesis
  • Exclusions:
    • Proliferative (B), Maturation (C), Remodeling (D) → Downstream phases affected, but early inflammation/angiogenesis is the most severely blunted
29. Which of the following should be given to promote wound healing in patients receiving corticosteroids?
  • A. Vitamin A
  • B. Vitamin B1
  • C. Vitamin B2
  • D. Vitamin C
Pharmacology:
  • Systemic steroids → Suppress early inflammation and healing
    • Antidote → Vitamin A administration → Partially reverses steroid-induced healing inhibition
  • Exclusions:
    • Vitamins B1 (B), B2 (C), C (D) → Critical general cofactors but do not reverse glucocorticoid-mediated healing suppression
30. A 43-year-old window cleaner fell off a scaffold. He sustained an open wound on the right leg. Debridement was carried out in the emergency department, and the edges of the wound were left open. The wound measures 4 cm × 6 cm. What is TRUE of wound contraction?
  • A. It occurs within 12 hours of injury.
  • B. It is more prominent over the tibia than gluteal region.
  • C. It is accelerated if wound is excised 3 days after injury.
  • D. It is experimentally less affected by excision of tissue from center of wound rather than at the periphery.
Pathophysiology:
  • Wound contraction mechanics:
    • Driver → Myofibroblasts situated at active wound margins (periphery)
    • Experimental finding → Excision of center has minimal effect, excision of periphery stops contraction
  • Exclusions:
    • Timeline (A) → Begins around day 4-5, not 12 hours
    • Anatomy (B) → Very poor over tight bony areas like the tibia
    • Excision on day 3 (C) → Delays contraction by resetting inflammatory phase
31. Which factor is least likely to inhibit wound contraction?
  • A. Radiation
  • B. Cytolytic drug
  • C. Transformation growth factor b
  • D. Full-thickness skin graft
Pathophysiology:
  • Exam Key Answer → Full-thickness skin graft (D)
  • Medical Clarification Note:
    • Medical reality → Full-thickness skin grafts are used to *maximally* inhibit contraction; TGF-b (C) actually *promotes* contraction (making it biologically the least likely to inhibit). Follow your university's answer key!
  • Exclusions:
    • Radiation (A), Cytolytic drugs (B) → Arrest active fibroblast/myofibroblast activity, inhibiting contraction
32. The most important factor that affects wound healing is:
  • A. Wound closure within 2 hours of injury.
  • B. Proper choice of suture material.
  • C. Daily change of wound dressing.
  • D. Adequate vascularity of the edges.
Pathophysiology:
  • Edge vascularity:
    • Clinical priority → Adequate blood supply to wound margins → Delivers oxygen, nutrients, and immune cells
  • Exclusions:
    • Speed of closure (A), Suture choice (B), Dressing changes (C) → Secondary factors that fail in the presence of tissue ischemia
33. The most important factor that affects wound healing is: (Duplicate Question)
  • A. Wound closure within 2 hours of injury.
  • B. Proper choice of suture material.
  • C. Daily change of wound dressing.
  • D. Adequate vascularity of the edges.
Pathophysiology:
  • Edge vascularity:
    • Clinical priority → Adequate blood supply to wound margins → Delivers oxygen, nutrients, and immune cells
  • Exclusions: Same as Q32.
34. Which of the following influences wound dehiscence:
  • A. Type of incision.
  • B. Type of closure.
  • C. Anemia-hypoproteinemia.
  • D. All of the above.
Pathophysiology:
  • Wound dehiscence risk factors:
    • Etiology → Incision type + closure method + systemic protein/nutritional status
  • Exclusions:
    • Since A, B, and C all independently contribute to fascial/cutaneous separation, "All of the above" (D) is correct
35. Steroids are responsible for all the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Decreased wound tensile strength.
  • B. GI ulceration.
  • C. Gall stone formation.
  • D. Avascular necrosis of hip.
Pharmacology:
  • Systemic corticosteroid side effects:
    • Excluded complication → Gallstone formation
  • Exclusions:
    • Decreased tensile strength (A) → Due to inhibited collagen synthesis
    • GI ulceration (B) → Due to suppressed protective mucosal prostaglandins
    • Avascular necrosis (D) → Due to femoral head microvascular ischemia
36. Factors that decrease collagen synthesis include all the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Hypoproteinemia
  • B. Infection.
  • C. Anemia.
  • D. Advanced age
Pathophysiology:
  • Collagen synthesis determinants:
    • Non-inhibitory factor → Mild-to-moderate anemia, provided perfusion is maintained
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypoproteinemia (A) → Deprives building blocks
    • Infection (B) → Bacterial collagenases degrade newly formed collagen
    • Advanced age (D) → Slower baseline metabolic rate and protein synthesis
37. Which of the following is commonly seen in Ehlers Danlos syndrome (EDS)?
  • A. Small bowel obstructions.
  • B. Spontaneous thrombosis.
  • C. Direct or recurrent hernias in children.
  • D. Abnormal scarring of the hands with contractures.
Pathophysiology:
  • Ehlers-Danlos Syndrome (EDS):
    • Structural vulnerability → Defective collagen cross-linking/synthesis → Tissue laxity and fragility
    • Pediatric presentation → Direct or recurrent hernias
  • Exclusions:
    • Small bowel obstruction (A), Spontaneous thrombosis (B), Hand contractures (D) → Not classic manifestations of EDS collagen laxity
38. Patients with Marfan syndrome are associated with what genetic defect?
  • A. MFN-1 gene deletion
  • B. Type I collagen gene mutation
  • C. COL7A 1 gene mutation
  • D. FBN-1 gene mutation 8
Pathophysiology:
  • Marfan syndrome:
    • Etiology → Autosomal dominant mutation of FBN1 gene (Chromosome 15) → Defective fibrillin-1 (elastic fiber component)
  • Exclusions:
    • MFN-1 (A) → Deletion unrelated
    • Type I collagen (B) → Osteogenesis Imperfecta
    • COL7A1 (C) → Dystrophic Epidermolysis Bullosa
39. Steroids impair wound healing by:
  • A. Decreasing angiogenesis and macrophage migration
  • B. Decreasing platelet plug integrity
  • C. Increasing release of lysosomal enzymes
  • D. Increasing fibrinolysis
Pharmacology:
  • Corticosteroids:
    • Primary anti-healing mechanism → Suppresses inflammation → Decreased angiogenesis and impaired macrophage migration
  • Exclusions:
    • Platelet plug (B) → Primary hemostasis is unaffected
    • Lysosomes (C), Fibrinolysis (D) → Not the primary drivers of steroid-induced healing failure
40. The major cause of impaired wound healing is
  • A. Anemia
  • B. Diabetes mellitus
  • C. Local tissue infection
  • D. Malnutrition
Pathophysiology:
  • Wound healing impairment:
    • Most common local cause → Local tissue infection
  • Exclusions:
    • Anemia (A), Diabetes (B), Malnutrition (D) → Important systemic factors, but localized bacterial contamination is the most prevalent obstacle to closure
41. How does diabetes mellitus impair wound healing?
  • A. Local hypoxemia, reduced angiogenesis, and inflammation due to vascular disease.
  • B. Glycosylation of proteoglycans and collagen in wound bed due to hyperglycemia.
  • C. Decreased collagen accretion noted in patients with type II diabetes mellitus.
  • D. Increased bacterial load due to hyperglycemia.
Pathophysiology:
  • Diabetes mellitus:
    • Healing impairment mechanism → Microvascular and macrovascular disease → Localized tissue hypoxemia, blunted angiogenesis, and chronic non-resolving inflammation
  • Exclusions:
    • Glycosylation (B), Decreased collagen (C), Bacterial load (D) → Occur, but tissue-level hypoxia and ischemia are the main drivers of chronic diabetic ulcers
42. Supplementation of which of the following micronutrients improves wound healing in patients without micronutrient deficiency?
  • A. Vitamin C
  • B. Vitamin A
  • C. Selenium
  • D. Zinc
Pharmacology:
  • Micronutrients in wound healing:
    • Suppressed steroid/diabetic wounds → Vitamin A reverses impairment even in non-deficient individuals
  • Exclusions:
    • Vitamin C (A), Selenium (C), Zinc (D) → Essential cofactors but do not accelerate healing above baseline in non-deficient patients
43. Postoperative wound infection can produce the following, except:
  • A. Stitch sinus.
  • B. Reactionary hemorrhage.
  • C. Septicemia.
  • D. Incisional hernia.
Pathophysiology:
  • Post-operative hemorrhage:
    • Reactionary hemorrhage → Occurs within first 24 hours (slipped ligature, spike in blood pressure), not caused by later wound infection (which peaks at 3–5 days)
  • Exclusions:
    • Stitch sinus (A), Septicemia (C), Incisional hernia (D) → Classic medium-to-long-term complications of postoperative wound infection
44. About scars, all the following are true, except:
  • a. Contracture impairs joint movement.
  • b. Squamous cell carcinoma may arise on top of a scar.
  • c. A scar regains the original tissue tensile strength in 6 months.
  • d. A superficial burn leaves no scar.
Pathophysiology:
  • Scar tissue:
    • False statement → A scar regains original tissue tensile strength in 6 months (never regains 100%; maxes at ~80% by 1 year)
  • Exclusions:
    • Joint contractures (a) → True; restricts joint range of motion
    • Squamous cell carcinoma (b) → True; Marjolin's ulcer can develop in chronic scars
    • Superficial burns (d) → True; epidermal-only burns heal by regeneration, leaving no scar
45. A keloid:
  • a. Is more frequently seen in black people than Caucasians.
  • b. Never follows a puncture wound.
  • c. Stops growing after 6 months.
  • d. Never develops in a sternotomy incision.
Pathophysiology:
  • Keloid:
    • Predisposition → Genetic susceptibility, highly prevalent in darker pigmented ethnicities (black individuals)
  • Exclusions:
    • Puncture wounds (b) → Can develop from any minor skin trauma
    • 6 months (c) → Grow continuously and do not regress
    • Sternotomy (d) → Highly prone to keloids due to chest wall tension
46. Signs of malignant transformation in a chronic wound include
  • a. Persistent granulation tissue with bleeding
  • b. Overturned wound edges
  • c. Nonhealing after 2 weeks of therapy
  • d. Distal edema
Pathophysiology:
  • Chronic wound malignant transformation (Marjolin's ulcer):
    • Diagnostic sign → Everted, overturned, or rolled wound edges
  • Exclusions:
    • Granulation tissue (a) → Normal component of healing
    • 2 weeks (c) → Far too short; malignant changes require years of chronic inflammation
    • Distal edema (d) → Indicates venous/lymphatic congestion, not neoplasia
47. What is the difference between hypertrophic scars (HTS) and keloids?
  • a. Keloids are an overabundance of fibroplasia because of healing, hypertrophic scars are a failure of collagen remodeling.
  • b. Hypertrophic scars often regress over time, whereas keloids rarely regress.
  • c. Hypertrophic scars are more common in darker pigmented ethnicities.
  • d. Hypertrophic scars extend beyond the border of the original wound.
Pathophysiology:
  • Hypertrophic Scars (HTS) vs Keloids:
    • Key distinction → HTS stay within original boundaries and often regress/flatten over time; keloids extend beyond borders and rarely regress
  • Exclusions:
    • Both involve overabundant collagen (a)
    • Keloids (not HTS) are more common in darker skin (c) and extend beyond original wound margins (d)
48. The treatment of choice for keloids is
  • a. Excision alone
  • b. Excision with adjuvant therapy (e.g., radiation)
  • c. Pressure treatment
  • d. Intralesional injection of steroids
Clinical Management:
  • Keloid therapy:
    • Standard of care → Surgical excision combined with adjuvant therapy (radiation, intralesional steroids, or silicone sheeting) to prevent extremely high (~80%+) recurrence
  • Exclusions:
    • Excision alone (a) → Almost guaranteed recurrence
    • Pressure (c), Steroids alone (d) → Ineffective for large, established keloid lesions
49. What is FALSE about peritoneal adhesions?
  • a. Most peritoneal adhesions are a result of intraabdominal surgery.
  • b. Intra-abdominal adhesions are the most common cause of small bowel obstruction.
  • c. Operations in the upper abdomen have a higher chance of causing adhesions that cause small bowel obstruction, especially involving the jejunum.
  • d. Adhesions are a leading cause of secondary infertility in women.
Pathophysiology:
  • Peritoneal adhesions:
    • False statement → Upper abdominal surgery has a higher rate of SBO-causing adhesions (lower abdominal/pelvic surgeries carry the highest risk)
  • Exclusions:
    • Statement a → True; most adhesions result from prior abdominal operations
    • Statement b → True; adhesions are the leading cause of mechanical SBO
    • Statement d → True; pelvic adhesions cause secondary tubal infertility
50. About wound management, one statement only of the following is correct:
  • A. Debridement is the removal of dead tissues and foreign bodies.
  • B. Debridement is necessary for cleaning incised wounds to get good drainage.
  • C. Nerve repair in lacerated crushed wounds is of priority.
  • D. In fresh clean-cut wounds mark the cut tendons for delayed treatment.
Clinical Management:
  • Surgical debridement:
    • Definition → Mechanical removal of non-viable (necrotic) tissue, foreign bodies, and debris to optimize the wound bed
  • Exclusions:
    • Clean incised wounds (B) → Do not require debridement
    • Crushed wounds (C) → Nerve repair is delayed, not a primary emergency
    • Clean-cut wounds (D) → Primary tendon repair is preferred over marking for delayed repair
51. Factors that make lacerated wounds more liable to infection include the following, except:
  • a. The presence of foreign bodies.
  • b. Ischemic or dead tissues.
  • c. Suturing the wound under tension.
  • d. Leaving the wound open after debridement.
Pathophysiology:
  • Lacerated wound infection:
    • Non-risk factor → Leaving the wound open (delayed primary closure) after debridement reduces anaerobic growth and infection risk
  • Exclusions:
    • Foreign bodies (a), Ischemic tissue (b), Suture tension (c) → Compromise tissue perfusion and provide substrates for bacterial growth
52. Delayed primary closure of the skin means:
  • A. Closure of the skin by sub-cuticular sutures.
  • B. Skin closure on the 12th day after surgery.
  • C. Skin closure when pus stops to drain from the wound.
  • D. Skin closure on the 5th day.
Clinical Definition:
  • Delayed primary closure:
    • Protocol → Leaving a contaminated wound open to heal initially, then closing the skin on the 5th day
  • Exclusions:
    • Sub-cuticular sutures (A) → Standard primary closure technique
    • 12 days (B) → Classed as secondary closure
    • Pus resolution (C) → Describes secondary closure of an actively infected wound
53. For contaminated incised wounds of 18 hours duration one choice is correct:
  • A. Wound irrigation and delayed primary closure are preferred.
  • B. Urgent repair of cut tendons provides the best results.
  • C. Immediate repair of cut nerves by nerve graft is advised:
  • D. Extensive debridement of muscles is necessary.
Clinical Management:
  • Late contaminated wound (18 hours old):
    • Management strategy → Past safe window for immediate primary closure → Thorough irrigation and delayed primary closure are preferred
  • Exclusions:
    • Immediate tendon (B) or nerve (C) repairs → Contraindicated due to high risk of infection
    • Extensive muscle debridement (D) → Only non-viable muscle should be debrided
54. Factors that make lacerated wounds liable to infection include the following, except: (Duplicate Question)
  • A. The presence of foreign bodies.
  • B. Ischemic or dead tissues.
  • C. The suture of the wound under tension.
  • D. Leaving the wound open after debridement.
Pathophysiology:
  • Lacerated wound infection:
    • Non-risk factor → Leaving the wound open (delayed primary closure) after debridement reduces anaerobic growth and infection risk
  • Exclusions: Same as Q51.
55. Which growth factor has been formulated and approved for treatment of diabetic foot ulcers?
  • A. PDGF
  • B. IGF-111
  • C. IL-8
  • D. Keratinocyte growth factor
Pharmacology:
  • Diabetic foot ulcer therapy:
    • Approved biological product → rhPDGF / Becaplermin
  • Exclusions:
    • IGF-111 (B), IL-8 (C), KGF (D) → Not approved or formulated as standalone topical therapies for diabetic ulcers
56. An alginate dressing is best used in which of the following wounds?
  • a. An open traumatic wound
  • b. An open surgical wound
  • c. An infected wound
  • d. A partial thickness burn wound
Clinical Management:
  • Alginate dressings:
    • Indication → Open, heavily exudating surgical wounds (seaweed-derived fibers gel upon contact with fluid, maintaining moisture)
  • Exclusions:
    • Dry traumatic wounds (a), partial-thickness burns (d) → Lacks sufficient exudate to activate alginate gelation
    • Active untreated infection (c) → Requires drainage and antimicrobial management
57. The following are absorbable sutures EXCEPT:
  • a. Catgut.
  • b. Polypropylene (prolene).
  • c. Polydioxanone (PDS).
  • d. Polyglactin (Vicryl).
Suture Material:
  • Absorbable sutures:
    • Excluded material → Polypropylene (Prolene) is a non-absorbable monofilament
  • Exclusions:
    • Catgut (a) → Natural absorbable
    • PDS (c) → Synthetic slow-absorbable monofilament
    • Vicryl (d) → Synthetic absorbable braided
58. A four-layer suture repair of an inguinal hernia is a:
  • a. Pott's repair
  • b. Shouldice repair
  • c. Mc Vay repair
  • d. Lichtenstein repair
Surgical Technique:
  • Inguinal hernia repair:
    • Shouldice repair → Imbricating transversalis fascia in a continuous four-layer tissue suture pattern
  • Exclusions:
    • McVay (c) → Suturing transversus aponeurosis to Cooper's ligament
    • Lichtenstein (d) → Mesh repair, not a primary four-layer tissue suture repair
59. For closure of a midline abdominal incision, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. Fascial sutures should be placed at least 1cm from the edge.
  • B. Peritoneal closure does not add to the wound's tensile strength.
  • C. Fascial sutures should be tied under tension in order to avoid burst abdomen.
  • D. It can be closed by either interrupted or continuous fascial sutures.
Surgical Technique:
  • Midline fascial closure:
    • False statement → Fascial sutures should be tied under tension to avoid burst abdomen (sutures must be tied snug, not tight, to prevent tissue ischemia and cut-through)
  • Exclusions:
    • Statement A → True; sutures should be placed at least 1 cm from fascial edge
    • Statement B → True; peritoneal closure adds no tensile strength
    • Statement D → True; can be closed with continuous or interrupted monofilament
60. For hernia repair the best suture material to be used is:
  • A. Polypropylene as it is non-absorbable.
  • B. Plain catgut as it is resistant to infection.
  • C. Silk as it is monofilament.
  • D. Chromic catgut because it is easily absorbable.
Suture Material:
  • Hernia repair:
    • Suture of choice → Polypropylene (Prolene) due to permanent, non-absorbable tensile strength and low-reactivity monofilament profile
  • Exclusions:
    • Catgut (B, D) → Absorbable; loses strength in weeks, causing high recurrence
    • Silk (C) → Braided multifilament; can harbor bacteria, leading to chronic suture sinuses

Section 3: Shock (Q61–Q87)

61. Shock can best be defined as:
  • a. Hypotension.
  • b. Hypoperfusion of tissues.
  • c. Hypoxemia.
  • d. Hypothermia.
Pathophysiology:
  • Shock definition → Systemic state of low tissue perfusion
    • Oxygen delivery falls below metabolic demand → Cellular dysfunction
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypotension (a) → Frequently accompanies shock, but shock can present with normal blood pressure (compensated shock)
    • Hypoxemia (c) → Indicates low arterial blood oxygen levels, which is respiratory, not circulatory per se
    • Hypothermia (d) → Refers to decreased core temperature, which may occur in shock but does not define it
62. What is true about baroreceptors?
  • a. Volume receptors can be activated in hemorrhage with reduction in left atrial pressure.
  • b. Receptors in the aortic arch and carotid bodies inhibit the autonomic nervous system (ANS) when stretched.
  • c. When baroreceptors are stretched, they induced increased ANS output and produce constriction of peripheral vessels.
  • d. None of the above.
Pathophysiology:
  • Baroreceptors are stretch receptors → Increase firing rate when blood pressure rises
    • Increased stretch → Inhibits sympathetic output and activates vagal (parasympathetic) tone → Lowers heart rate and vasodilates
  • Exclusions:
    • Volume receptors (a) → Primarily located in the right atrium and vena cava, not left atrium
    • Stretched baroreceptors (c) → Induce *decreased* sympathetic ANS output and vasodilatation to reduce blood pressure
63. Chemoreceptors in the aorta and carotid bodies do NOT sense which of the following?
  • a. Changes in O2 tension
  • b. H+ ion concentration
  • c. HCO3- concentration
  • d. Carbon dioxide (CO) levels
Pathophysiology:
  • Peripheral chemoreceptors → Detect arterial oxygen tension (PaO2), carbon dioxide (PaCO2), and hydrogen ion concentrations (pH)
    • Physiological role → Regulate ventilation in response to acid-base and gas imbalances
  • Exclusions:
    • Bicarbonate (c) → Not directly sensed by peripheral chemoreceptors; alterations in HCO3- affect pH, which is what is registered
    • Oxygen (a), Hydrogen (b), and Carbon Dioxide (d) → Directly sensed to adjust respiratory drive
64. Which of the following is true about antidiuretic hormone (ADH) production in injured patients?
  • a. ADH acts as a potent mesenteric vasoconstrictor.
  • b. ADH levels fall to normal within 2 to 3 days of the initial insult.
  • c. ADH decreases hepatic gluconeogenesis.
  • d. ADH secretion is mediated by the renin-angiotensin system.
Pathophysiology:
  • Vasopressin (ADH) release in trauma → Released from posterior pituitary in response to hypovolemia
    • Primary vasoactive effect → Promotes systemic and potent mesenteric vasoconstriction to shunt blood to vital organs
  • Exclusions:
    • Normal levels (b) → Remain elevated for several days following severe ongoing injury or stress
    • Gluconeogenesis (c) → ADH actually *promotes* hepatic gluconeogenesis and glycogenolysis
    • Secretion pathway (d) → Stimulated directly by increased plasma osmolality and baroreceptor pathways, not renin-angiotensin
65. Which of following occur because of epinephrine and norepinephrine?
  • a. Hepatic glycogenolysis
  • b. Hypoglycemia
  • c. Insulin sensitivity
  • d. Lipogenesis
Pathophysiology:
  • Catecholamine surge → Stimulates glycogen breakdown in the liver (glycogenolysis)
    • Physiological goal → Mobilize glucose rapidly for metabolic utilization during stress or shock
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypoglycemia (b) → Epinephrine and norepinephrine cause hyperglycemia
    • Insulin sensitivity (c) → Induces insulin resistance to preserve circulating glucose
    • Lipogenesis (d) → Stimulates lipolysis (fat breakdown) rather than fat storage
66. Which cytokine is anti-inflammatory and increases after shock and trauma?
  • a. Interleukin (IL)-1
  • b. IL-2
  • c. IL-6
  • d. IL-10
Pathophysiology:
  • Interleukin-10 (IL-10) → Major anti-inflammatory cytokine
    • Function → Synthesized to downregulate the release of pro-inflammatory cytokines and prevent systemic hyper-inflammation
  • Exclusions:
    • IL-1 (a) → Highly pro-inflammatory, induces fever and acute phase response
    • IL-2 (b) → Pro-inflammatory, regulates T-cell proliferation
    • IL-6 (c) → Pro-inflammatory mediator that stimulates acute-phase protein synthesis by hepatocytes
67. Tumor necrosis factor-alpha (TNF-a)
  • a. Can be released as a response to bacteria or endotoxin
  • b. Increased more in trauma than septic patients
  • c. Induces procoagulant activity and peripheral vasoconstriction
  • d. Contributes to anemia of chronic illness
Pathophysiology:
  • TNF-a release → Synthesized primarily by activated macrophages
    • Trigger → Strongly induced by lipopolysaccharide (LPS)/endotoxin and Gram-negative bacteria
  • Exclusions:
    • Trauma vs Sepsis (b) → Elevation is markedly higher and more sustained in septic states
    • Coagulant/vascular effects (c) → Induces procoagulant states but causes profound *vasodilation* (hypotension) rather than vasoconstriction
    • Anemia of chronic illness (d) → This is a chronic systemic effect, whereas shock reviews address acute cytokine dynamics
68. What is FALSE about serum lactate?
  • a. Generated from pyruvate in the setting of insufficient O2.
  • b. Metabolized by the liver and kidneys.
  • c. Is an indirect measure of the magnitude and severity of shock.
  • d. The time to peak lactate from admission predicts rates of survival.
Pathophysiology:
  • Serum lactate clearance → The *clearance rate* (time to normalize) predicts survival, not the time to peak lactate
    • Clinical utility → Rapid normalization is associated with improved clinical outcomes
  • Exclusions:
    • Generation (a) → Pyruvate is converted to lactate under anaerobic glycolysis
    • Metabolism (b) → Chiefly cleared by hepatic gluconeogenesis and to a lesser extent by the kidneys
    • Magnitude of shock (c) → Correlation exists between peak lactate levels and cumulative tissue oxygen debt
69. In hemorrhagic shock all the following are present EXCEPT:
  • a. Low cardiac output.
  • b. Decreased venous return.
  • c. Peripheral pooling.
  • d. Increased peripheral resistance.
Pathophysiology:
  • Hemorrhagic (hypovolemic) shock compensatory mechanisms → Vasoconstriction of peripheral vessels
    • Vascular tone → Shift of blood to the core, preventing peripheral pooling
  • Exclusions:
    • Low cardiac output (a) & Decreased venous return (b) → Direct results of reduced blood volume
    • Increased peripheral resistance (d) → Sympathetic action increases systemic vascular resistance (SVR)
70. Class 4 hypovolemic shock is present in presence of all EXCEPT:
  • a. Blood loss more than 40%
  • b. Heart rate > 140 min.
  • c. Urine output 10-15 ml/hr.
  • d. Drowsy, confused patient.
Pathophysiology:
  • Class IV Shock → Characterized by severe, life-threatening hypovolemia (>40% blood loss)
    • Renal blood flow → Profoundly compromised, resulting in near-total anuria (negligible urine output, often < 5 ml/hr)
  • Exclusions:
    • 10-15 ml/hr (c) → This level of oliguria is typical of Class III shock; Class IV has near-zero output
    • Blood loss >40% (a) & HR >140 (b) & Altered mental status (d) → Classic criteria for Class IV hemorrhagic shock
71. When a patient with hemorrhagic shock is resuscitated using an intravenous colloid solution rather than lactated Ringer, all the following are true EXCEPT:
  • a. Circulating levels of immunoglobulins are decreased.
  • b. Colloid solutions may bind to the ionized fraction of serum calcium.
  • c. Endogenous production of albumin is decreased.
  • d. Extracellular fluid volume deficit is restored.
Pathophysiology:
  • Colloid vs Crystalloid → Colloids remain within the intravascular space and do not readily cross into the interstitium
    • Physiological effect → Does not correct the functional extracellular (interstitial) fluid deficit
  • Exclusions:
    • Immunoglobulins (a) → Diluted due to plasma expansion
    • Calcium binding (b) → Exogenous colloids can chelate ionized calcium
    • Albumin production (c) → Infusion of oncotic agents downregulates hepatic synthesis of albumin
72. Which categories of shock are associated with vasodilation of larger arterioles?
  • a. Septic shock
  • b. Cardiogenic shock
  • c. Neurogenic shock
  • d. Septic and neurogenic shock
Pathophysiology:
  • Vasodilatory (distributive) shock states → Characterized by pathologically low systemic vascular resistance
    • Mechanism → Inflammatory cytokine storms (sepsis) or loss of sympathetic vasomotor tone (neurogenic shock) relax larger arterioles
  • Exclusions:
    • Cardiogenic (b) & Hypovolemic shocks → Characterized by intense, compensatory vasoconstriction
73. A patient has a BP of 70/50 mm Hg, serum lactate of 30, CO of 1.9 L/min, and central venous pressure is 2 mm H2O. The most likely diagnosis is:
  • a. Congestive heart failure
  • b. Cardiac tamponade
  • c. Hypovolemic shock
  • d. Septic shock
Diagnosis:
  • Low CVP (2 mm H2O) + Low Cardiac Output (1.9 L/min) + Hypotension (70/50) → Reflects profound intravascular depletion
    • Primary defect → Hypovolemic shock
  • Exclusions:
    • CHF (a) & Tamponade (b) → Would present with elevated CVP due to venous congestion or backup
    • Septic shock (d) → Characterized by a high or normal cardiac output during its early/warm hyperdynamic phase
74. A 70-kg male presents with abdominal stab wound, profound hypotension, severe tachycardia, and marked confusion/lethargy. What percent of blood volume has he lost?
  • a. 5%
  • b. 15%
  • c. 35%
  • d. 55%
Diagnosis:
  • Class IV Hemorrhagic Shock → Characterized by severe, life-threatening volume depletion, profound hypotension, marked tachycardia, and significant central nervous system depression (such as severe confusion, lethargy, or obtundation).
    • Loss range → >40% of total blood volume (represented here by 55%)
  • Exclusions:
    • 5% (a) & 15% (b) → Class I/II shock; compensatory mechanisms typically maintain normal blood pressure and mental status.
    • 35% (c) → Class III shock; while associated with hypotension and anxiety/confusion, it represents a lower volume loss (30% to 40%) compared to the critical state of Class IV shock represented by 55%.
75. A 43-year-old man is struck by a vehicle; he arrives hypotensive, bradycardic, and unable to move his extremities. What is the most likely cause of his hypotension?
  • a. Hypovolemic shock
  • b. Obstructive shock
  • c. Neurogenic shock
  • d. Vasodilatory shock
Diagnosis:
  • Academic/Exam Key Answer → Hypovolemic shock (a)
    • Departmental Key Note → Even though the classic presentation (hypotension, bradycardia, paralysis) points strongly to Neurogenic shock (c), departmental keys often select Hypovolemic shock as the default initial cause of hypotension in trauma
  • Exclusions:
    • Neurogenic shock (c) → Clinically correct diagnosis due to loss of sympathetic tone, but be prepared for "Hypovolemic" on exams
76. Cardiogenic shock:
  • a. Is most caused by exacerbation of congestive heart failure.
  • b. Cardiogenic shock following an acute myocardial infarction is typically present on admission.
  • c. Cardiogenic shock occurs in 5 to 10% of acute MIs.
  • d. Is characterized by hypotension, reduced cardiac index, and reduced pulmonary artery wedge pressure.
Pathophysiology:
  • Myocardial Infarction complication → Cardiogenic shock develops in 5–10% of patients following acute MI
    • Primary etiology → Massive loss of contractile myocardium
  • Exclusions:
    • CHF (a) → Usually chronic, acute cardiogenic shock is most commonly caused by acute MI
    • Timing (b) → Often develops during hospital stay, rather than being present on initial admission
    • PAWP (d) → Characterized by *elevated* pulmonary artery wedge pressure (PAWP > 18 mmHg) due to left ventricular failure
77. All result from the placement of an intra-aortic balloon pump in a patient with acute myocardial failure EXCEPT:
  • a. Reduction of systolic afterload
  • b. Increased cardiac output
  • c. Increased myocardial O2 demand
  • d. Increased diastolic perfusion pressure
Pathophysiology:
  • IABP physiological benefits → Deflates during systole (reducing afterload) and inflates during diastole (enhancing coronary perfusion)
    • Result → Decreases myocardial work, thereby *reducing* myocardial O2 demand
  • Exclusions:
    • Sustained afterload (a), low output (b), and poor coronary perfusion (d) → These are baseline deficits corrected by IABP
78. Which constellation of clinical findings is suggestive of cardiac tamponade?
  • a. Hypotension, wide pulse pressure, tachycardia
  • b. Tachycardia, hypotension, jugular venous distension
  • c. Hypotension, wide pulse pressure, jugular venous distension
  • d. Hypotension, muffled heart tones, jugular venous distension
Diagnosis:
  • Beck's Triad → Hypotension, muffled heart sounds, and jugular venous distension (JVD)
    • Mechanism → High pericardial pressure compresses cardiac chambers, restricting ventricular filling
  • Exclusions:
    • Wide pulse pressure (a, c) → Tamponade produces a narrow pulse pressure
    • Isolated tachycardia/JVD (b) → Less specific; can occur in isolated heart failure or volume overload without tamponade
79. Neurogenic shock is characterized by the presence of:
  • a. Cool, moist skin
  • b. Increased cardiac output
  • c. Decreased peripheral vascular resistance
  • d. Decreased blood volume
Pathophysiology:
  • Loss of sympathetic tone → Leads to loss of vasomotor regulation
    • Direct effect → Marked vasodilatation and decreased peripheral vascular resistance (SVR)
  • Exclusions:
    • Cool, moist skin (a) → Warm, dry skin is typical due to cutaneous vasodilation
    • Increased cardiac output (b) → Bradycardia and reduced venous return decrease CO
    • Decreased blood volume (d) → Intravascular volume is unchanged; the shock is distributive
80. Vasodilatory shock:
  • a. Is characterized by failure of vascular smooth muscle to constrict due to low levels of catecholamines
  • b. Leads to suppression of the renin-angiotensin system
  • c. Can also be caused by carbon monoxide poisoning
  • d. Is like early cardiogenic shock
Pathophysiology:
  • Carbon Monoxide (CO) poisoning → Blocks cellular respiration and impairs oxygen delivery
    • End-stage shock pathway → Profound metabolic acidosis and lactic acid accumulation prompt severe system-wide vasodilation
  • Exclusions:
    • Low catecholamines (a) → Endogenous catecholamine levels are actually elevated; tissues simply fail to respond
    • RAAS suppression (b) → Hypotension activates, rather than suppresses, the renin-angiotensin system
    • Cardiogenic (d) → Cardiogenic shock involves cold, constricted extremities, not vasodilation
81. SIRS may occur because of:
  • a. Infection: bacterial, viral, fungal
  • b. Trauma & Burns
  • c. Pancreatitis
  • d. All of the above
Pathophysiology:
  • Systemic Inflammatory Response Syndrome (SIRS) → Generalized inflammatory state
    • Etiology → Triggered by both infectious pathogens and sterile tissue injury (e.g., pancreatitis, severe trauma, massive burns)
  • Exclusions:
    • Since a, b, and c are all well-established causes of SIRS, "All of the above" (d) is the correct choice
82. Septic shock responds best to:
  • a. Massive antibiotics.
  • b. Adrenocortical steroids.
  • c. Drainage of septic collections.
  • d. Vasopressors.
Clinical Management:
  • Source control → The definitive principle of sepsis management
    • Rationale → Retained abscesses or collections sustain the inflammatory response; drainage is paramount
  • Exclusions:
    • Antibiotics (a) & Vasopressors (d) → Mandatory, but remain ineffective in the long term without adequate surgical source control
    • Steroids (b) → Only recommended for refractory vasopressor-dependent shock
83. Regarding the role of the gut in shock and sepsis, all are true EXCEPT:
  • a. Selective decontamination of the digestive tract with oral antibiotics reduces nosocomial pneumonias and improves mortality.
  • b. Enteral nutrition preserves the villus architecture of the gut.
  • c. Gut dysfunction may contribute to the development of MODS by bacterial translocation.
  • d. As compared with parenteral nutrition, enteral nutrition is associated with a reduction in septic morbidity.
Pathophysiology:
  • Selective Decontamination of Digestive tract (SDD) → Controversial clinical intervention
    • Efficacy → While SDD may reduce pneumonia rates, evidence has not consistently demonstrated a significant mortality benefit
  • Exclusions:
    • Enteral nutrition (b) → Direct luminal nutrients are essential to maintain mucosal barrier integrity
    • Bacterial translocation (c) → Hypoperfused gut allows systemic migration of gut microbes, fueling MODS
    • Enteral vs Parenteral (d) → Enteral route is consistently linked to lower infectious complications
84. What is often given to patients with hypotension refractory to norepinephrine?
  • a. Dopamine
  • b. Arginine vasopressin
  • c. Dobutamine
  • d. Milrinone
Pharmacology:
  • Vasopressin (AVP) therapy → Used as a second-line vasopressor in refractory septic shock
    • Mechanism → Restores vascular sensitivity and bypasses catecholamine receptor desensitization
  • Exclusions:
    • Dopamine (a) → Associated with a higher risk of tachyarrhythmias, making it less preferred
    • Dobutamine (c) & Milrinone (d) → Inodilators (cause vasodilation); used for low cardiac output, not refractory hypotension
85. Tight glucose management in critically ill and septic patients:
  • a. Requires insulin to keep serum glucose < 140
  • b. Has no effect on mortality
  • c. Has no effect on ventilator support
  • d. Decreases length of antibiotic therapy
Clinical Management:
  • Glycemic control benefits → Strict glucose control in critically ill patients enhances immune response and neutrophil function
    • Clinical outcome → Accelerates infection resolution and shortens the required course of antibiotic therapy
  • Exclusions:
    • Glucose target (a) → Current guidelines target a moderate range (140-180 mg/dL) to avoid hypoglycemia
    • Mortality (b) → Modulating severe hyperglycemia is shown to reduce complications and ICU mortality
    • Ventilator (c) → Proper control decreases ICU days and the duration of mechanical ventilation
86. Corticosteroids in the treatment of septic shock:
  • a. Improves rates of shock reversal in patients requiring vasopressors
  • b. Improves mortality in patients with relative adrenal insufficiency
  • c. Is contraindicated in patients with positive bacterial blood cultures
  • d. None of the above
Pathophysiology:
  • Relative Adrenal Insufficiency (RAI) → Occurs when endogenous cortisol production is inadequate for severe shock states
    • Steroid replacement → Administration of low-dose hydrocortisone reduces mortality in this specific subset
  • Exclusions:
    • Shock reversal (a) → While they facilitate faster shock reversal, key guidelines prioritize mortality endpoints
    • Contraindication (c) → Steroids are not contraindicated in bacteremia; they are indicated in refractory septic shock regardless of culture status
87. Shock caused by a tension pneumothorax is categorized as:
  • a. Trauma shock
  • b. Vasodilatory shock
  • c. Cardiogenic shock
  • d. Obstructive shock
Pathophysiology:
  • Obstructive shock mechanism → Mechanical block to cardiac filling or output
    • Tension pneumothorax → Mediastinal shift compresses the vena cava, reducing venous return
  • Exclusions:
    • Trauma shock (a) → Not a formal physiological category of shock
    • Vasodilatory (b) & Cardiogenic (c) → Involve poor vascular tone or intrinsic pump failure, respectively, not external physical obstruction

Section 4: Fluids & Electrolytes (Q88–Q148)

88. Regarding total body water all the following are true EXCEPT:
  • A. Females and obese persons have an increased percentage of body water.
  • B. Increased muscle mass is associated with increased total body water.
  • C. Newborn infants have the greatest proportion of total body water.
  • D. Total body water decreases steadily with age.
Pathophysiology:
  • Adipose tissue vs water content → Adipose tissue contains very little water compared to muscle
    • Direct consequence → Females (higher fat ratio) and obese individuals have a *lower* percentage of total body water
  • Exclusions:
    • Muscle mass (B) → Highly vascular, rich in water (~75%)
    • Newborns (C) & Age decline (D) → Infants are ~75-80% water; this ratio decreases progressively throughout life
89. Regarding extracellular fluid all the following are true EXCEPT:
  • A. The total extracellular fluid volume represents 20% of the body weight.
  • B. Plasma volume constitutes 5% of body weight.
  • C. Potassium is the principal cation in extracellular fluid.
  • d- The interstitial fluid equilibrates rapidly with the other body compartments.
Pathophysiology:
  • Cation distribution → Sodium (Na+) is the primary extracellular cation
    • Potassium (K+) → Kept intracellularly via the Na+/K+ ATPase pump
  • Exclusions:
    • ECF 20% (A) & Plasma 5% (B) → Classic physiological volume distributions (60-40-20 rule)
    • Interstitial fluid (d) → Exists in equilibrium with intravascular and cellular spaces
90. Total body water constitutes what percentage of adult body weight:
  • A. 40
  • B. 50
  • C. 60
  • D. 70
Pathophysiology:
  • Standard adult water ratio → Averaged at ~60% of total body weight in adult males (50% in females)
    • Clinical application → Essential multiplier for fluid deficit calculations
  • Exclusions:
    • 40% (A) & 50% (B) → Underestimates water content in healthy males
    • 70% (D) → Typical of infants, not standard adults
91. Insensible loss of fluid from skin and lungs for 24 hours is normally in the span of:
  • A. 100 – 250 ml.
  • B. 250 – 500 ml.
  • C. 500 – 750 ml.
  • D. 750 – 1000 ml.
Pathophysiology:
  • Insensible loss definition → Evaporative loss through skin and respiration
    • Daily volume → Typically ranges from 750 to 1000 ml under standard conditions
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Underestimate basic baseline metabolic and respiratory evaporative fluid losses
92. Insensible water loss from lungs is:
  • A. 200 ml.
  • B. 500 ml.
  • C. 1000 ml.
  • D. 1500 ml.
Pathophysiology:
  • Pulmonary insensible loss → Respiratory evaporation accounts for approximately 400–500 ml/day
    • Clinical note → Increases with tachypnea, hyperventilation, or high ambient temperature
  • Exclusions:
    • 200 ml (A) → Too low for respiratory loss
    • 1000 ml (C) & 1500 ml (D) → Exceeds normal physiological limits unless the patient is hyperventilating on dry gases
93. The extracellular fluid differs from the intracellular fluid in all the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Protein content.
  • B. Electrolyte composition.
  • C. Volume.
  • D. Osmolarity (tonicity).
Pathophysiology:
  • Osmotic equilibrium → Cell membranes allow water to diffuse freely
    • Net effect → ECF and ICF maintain equal osmolarity (~290-310 mOsm/L)
  • Exclusions:
    • Protein (A) → Intracellular space has a significantly higher protein concentration
    • Electrolytes (B) → ECF is high in Na+/Cl-; ICF is high in K+/Mg++
    • Volume (C) → ICF constitutes 2/3 of TBW, whereas ECF is only 1/3
94. All the following statements regarding the anion gap are true EXCEPT:
  • A. normal range is between 10 and 19 mmoL
  • B. it reflects the concentrations of normally unmeasured anions in the serum
  • C. It is increased in diabetic ketoacidosis
  • D. It is increased in chronic diarrhea.
Pathophysiology:
  • Diarrhea acid-base imbalance → Loss of HCO3- in stool
    • Renal compensation → Retains chloride to preserve charge, resulting in a normal anion gap metabolic acidosis (NAGMA)
  • Exclusions:
    • Normal range (A) & Unmeasured anions (B) → Standard definitions of the calculated anion gap
    • DKA (C) → Elevated anion gap due to unmeasured ketoacids
95. Regarding crystalloid and colloid solutions:
  • A. Normal saline contains 154 mmol sodium and 154 mmol of chloride.
  • B. Hartmann's solution contains calcium and bicarbonate.
  • C. Albumin has a half-life in the circulation of about 15 hours.
  • D. Dextran reduces platelet aggregation and can induce anaphylaxis he daily maintenance.
Pharmacology:
  • Albumin half-life → Intravascular half-life of infused albumin is roughly 15 hours in critically ill patients
    • Note → Physiological half-life in a healthy individual is longer (approx. 15–20 days)
  • Exclusions:
    • Normal saline (A) → This is a true statement, but C is marked in keys targeting albumin properties
    • Hartmann's (B) → Contains lactate as a bicarbonate precursor, not direct bicarbonate
96. Which of the following is the predominant cation in intracellular fluid?
  • A. Chloride.
  • B. Calcium.
  • C. Sodium
  • D. Potassium
Pathophysiology:
  • Intracellular ion balance → Potassium (K+) is maintained inside the cell at ~150 mEq/L
    • Regulatory mechanism → Sustained by the active Na+/K+ ATPase pump
  • Exclusions:
    • Chloride (A) & Sodium (C) → Principal extracellular ions
    • Calcium (B) → Intracellular free calcium is kept low to prevent cell signaling cascade activation
97. Which of the following is FALSE regarding hypertonic saline?
  • A. Is an arteriolar vasodilator and may increase bleeding
  • B. Should be avoided in closed head injury
  • C. Should not be used for initial resuscitation
  • D. Increases cerebral perfusion
Clinical Management:
  • Hypertonic Saline (HTS) in TBI → Standard therapy for intracranial hypertension
    • Effect → Draws water out of brain parenchyma to reduce intracranial pressure (ICP)
  • Exclusions:
    • Vasodilator (A) → True; causes transient arteriolar vasodilation
    • Resuscitation (C) & Perfusion (D) → True; can raise cerebral perfusion but is not used as a general resuscitator
98. Normal saline is:
  • A. 135 mEq NaCL
  • B. 145 mEq NaCL
  • C. 148 mEq NaCL
  • D. 154 mEq NaCL
Pharmacology:
  • 0.9% Sodium Chloride (NS) composition → Contains 154 mEq/L of Sodium and 154 mEq/L of Chloride
    • Total osmolarity → 308 mOsm/L (slightly hypertonic to plasma)
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Represent physiological plasma levels or sub-normal concentrations
99. Fluid resuscitation using albumin:
  • A. Is associated with coagulopathy
  • B. Is available as 1% or 5% solutions
  • C. Can lead to pulmonary edema
  • D. Decreased factor XIII
Clinical Complications:
  • Hyperoncotic states → Albumin draws fluid from the interstitium into the capillaries
    • Complication → Can cause volume overload and hydrostatic pulmonary edema, especially in patients with capillary leak syndrome
  • Exclusions:
    • Coagulopathy (A) & Factor XIII (D) → Not directly associated with clinical coagulopathies unlike synthetic starches/dextrans
    • Concentrations (B) → Typically prepared as 5% or 25% solutions, not 1%
100. Water constitutes what percentage of total body weight?
  • A. 30-40%
  • B. 40-50%
  • C. 50-60%
  • D. 60-70%
Pathophysiology:
  • Normal physiological water ratio → Typically 50–60% in adult females and 60% in males
    • Key note → The exam key highlights C (50-60%) as the standard range across the general population
  • Exclusions:
    • 30-40% (A) & 40-50% (B) → Abnormally low, typical only of extreme cachexia or obesity
    • 60-70% (D) → Typical of young, lean males and children
101. If a patient's glucose increases by 180 mg/dL, what is the increase in serum osmolality?
  • A. Does not change
  • B. 8
  • C. 10
  • D. 12
Pathophysiology:
  • Osmolality contribution of glucose → Calculated as Glucose / 18
    • Calculation → 180 mg/dL / 18 = 10 mOsm/kg increase
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and D → Chemically incorrect values based on stoichiometric and osmotic calculations
102. Calculate the maintenance fluids needed for a 60-kg female:
  • A. 2060
  • B. 2100
  • C. 2160
  • D. 2400
Clinical Calculation:
  • 4-2-1 Rule for hourly maintenance → 4 ml/kg/hr for first 10 kg + 2 ml/kg/hr for second 10 kg + 1 ml/kg/hr for remainder
    • Hourly → (10 * 4) + (10 * 2) + (40 * 1) = 40 + 20 + 40 = 100 ml/hr
    • Daily (24 hrs) → 100 ml/hr * 24 hours = 2400 ml. However, using another common formula (35 ml/kg/day) → 60 * 35 = 2100 ml. The exam key utilizes the 35 ml/kg/day standard, marking 2100 ml (B) as correct.
  • Exclusions:
    • A, C, and D → Diverge from the standard 35 ml/kg/day exam standard
103. The actual AG of a chronic alcoholic with Na 133, K 4, CI-101, HC03-22, albumin of 2.5 mg/dL is:
  • A. 6
  • B. 10
  • C. 14
  • D. 15
Clinical Calculation:
  • Observed Anion Gap → Na - (Cl + HCO3) = 133 - (101 + 22) = 10
    • Hypoalbuminemia correction → For every 1 g/dL decrease in albumin below 4.0, the "normal" baseline anion gap drops by 2.5. Here, albumin is 2.5 (1.5 below normal). Adjusted gap = Observed + 2.5 * (4.0 - 2.5) = 10 + 3.75 = ~13.75-14. The closest textbook key choice is D (15).
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Do not account for the compensatory gap shift required by severe chronic hypoalbuminemia
104. The effective osmotic pressure between the plasma and interstitial fluid compartments is primarily controlled by:
  • A. Bicarbonate
  • B. Chloride ion
  • C. Potassium ion
  • D. Protein
Pathophysiology:
  • Starling forces → Oncotic pressure gradient
    • Primary driver → Plasma proteins (principally albumin) which are too large to traverse the capillary membrane easily
  • Exclusions:
    • Electrolytes (A, B, C) → Readily cross capillary walls, meaning they do not generate an effective oncotic pressure gradient across the capillary membrane
105. Which of the following diagnoses would be most likely in a patient who presents with normovolemic hyponatremia?
  • a. SIADH
  • b. High output renal failure
  • c. Water toxicity
  • d. GI losses
Diagnosis:
  • SIADH → Syndrome of Inappropriate Antidiuretic Hormone
    • Physiological state → Excess ADH causes water retention and mild volume expansion, presenting clinically as normovolemic hyponatremia
  • Exclusions:
    • High output renal failure (b) & GI losses (d) → Present with hypovolemia due to direct fluid depletion
    • Water toxicity (c) → Presents with acute hypervolemia
106. Concerning the sodium ion, which statement is untrue?
  • a. Diffuses readily through cell membranes.
  • b. Is the major cation of the extracellular fluid.
  • c. Is the chief regulator of body water.
  • d. Its urinary excretion is reduced after trauma.
Pathophysiology:
  • Membrane permeability → Cell membranes are highly impermeable to sodium
    • Cellular transport → Requires active ATP-dependent pumping to maintain extracellular localization
  • Exclusions:
    • ECF Cation (b) & Chief regulator (c) → Chemically accurate definitions of sodium's role
    • Trauma (d) → True; aldosterone release post-trauma actively conserves sodium and reduces its urinary excretion
107. Which of the following contributes maximum to body fluid osmolality:
  • a. Glucose.
  • b. Sodium.
  • c. Chloride.
  • d. Bicarbonate.
Pathophysiology:
  • Plasma Osmolality equation → Osm = 2[Na] + Glucose/18 + BUN/2.8
    • Primary driver → Sodium (and its accompanying anions) accounts for over 90% of extracellular osmolality
  • Exclusions:
    • Glucose (a) → Contributes minimally under normal conditions (~5-10 mOsm)
    • Chloride (c) & Bicarbonate (d) → Anions that balance sodium, but sodium is the principal cation driving the calculated index
108. All are possible causes of postoperative hyponatremia EXCEPT:
  • a. Excess infusion of normal saline intraoperatively.
  • b. Administration of antipsychotic medication.
  • c. Transient decrease in antidiuretic hormone (ADH) secretion.
  • d. Excess oral water intake.
Pathophysiology:
  • ADH effect on water clearance → ADH limits renal free-water excretion
    • Physiological dynamic → *Elevated* (not decreased) ADH is what retains water and drives post-op hyponatremia
  • Exclusions:
    • Normal saline (a) → Large volumes of 0.9% NaCl can lead to hyperchloremic states or dilution over time
    • Antipsychotics (b) & excess water (d) → Induce psychogenic polydipsia or mimic SIADH, lowering sodium levels
109. The free water deficit of a 70 kg man with serum sodium of 154 is:
  • a. 0.1 L
  • b. 0.7 L
  • c. 1 L
  • d. 7 L
Pathophysiology:
  • Mathematical calculation → TBW * (Na/140 - 1)
    • Calculation → 42 L * (154/140 - 1) = 42 * 0.1 = 4.2 Liters
    • Note on Key → While 4.2 L is mathematically correct, "7 L" (d) is the designated answer key selection in this specific question bank. Follow the university key!
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Significantly underestimate the calculated free water deficit
110. Which of the following can contribute to hyperkalemia in patients with renal insufficiency?
  • a. Loop diuretics
  • b. Aspirin
  • c. Calcium channel blockers
  • d. NSAIDs
Pathophysiology:
  • NSAID mechanism → Inhibit renal prostaglandin synthesis
    • Direct effect → Reduces renin release and aldosterone synthesis, impairing distal potassium excretion and causing hyperkalemia
  • Exclusions:
    • Loop diuretics (a) → Promotes potassium excretion, lowering serum potassium
    • Aspirin (b) & CCBs (c) → Do not significantly alter the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone axis to a degree that limits potassium excretion
111. Which of the following would cause decreased deep tendon reflexes?
  • a. Hypokalemia
  • b. Hypomagnesemia
  • c. Hypocalcemia
  • d. Hypoglycemia
Pathophysiology:
  • Potassium and nerve conduction → Hypokalemia hyperpolarizes cell membranes
    • Skeletal muscle impact → Impairs muscle contraction and diminishes neuromuscular transmission, leading to hyporeflexia
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypomagnesemia (b) & Hypocalcemia (c) → Promote neuromuscular hyperexcitabilty, causing hyperreflexia and tetany
    • Hypoglycemia (d) → Typically causes adrenergic stimulation and tremors, not directly blunted reflexes
112. Which of the following is an early ECG change seen in hyperkalemia?
  • a. Prolonged PR interval
  • b. Sine wave formation
  • c. Peaked T waves
  • d. Flattened P wave
Pathophysiology:
  • Hyperkalemia ECG progression → Accelerated repolarization
    • First sign → Symmetrical, narrow, peaked T waves (usually when K+ > 5.5 mEq/L)
  • Exclusions:
    • PR prolongation (a) & Flat P wave (d) → Intermediate changes occurring at higher potassium levels (K+ > 6.5)
    • Sine wave (b) → Late, pre-terminal finding preceding ventricular fibrillation
113. A patient with K of 2.9 is given KCL replacement. Repeat tests show K to be 3.0. The most likely diagnosis is:
  • a. Hypomagnesemia
  • b. Hypocalcemia
  • c. Metabolic acidosis
  • d. Metabolic alkalosis
Diagnosis:
  • Magnesium's role in potassium retention → Magnesium is a cofactor for the Na+/K+ ATPase pump
    • Refractory hypokalemia → Low magnesium permits renal potassium wasting; hypokalemia cannot be corrected until magnesium is repleted
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypocalcemia (b) → Does not directly regulate renal potassium excretion channels
    • Metabolic acidosis (c) → Typically shifts potassium *out* of cells, raising serum levels
    • Metabolic alkalosis (d) → Can cause hypokalemia, but does not render it completely refractory to replacement
114. Which should be the first treatment administered to a patient with potassium level of 6.3 and flattened P wave?
  • a. K-oxalate
  • b. Insulin and glucose
  • c. Calcium gluconate
  • d. Inhaled albuterol
Clinical Management:
  • Exam Key Selection → Insulin and glucose (b)
    • Note on Clinical Practice → Clinically, *Calcium Gluconate* is the immediate first-line action to stabilize the cardiac membrane and prevent arrest. However, this test bank emphasizes insulin/glucose as the primary active intracellular shifter. Follow your university key!
  • Exclusions:
    • K-oxalate (a) → Resins take hours to clear potassium and are not used for acute ECG changes
115. Among the following statements about potassium, which one is untrue?
  • a. Is the major cation of the intracellular fluid.
  • b. Its intracellular concentration is about 150 mEq/L.
  • c. Its average daily need is about 60 mEq/L.
  • d. Is well-conserved by the kidney after trauma.
Pathophysiology:
  • Renal potassium regulation → The kidneys do not conserve potassium efficiently
    • Trauma response → Aldosterone release conserves sodium but actively increases renal potassium wasting
  • Exclusions:
    • ICF Cation (a) & Intracellular level (b) & Daily need (c) → Chemically and physiologically accurate statements
116. Potassium deficiency is present if the plasma-potassium level is less than:
  • a. 5 mmol/l
  • b. 4.5 mmol/l.
  • c. 4 mmol/l.
  • d. 3.5 mmol/l.
Pathophysiology:
  • Normal serum potassium range → 3.5 to 5.0 mmol/L
    • Hypokalemia definition → Clinically present when levels fall below 3.5 mmol/L
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Normal physiologic ranges for serum potassium levels
117. Concerning potassium depletion, the incorrect statement is:
  • a. Follows loss of gastrointestinal secretions.
  • b. May be produced by diuretics.
  • c. Is often associated with acidosis.
  • d. Predisposes to cardiac arrhythmias.
Pathophysiology:
  • Acid-base potassium shifting → Acidosis drives potassium *out* of cells into plasma (hyperkalemia)
    • Correct association → Potassium depletion is typically associated with *alkalosis*
  • Exclusions:
    • GI loss (a) → Gastric secretions are rich in potassium and hydrogen ions
    • Diuretics (b) → Loop and thiazide diuretics cause significant renal potassium wasting
    • Arrhythmias (d) → Hypokalemia destabilizes myocardial membranes, predisposing to ectopy and arrhythmias
118. Symptoms of hyperkalemia is reflected in which organ system:
  • a. Respiratory.
  • b. Gastrointestinal.
  • c. CVS
  • d. CNS.
Pathophysiology:
  • Hyperkalemia cardiac risks → Promotes conduction delays and ventricular arrhythmias
    • Target organ → The cardiovascular system (CVS) is the most critical and lethal site of hyperkalemic symptoms
  • Exclusions:
    • Respiratory (a), GI (b), and CNS (d) → While generalized muscle weakness occurs, cardiac conduction blocks are the dominant clinical presentation
119. Hypokalemia causes:
  • a. Peaked P waves.
  • b. Adynamic ileus.
  • c. Hyperreflexia.
  • d. All the above.
Pathophysiology:
  • Smooth muscle hyperpolarization → Low potassium limits gastrointestinal motility
    • Complication → Leads to paralytic or adynamic ileus
  • Exclusions:
    • Peaked P waves (a) & Hyperreflexia (c) → Characteristic of hyperkalemia and hypocalcemia/hypomagnesemia, respectively
120. Regarding hypokalemia, all the following are true, except:
  • a. Hypokalemia causes acidosis.
  • b. Severe diarrhea may cause Hypokalemia.
  • c. Hypokalemia causes muscle weakness.
  • d. Hypokalemia causes paralytic ileus.
Pathophysiology:
  • Hypokalemia and pH → Hypokalemia causes hydrogen to shift intracellularly as potassium exits cells
    • Systemic outcome → Promotes metabolic *alkalosis*, not acidosis
  • Exclusions:
    • Diarrhea (b) → Stool contains high potassium concentrations
    • Weakness (c) & Ileus (d) → Classic neuromuscular manifestations of low intracellular potassium
121. Which of the following is an early sign of hyperkalemia?
  • A. Peaked T waves
  • B. Peaked P waves
  • C. Peaked (shortened) QRS complex
  • D. Peaked U waves
Pathophysiology:
  • ECG changes in hyperkalemia:
    • Symmetrical peaked T waves are the initial electrical indicator of hyperkalemia
  • Exclusions: Same as Q112.
122. What is the actual potassium of a patient with pH of 7.8 and serum potassium of 2.2?
  • A. 2.2
  • B. 2.8
  • C. 3.2
  • D. 3.4
Pathophysiology:
  • pH and potassium shifting → For every 0.1 unit increase in pH, serum potassium drops by approximately 0.3 mEq/L due to intracellular shifting
    • Calculation → pH of 7.8 is 0.4 units above normal (7.4). Shift = 4 * 0.3 = 1.2 mEq/L drop. Therefore, the "actual/corrected" baseline potassium without the alkalotic shift is 2.2 + 1.2 = 3.4 mEq/L (D).
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Fail to account for the magnitude of potassium shift induced by a severe alkalemia of 7.8
123. All the following treatments for hyperkalemia reduce serum potassium EXCEPT:
  • A. Bicarbonate
  • B. Kayexalate
  • C. Glucose infusion with insulin
  • D. Calcium
Pharmacology:
  • Calcium's physiological role in hyperkalemia → Stabilizes the cardiac membrane
    • Action mechanism → Antagonizes the neuromuscular effects of excess potassium without altering circulating serum levels
  • Exclusions:
    • Bicarbonate (A) & Insulin/glucose (C) → Shift potassium intracellularly
    • Kayexalate (B) → Removes potassium from the body via GI tract exchange
124. Hypocalcemia may cause which of the following?
  • A. Congestive heart failure
  • B. Atrial fibrillation
  • C. Pancreatitis
  • D. Hypoparathyroidism
Pathophysiology:
  • Calcium in myocardial contractility → Essential for excitation-contraction coupling
    • Deficit consequence → Profound hypocalcemia impairs ventricular contraction, potentially triggering acute congestive heart failure
  • Exclusions:
    • Atrial fibrillation (B) & Pancreatitis (C) & Hypoparathyroidism (D) → These are *causes* of hypocalcemia, not clinical effects of it
125. A patient with serum calcium of 6.8 and albumin of 1.2 has a corrected calcium of:
  • A. 7.7
  • B. 8.0
  • C. 8.6
  • D. 9.2
Clinical Calculation:
  • Calcium correction formula → Corrected Ca = Observed Ca + 0.8 * (4.0 - Albumin)
    • Calculation → 6.8 + 0.8 * (4.0 - 1.2) = 6.8 + 0.8 * 2.8 = 6.8 + 2.24 = 9.04 mg/dL. The closest exam key choice is D (9.2).
  • Exclusions:
    • A, B, and C → Do not reflect the necessary correction for severe hypoalbuminemia
126. A patient who has spasms in the hand when a blood pressure cuff is blown up most likely has:
  • A. Hypercalcemia
  • B. Hypocalcemia
  • C. Hypermagnesemia
  • D. Hypomagnesemia
Diagnosis:
  • Trousseau's sign → Carpopedal spasm induced by ischemia
    • Clinical significance → Highly specific indicator of latent hypocalcemic neuromuscular excitability
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypercalcemia (A) & Hypermagnesemia (C) → Depress neuromuscular excitability
    • Hypomagnesemia (D) → Can present with similar hyperreflexia, but Trousseau's is classically diagnostic of hypocalcemia
127. The management of acute hypercalcemia should be:
  • A. Correction of deficit of extracellular fluid volume
  • B. Hemodialysis.
  • C. Administration of furosemide.
