  • D. Administration of mithramycin.
Clinical Management:
  • First-line hypercalcemia therapy → Volume expansion with 0.9% Normal Saline
    • Rationale → Restores intravascular volume, increases GFR, and promotes urinary calcium excretion
  • Exclusions:
    • Hemodialysis (B) → Reserved for severe refractory cases or renal failure
    • Furosemide (C) → Initiated only *after* volume correction is achieved
    • Mithramycin (D) → Late-line osteoclast inhibitor, not for acute primary resuscitation
128. Which of the following is a cause of acute hypophosphatemia?
  • a. Chronic ingestion of magnesium containing laxatives
  • b. Insulin coma
  • c. Refeeding syndrome
  • d. Rhabdomyolysis
Pathophysiology:
  • Refeeding syndrome dynamics → Carbohydrate reintroduction in malnourished states
    • Mechanism → Insulin surge drives glucose and phosphate into cells for ATP synthesis, causing severe extracellular hypophosphatemia
  • Exclusions:
    • Insulin coma (b) & laxatives (a) → Do not cause the systemic anabolic shift of refeeding
    • Rhabdomyolysis (d) → Causes cellular lysis, releasing phosphate and causing *hyper*phosphatemia
129. Refeeding syndrome can be associated with all the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Respiratory failure
  • B. Hyperkalemia
  • C. Confusion
  • D. Cardiac arrhythmias
Pathophysiology:
  • Intracellular shifting in refeeding → Insulin surge drives potassium *into* cells
    • Electrolyte outcome → Causes hypokalemia, not hyperkalemia
  • Exclusions:
    • Respiratory failure (A) & Arrhythmias (D) → Caused by severe hypophosphatemia and ATP depletion
    • Confusion (C) → Encephalopathy due to thiamine and phosphate depletion
130. Hypomagnesaemia resembles the clinical picture of which one of the following:
  • a. Hypokalemia.
  • b. Hyperphosphatemia.
  • c. Hyperglycemia.
  • d. Hypocalcemia
Pathophysiology:
  • Neuromuscular manifestations → Both hypomagnesemia and hypocalcemia cause increased neuromuscular excitability
    • Shared findings → Hyperreflexia, tetany, positive Chvostek's/Trousseau's signs
  • Exclusions:
    • Hypokalemia (a) → Presents with hyporeflexia and weakness, not neuromuscular excitability
    • Hyperphosphatemia (b) & Hyperglycemia (c) → Distinct clinical profiles without shared tetany characteristics
131. An alcoholic patient with albumin 3.9, K 3.1, Mg 2.4, Ca 7.8, P04 3.2 receives three boluses of IV potassium and has serum potassium of 3.3. You should:
  • A. Continue to bolus potassium until the serum level is > 3.6.
  • B. Give MgSO4 IV
  • C. Check the ionized calcium.
  • D. Check the BUN and creatinine.
Pathophysiology:
  • Refractory Hypokalemia → Despite a normal serum Mg level of 2.4, alcoholics typically have severe intracellular magnesium depletion
    • Indication → Replenish intracellular Mg with IV MgSO4 to restore Na+/K+ pump function and retain potassium
  • Exclusions:
    • A, C, and D → Do not address the underlying intracellular transport defect keeping potassium low
132. Delirium, muscle tremors, and hyperactive tendon reflexes post-gastric surgery. Magnesium deficiency is suspected. All are true EXCEPT:
  • A. A decision to administer magnesium should be based on the serum magnesium level.
  • B. Adequate cellular replacement of magnesium will require 1 to 3 weeks.
  • C. A concomitant calcium deficiency should be suspected.
  • D. Calcium is a specific antagonist of the myocardial effects of magnesium.
Pathophysiology:
  • Total body magnesium pool → Serum magnesium accounts for only ~1% of total body magnesium stores
    • Clinical implication → Patients can have severe intracellular magnesium depletion despite normal serum levels; treatment decisions should be guided by clinical findings
  • Exclusions:
    • Replacement time (B) → Cellular repletion is slow, often taking weeks
    • Calcium deficit (C) → Magnesium is required for parathyroid hormone (PTH) secretion; magnesium depletion often induces secondary hypocalcemia
    • Antagonism (D) → Calcium stabilizes the myocardium against hypermagnesemic depression
133. In health the pH of the blood lies between the ranges:
  • a. 7.05 -7.19
  • b. 7.20-7.35
  • c. 7.36-7.44.
  • d. 7.45 - 7.59.
Pathophysiology:
  • Normal physiological pH → Strictly regulated between 7.35 and 7.45
    • Textbook range → 7.36–7.44 is the standard range for health
  • Exclusions:
    • A & B → Acidemia
    • D → Alkalemia
134. The standard bicarbonate level in the plasma is normally:
  • a. 12 - 15 mmol/l.
  • b. 16 - 21 mmol/1.
  • c. 22 - 25 mmol/1.
  • d. 26 - 31 mmol/l.
Pathophysiology:
  • Physiological bicarbonate range → Normal arterial bicarbonate is 22 to 26 mmol/L
    • Textbook range → 22–25 mmol/L is standard on health scales
  • Exclusions:
    • A & B → Severe to moderate metabolic acidosis values
    • D → Metabolic alkalosis range
135. Concerning metabolic acidosis, which of the following statements is untrue:
  • a. Occurs in diabetes, starvation, shock, and anuria.
  • b. May be due to diarrhoea and intestinal fistulas.
  • c. Is often associated with air hunger.
  • d. Causes bradycardia and hypotension.
Pathophysiology:
  • Systemic acidosis effects → Severe acidemia (pH < 7.15) impairs myocardial contractility and induces arteriolar vasodilation (causing hypotension)
    • Heart rate response → Direct sympathetic stimulation typically causes *tachycardia* rather than bradycardia
  • Exclusions:
    • Diabetes/Shock (a) & Diarrhea (b) → Well-known clinical causes of metabolic acidosis
    • Air hunger (c) → Prompts compensatory hyperventilation (Kussmaul breathing)
136. The correction of hypokalemic alkalosis due to pyloric obstruction is:
  • a. Normal saline.
  • b. Potassium chloride in 5% glucose.
  • c. Ringer's solution.
  • d. Ammonium chloride.
Clinical Management:
  • Contraction alkalosis from gastric fluid loss → Loss of H+ and Cl-
    • Correction mechanism → Normal Saline (0.9% NaCl) provides high chloride concentrations, allowing the kidneys to excrete bicarbonate and correct the alkalosis
  • Exclusions:
    • Glucose (b) → Insulin release shifts potassium intracellularly, worsening the hypokalemia
    • Ringer's (c) → Contains lactate, which converts to bicarbonate and can worsen metabolic alkalosis
    • Ammonium chloride (d) → Worsens acidosis too aggressively; rarely used clinically
137. The following are known to cause a metabolic acidosis EXCEPT:
  • a. ischemic bowel
  • b. diabetic ketoacidosis
  • c. sepsis
  • d. persistent vomiting
Pathophysiology:
  • Vomiting and acid loss → Loss of hydrochloric acid (HCl) from the stomach
    • Acid-base effect → Promotes hypokalemic hypochloremic metabolic *alkalosis*
  • Exclusions:
    • Ischemic bowel (a) & Sepsis (c) → Cause tissue hypoxia and lactic acidosis
    • DKA (b) → Causes accumulation of acetoacetate and beta-hydroxybutyrate ketoacids
138. About metabolic acidosis, one statement only is true:
  • a. It is caused by pyloric stenosis.
  • b. It causes hyperkalemia.
  • c. Arterial bicarbonate level is elevated.
  • d. PH is above 7.4.
Pathophysiology:
  • Acidosis-induced hyperkalemia → Excess extracellular H+ ions shift intracellularly
    • Compensatory shift → Potassium shifts out of cells into the extracellular space, raising serum potassium levels
  • Exclusions:
    • Pyloric stenosis (a) → Causes metabolic alkalosis
    • Bicarbonate (c) & pH > 7.4 (d) → Findings diagnostic of alkalosis, not acidosis
139. Metabolic acidosis with a normal anion gap (AG) occurs with:
  • a. Diabetic acidosis
  • b. Renal failure
  • c. Severe diarrhea
  • d. Starvation
Pathophysiology:
  • Normal Anion Gap Metabolic Acidosis (NAGMA):
    • Severe diarrhea results in direct bicarbonate loss, which is balanced by renal chloride retention (hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis)
  • Exclusions: Same as Q94.
140. The next most appropriate test to order in a patient with a pH of 7.1, Pco2 of 40, sodium of 132, potassium of 4.2, and chloride of 105 is:
  • A. Serum bicarbonate
  • B. Serum magnesium
  • C. Serum ethanol
  • D. Serum salicylate
Clinical Evaluation:
  • Anion gap evaluation → To characterize metabolic acidosis
    • Missing variable → Anion Gap = Na - (Cl + HCO3). Bicarbonate must be measured to calculate the gap and identify the etiology of the acidosis.
  • Exclusions:
    • Mg (B), Ethanol (C), Salicylate (D) → Specialized diagnostic tests; calculating the basic anion gap with bicarbonate is the standard initial step
141. The metabolic derangement most seen in patients with profuse vomiting:
  • A. Hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis
  • B. Hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic acidosis
  • C. Hypochloremic, hyperkalemic metabolic alkalosis
  • D. Hypochloremic, hyperkalemic metabolic acidosis
Diagnosis:
  • Exam Key Selection → Hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic acidosis (B)
    • Note on Clinical Physiology → Physiologically, profuse gastric vomiting causes metabolic *alkalosis* (A). However, this specific test bank key designates option (B) as correct. Follow your university key!
  • Exclusions:
    • A → Clinically correct physiological choice, but marked as wrong by the key
142. A low urinary [NH4+] with hyperchloremic acidosis indicates what cause?
  • A. Excessive vomiting
  • B. Enterocutaneous fistula
  • C. Chronic diarrhea
  • D. Renal tubular acidosis
Diagnosis:
  • Renal Tubular Acidosis (RTA) → Characterized by impaired renal acidification
    • Key marker → Low urinary ammonium excretion [NH4+] in the setting of hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis
  • Exclusions:
    • Vomiting (A) & Diarrhea (C) & Fistula (B) → GI causes of acid-base imbalance; the kidneys remain functional and secrete ammonium in response to the acidosis
143. When lactic acid is produced in response to injury, the body minimizes pH change by:
  • A. Decreasing production of sodium bicarbonate in tissues
  • B. Excreting carbon dioxide through the lungs
  • C. Excreting lactic acid through the kidneys
  • D. Metabolizing the lactic acid in the liver
Pathophysiology:
  • Respiratory compensation for metabolic acidosis:
    • Mechanism → Lactic acid is buffered by bicarbonate, generating CO2 and H2O. Hyperventilation then excretes the excess CO2 to normalize pH.
  • Exclusions:
    • Bicarbonate (A) → The body *increases* bicarbonate utilization to buffer hydrogen ions
    • Renal excretion (C) & Hepatic metabolism (D) → Effective pathways, but too slow to provide rapid, acute buffering of pH shifts
144. What is the best determinant of whether a patient has a metabolic acidosis versus alkalosis?
  • A. Arterial pH
  • B. Serum bicarbonate
  • C. Pco2
  • D. Serum CO2 level
Pathophysiology:
  • Arterial pH as the gold standard → Determines the primary acidemic (<7.35) or alkalemic (>7.45) state
    • Surogate measures → Bicarbonate or Pco2 can be altered by compensation, whereas pH reflects the net balance of the primary disorder
  • Exclusions:
    • Bicarbonate (B) & Pco2 (C) → Can be elevated or reduced in both acidosis and alkalosis depending on compensation
145. If a patient’s arterial Pco2 is found to be 25mm Hg, the arterial pH will be approximately:
  • A. 7.52
  • B. 7.40
  • C. 7.32
  • D. 7.28
Pathophysiology:
  • Compensatory respiratory alkalosis in acidosis → Seen in severe shock states
    • Mechanism → Severe primary metabolic acidosis drives compensatory hyperventilation, dropping Pco2 to 25. Despite compensation, the patient remains acidotic (represented by 7.28).
  • Exclusions:
    • 7.52 (A) → Reflects primary respiratory alkalosis, not typical compensation for trauma
    • 7.40 (B) & 7.32 (C) → Do not match the severe metabolic acidosis/compensation dynamics typical of shock
146. Which of the following are NOT characteristic findings of acute renal failure?
  • A. BUN > 100 mg/dL
  • B. Hypokalemia
  • C. Severe acidosis
  • D. Uremic pericarditis
Diagnosis:
  • BUN progression in acute renal failure → While BUN rises, a level >100 mg/dL is a chronic or late finding
    • Note on Key → Note that hypokalemia (B) is also not typical (hyperkalemia is classic), but the exam key designates option (A) as correct.
  • Exclusions:
    • Acidosis (C) & Pericarditis (D) → Classic medium-to-late uremic complications of renal failure
147. A victim of a motor vehicle accident arrives in hemorrhagic shock. ABG: pH 7.25, Pco2 25, HCO3- 15. The patient's metabolic acidosis would be treated best with:
  • A. Ampule of sodium bicarbonate
  • B. Sodium bicarbonate infusion
  • C. Lactated Ringer solution
  • D. Hyperventilation
Clinical Management:
  • Fluid Resuscitation in shock → Lactated Ringer's is the fluid of choice
    • Rationale → Restores circulating blood volume and improves tissue perfusion, reversing anaerobic metabolism. The liver then metabolizes lactate to bicarbonate, correcting the acidosis.
  • Exclusions:
    • Bicarbonate (A, B) → Exogenous bicarbonate is contraindicated for lactic acidosis (pH > 7.1) as it can cause rebound intracellular acidosis
    • Hyperventilation (D) → The patient's low Pco2 of 25 shows they are already maximally hyperventilating
148. Symptoms and signs of extracellular fluid volume deficit include all the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Anorexia
  • B. Apathy
  • C. Decreased body temperature
  • D. High pulse pressure
Pathophysiology:
  • Vascular tone in hypovolemia → Reduced stroke volume
    • Direct effect → Promotes compensatory peripheral vasoconstriction, resulting in a *narrowed* (low), not high, pulse pressure
  • Exclusions:
    • Anorexia (A) & Apathy (B) & Decreased temperature (C) → Classic systemic indicators of severe hypovolemic dehydration

Section 5: Skin & Subcutaneous Tissues (Q149–Q203)

149-1. An abnormal communication between two epithelial-lined surfaces. The communication or tract may be lined by granulation tissue but in chronic cases it may be epithelialized:
  • A. Ulcer
  • B. Sinus
  • C. Fistula
Diagnosis:
Abnormal connection between two epithelial surfaces → Fistula

Exclusions:
Ulcer (A) → Discontinuity of epithelial surface
Sinus (B) → Blind-ending tract starting from a cavity to epithelial surface
149-2. A blind-ending tract that connects a cavity lined with granulation tissue (usually an abscess) with an epithelial surface:
  • A. Ulcer
  • B. Sinus
  • C. Fistula
Diagnosis:
Blind tract connecting an underlying cavity with skin → Sinus

Exclusions:
Ulcer (A) → Open break on epithelial surface
Fistula (C) → Pathological connection between two epithelialized lumens
149-3. A discontinuity of an epithelial surface:
  • A. Ulcer
  • B. Sinus
  • C. Fistula
Diagnosis:
Loss/discontinuity of cutaneous or mucosal epithelium → Ulcer

Exclusions:
Sinus (B) → Blind-ended tract from a cavity
Fistula (C) → Pathological communication tunnel
150-1. A polymicrobial synergistic infection most often caused by group A beta-hemolytic Streptococcus:
  • A. Impetigo
  • B. Erysipelas
  • C. Cellulitis/lymphangitis
  • D. Necrotising fasciitis
  • E. Purpura fulminans
Diagnosis:
Highly aggressive polymicrobial infection of the deep fascia → Necrotising fasciitis

Exclusions:
Impetigo (A) → Superficial epidermal crusting
Erysipelas (B) → Superficial lymphatic/dermal demarcated infection
Cellulitis (C) → Diffuse dermis/subcutaneous infection
150-2. In this lesion there is intravascular thrombosis producing hemorrhagic skin infarction:
  • A. Impetigo
  • B. Erysipelas
  • C. Cellulitis/lymphangitis
  • D. Necrotising fasciitis
  • E. Purpura fulminans
Diagnosis:
Microvascular thrombosis causing rapidly spreading hemorrhagic necrosis → Purpura fulminans

Exclusions:
Impetigo (A) → Lacks thrombosis and systemic hemorrhagic infarcts
Necrotising fasciitis (D) → Primarily infects fascial planes, not isolated intravascular thrombosis
150-3. This is a highly infectious skin lesion, usually affecting children, caused by Staphylococcus and/or Streptococcus:
  • A. Impetigo
  • B. Erysipelas
  • C. Cellulitis/lymphangitis
  • D. Necrotising fasciitis
  • E. Purpura fulminans
Diagnosis:
Highly contagious epidermal infection with honey-colored crusts in children → Impetigo

Exclusions:
Erysipelas (B) → Raised, well-demarcated plaque, not typically crusty
Cellulitis (C) → Deep, non-contagious systemic infection
150-4. This is a generalised bacterial infection of the skin and subcutaneous tissue, usually preceded by trauma or ulceration:
  • A. Impetigo
  • B. Erysipelas
  • C. Cellulitis/lymphangitis
  • D. Necrotising fasciitis
  • E. Purpura fulminans
Diagnosis:
Spreading inflammation of deep dermis and subcutaneous tissues → Cellulitis/lymphangitis

Exclusions:
Impetigo (A) → Confined strictly to superficial epidermis
Necrotising fasciitis (D) → Rapidly crosses fascial planes causing muscular necrosis
150-5. This produces a well-demarcated infection by streptococci, usually on the face:
  • A. Impetigo
  • B. Erysipelas
  • C. Cellulitis/lymphangitis
  • D. Necrotising fasciitis
  • E. Purpura fulminans
Diagnosis:
Streptococcal superficial cellulitis with sharp, raised borders on the face → Erysipelas

Exclusions:
Cellulitis (C) → Characterized by poorly defined, diffuse borders
Impetigo (A) → Presents as honey-colored vesicular crusts
151. Which of the following statements are false with regard to necrotizing fasciitis?
  • A. This is a surgical emergency.
  • B. It is a polymicrobial synergistic infection.
  • C. To confirm the diagnosis a plain X-ray is done to look for air.
  • D. 80% of cases have a history of trauma or previous infection.
  • E. A period of observation is advisable to see if the condition spreads.
Diagnosis:
Immediate surgical debridement is mandatory; observation is highly dangerous → False statement is (E)

Exclusions:
A, B, C, D → True physiological and diagnostic features of necrotizing fasciitis
152. A 21-year-old male presents to the emergency department (ED) with pain at his upper mid buttocks. On exam, he has a tender mass at the intergluteal region overlying the natal cleft with a sinus tract draining purulent fluid. A single strand of hair is seen protruding from the tract. He reports that he has been treated for this condition several times. Which of the following is most correct?
  • a. Control of hair growth at the intergluteal cleft is unlikely to prevent recurrence.
  • b. Incision and drainage should be performed in the ED.
  • c. Surgical excision of the sinus tract and marsupialization of the wound should be performed in the OR.
  • d. The pathogenesis likely involves apocrine glands.
  • e. CT scan of the pelvis should be performed.
Clinical Evaluation:
Acute fluctuant pilonidal abscess → Immediate incision and drainage (I&D) in the emergency department

Exclusions:
Excision/marsupialization (c) → Elective definitive procedures performed after acute infection subsides
Apocrine path (d) → Refers to hidradenitis suppurativa, not hair-driven pilonidal disease
CT Pelvis (e) → Routine imaging is unnecessary for localized superficial abscesses
153. The presence of sulfur granules in a draining wound should lead to the use of which of the following antibiotics?
  • a. Rifampin
  • b. Gentamicin
  • c. Penicillin
  • d. Amphotericin
Diagnosis:
Actinomyces israelii infection (exhibiting diagnostic sulfur granules) → High-dose Penicillin therapy

Exclusions:
Rifampin (a), Gentamicin (b) → Ineffective for anaerobic Actinomyces infections
Amphotericin (d) → Antifungal agent; actinomyces is a filamentous bacterium
154. Initial Treatment of non-purulent, complicated cellulitis is:
  • a. Vancomycin
  • b. Lactam
  • c. Linezolid
  • d. Clindamycin
Clinical Evaluation:
Non-purulent cellulitis (typically streptococcal) → Beta-lactam antibiotic

Exclusions:
Vancomycin (a), Linezolid (c), Clindamycin (d) → Primary choices for purulent cellulitis/MRSA risk
155. The chronic inflammatory disease presenting as painful subcutaneous nodules is:
  • a. Pyoderma gangrenosum
  • b. Toxic epidermal necrolysis syndrome
  • c. Hidradenitis suppurative
  • d. Steven-Johnson syndrome
Diagnosis:
Occlusive apocrine gland inflammation forming painful axillary/inguinal nodules → Hidradenitis suppurativa

Exclusions:
Pyoderma gangrenosum (a) → Rapidly progressive painful violaceous ulcerations
TEN (b) / SJS (d) → Severe drug-induced epidermal detachment
156. Correct statements about toxic epidermal necrolysis (TEN) include all of the following EXCEPT:
  • a. Toxic epidermal necrolysis is believed to be an immunologic problem.
  • b. Lesions are similar in appearance to partial thickness burns.
  • c. The process develops at the dermo-epidermal junction.
  • d. Corticosteroid use is a primary part of therapy.
Therapeutics:
Corticosteroid use is controversial/avoided in TEN due to severe sepsis risks → Statement (d) is false

Exclusions:
a, b, c → Accurately describe the immunologic dermo-epidermal split and partial-thickness burn appearance
157. List the layers of skin from the most superficial to the deepest layer adjacent to the dermis: (a) basal layer, (b) granular layer, (c) prickle layer, and (d) stratum corneum.
  • A. a b c d
  • B. d b a c
  • C. d c b a
  • D. c a b d
  • E. c a d b
Anatomy:
Correct sequence (referencing provided key) → d (Corneum) → c (Prickle/Spinosum) → b (Granular) → a (Basal)

Exclusions:
Other choices present anatomically incorrect sequences from superficial to deep dermis junction.
158. Which of the following statements are true?
  • a. The epidermis accounts for the major part of the skin.
  • b. The majority of epidermal cells are keratinocytes.
  • c. Melanocytes are cells found in the dermis.
  • d. Sweat glands are of two types: eccrine and apocrine.
  • e. Adnexal structures span the epidermis and dermis
Anatomy:
Over 90% of the cells within the epidermis are keratinocytes → (b) is true

Exclusions:
Epidermis (a) → Dermis comprises the bulk of skin thickness
Melanocytes (c) → Located within the basal layer of epidermis, not dermis
EMQ-1. A maculopapular pigmented lesion that is most prominent at puberty. It is a junctional proliferation of naevus cells with nests and columns in the dermis:
  • A. Basal cell papilloma
  • B. Papillary wart
  • C. Junctional naevus
  • D. Compound naevus
  • E. Keratoacanthoma
Diagnosis:
Nevus containing nests in both dermo-epidermal junction and deeper dermis → Compound naevus

Exclusions:
Junctional naevus (C) → Nests are located strictly at the dermo-epidermal junction
EMQ-2. A deeply pigmented macular or papular lesion commonly seen in childhood. It is caused by a dermo-epidermal proliferation of naevus cells and progresses in the older person to form a compound or intradermal naevus. It has no malignant potential:
  • A. Basal cell papilloma
  • B. Papillary wart
  • C. Junctional naevus
  • D. Compound naevus
  • E. Keratoacanthoma
Diagnosis:
Early childhood melanocytic proliferation limited to junctional zones → Junctional naevus

Exclusions:
Compound naevus (D) → Contains cellular nests extending into dermis
EMQ-3. A benign skin tumour caused by the human papillomavirus (HPV) which also causes plantar warts and condylomata acuminata:
  • A. Basal cell papilloma
  • B. Papillary wart
  • C. Junctional naevus
  • D. Compound naevus
  • E. Keratoacanthoma
Diagnosis:
HPV-driven benign epidermal proliferation → Papillary wart (verruca vulgaris)

Exclusions:
Basal cell papilloma (A) → Seborrheic keratosis; benign, non-viral etiology
EMQ-4. A soft, warty lesion which is often pigmented and arises from the basal layer of the epidermal cells containing melanocytes:
  • A. Basal cell papilloma
  • B. Papillary wart
  • C. Junctional naevus
  • D. Compound naevus
  • E. Keratoacanthoma
Diagnosis:
Benign seborrheic keratosis arising from basal keratinocytes → Basal cell papilloma

Exclusions:
Papillary wart (B) → HPV-associated skin verruca
EMQ-5. This is one of the commonest benign skin lesions in the elderly:
  • A. Basal cell papilloma
  • B. Papillary wart
  • C. Junctional naevus
  • D. Compound naevus
  • E. Keratoacanthoma
Diagnosis:
Ectodermal crateriform tumor highly prevalent in elderly cohorts → Keratoacanthoma

Exclusions:
Papillary warts (B) → More common in pediatric/young adult cohorts
159. A 12-year-old boy has multiple skin lesions that are diagnosed as von Recklinghausen’s syndrome (NF-1). What is TRUE of this condition?
  • a. It does not show other malignant lesions.
  • b. It is autosomal recessive.
  • c. It is associated with optic nerve gliomas.
  • d. It is characterized by atrioventricular (AV) malformation.
  • e. It is associated with dermoid.
Diagnosis:
NF-1 association → Optic nerve gliomas (pilocytic astrocytoma)

Exclusions:
Maligancy risk (a) → High predisposition to MPNSTs
Inheritance (b) → Autosomal dominant transmission
160. A 49-year-old male postman had undergone several operations to excise recurrent infections in both axillary lesions and perianal region. The lesions are hydradenitis suppurativa. Which is TRUE of these?
  • a. They arise from stratum corneum of skin.
  • b. They are noninflammatory condition.
  • c. They always require surgical intervention.
  • d. They frequently involve the scalp.
  • e. They are usually caused by staphylococci and streptococci.
Clinical Evaluation:
Hidradenitis suppurativa lesions → Frequently superinfected by Streptococci & Staphylococci

Exclusions:
Corneum (a) → Disease originates in follicle-apocrine units
Noninflammatory (b) → High clinical inflammation is present
161. A 12-year-old boy has multiple skin lesions that are diagnosed as von Recklinghausen’s syndrome (NF 1). What is TRUE of this condition? (Duplicate Question)
  • a. It does not show other malignant lesions.
  • b. It is autosomal recessive.
  • c. It is associated with optic nerve gliomas.
  • d. It is characterized by AV malformation.
  • e. It is associated with dermoid.
Diagnosis:
Optic pathway pilocytic astrocytomas are strongly linked to NF-1 → (c) is correct

Exclusions: Same as Q159.
162. A 35-year-old White male previously diagnosed with basal cell nevus syndrome (Gorlin’s syndrome) presents with a new lesion for treatment. Apart from multiple basal cell lesions, other features of this disorder may include:
  • a. The disorder is genetically determined and transmitted as an autosomal dominant.
  • b. Rib abnormalities such as splayed or bifid ribs.
  • c. Skin pits on the palms and soles.
  • d. A benign clinical course before puberty.
  • e. Normal mental development.
Diagnosis:
Gorlin's syndrome clinical features → Normal mental development is typically preserved

Exclusions:
While a, b, and c represent key physical features of Gorlin's, choice (e) is highlighted by the key as the correct answer.
163. A 29-year-old male presents with left wrist pain. He has a mass at the volar wrist that has been growing in size for the past 4 months and recently started causing him pain. The mass is compressible, freely moving but tethered in place, and transilluminates. Which of the following is true regarding this condition?
  • a. It affects the volar wrist more commonly than it does the dorsal wrist.
  • b. It is unlikely to resolve without intervention.
  • c. Ligation of the tethering pedicle is required to achieve the lowest recurrence rate.
  • d. Simple aspiration is the preferred treatment option.
  • e. The pain is likely secondary to compression of the terminal branches of the posterior interosseous nerve.
Diagnosis:
Volar wrist ganglion cyst → Often persistent and unlikely to resolve without intervention

Exclusions:
Dorsal frequency (a) → Dorsal cysts represent ~70% of wrist ganglia (volar is less common)
Aspiration (d) → High recurrence rates compared to surgical excision
164. A 65-year-old female presents with a 5-cm right-sided rubber-like mass located on her back. Imaging shows an unencapsulated lenticular mass with alternating streaks of fibrous and fatty tissue located between the subscapular region and the serratus anterior muscle. Which of the following is true regarding this condition?
  • a. This is a malignant condition.
  • b. It is a benign tumor composed of adipose tissue.
  • c. Biopsy is necessary even when radiologic findings are typical.
  • d. Simple excision should be performed.
  • e. Wide local excision should be performed.
Diagnosis:
Elastofibroma dorsi (benign pseudotumor of back) → Simple excision is curative if symptomatic

Exclusions:
Malignancy (a) → Fully benign lesion
Adipose tumor (b) → Composed of fibroelastic tissue and fat streaks
165. Capillary hemangioma of the face requires which of the following investigations before starting treatment?
  • a. MRI of the sinuses.
  • b. CT of the brain.
  • c. Liver ultrasound.
  • d. Venous Duplex of the lower limbs
Clinical Evaluation:
Facial hemangiomas (screens for PHACE association) → CT/MRI of the brain to rule out intracranial vascular anomalies

Exclusions:
MRI sinuses (a), Liver ultrasound (c), Leg venous duplex (d) → Not standard baseline screens for PHACE anomalies
166. All the following statements about sebaceous cysts are true, except:
  • a. They are common in children.
  • b. They do not appear on the palm.
  • c. They are attached to the skin.
  • d. Sometimes the punctum cannot be seen.
Diagnosis:
Sebaceous/epidermoid cysts are rare in pediatric cohorts without genetic syndromes → (a) is false

Exclusions:
Palms (b) → True; palms lack sebaceous glands
Attachment (c) & Punctum (d) → True anatomical features
167. A port wine stain (Naevus flammeus) is:
  • a. A premalignant lesion of the skin.
  • b. A type of melanoma.
  • c. A type of bruising of the skin.
  • d. A type of haemangioma.
Diagnosis:
Capillary vascular malformation historically grouped under benign vascular tumors → Haemangioma variant

Exclusions:
Premalignant (a), melanoma (b), bruises (c) → Entirely unrelated benign or neoplastic states
168. «Sign of emptying» is seen in:
  • a. Salmon patch.
  • b. Port wine stain.
  • c. Plexiform angioma.
  • d. Strawberry hemangioma.
Diagnosis:
The "sign of emptying" (or compressibility) is elicited when direct manual compression collapses the highly vascular channels, causing the lesion to shrink or blanch, followed by a slow refilling of blood when pressure is released. This is a hallmark clinical characteristic of hemangiomas, such as a Strawberry hemangioma (capillary/infantile hemangioma).

Exclusions:
Salmon patch (a) & Port wine stain (b) → These are flat vascular malformations (ectasias) that do not completely empty or collapse under focal pressure.
Plexiform angioma (c) → Typically lacks this classic diagnostic feature compared to true infantile hemangiomas, or can be confused with solid tumor lesions (like plexiform neurofibromas) which do not demonstrate compressibility.
EMQ-1. This is a slow growing, locally invasive tumor (hence also called a rodent ulcer) of a few years' duration. The edges are typically raised and rolled and occur more often on the face, head and neck:
  • A. Bowen’s disease
  • B. Extramammary Paget’s disease
  • C. Basal cell carcinoma (BCC)
  • D. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)
  • E. Malignant melanoma (MM)
Diagnosis:
Basal cell carcinoma (BCC) is historically referred to as a "rodent ulcer" due to its slow-growing, locally destructive, and invasive behavior. It classically presents on sun-exposed areas (face, head, neck) with characteristic pearly, raised, and rolled borders.

Exclusions:
Squamous cell carcinoma (D) features a more rapid onset (months rather than years) and typically presents with a raised, everted (rolled-out) border instead of a rolled, pearly edge.
EMQ-2. This occurs in the genital or perianal regions or in skin rich in apocrine glands, such as the axilla. It is a form of intraepidermal adenocarcinoma:
  • A. Bowen’s disease
  • B. Extramammary Paget’s disease
  • C. Basal cell carcinoma (BCC)
  • D. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)
  • E. Malignant melanoma (MM)
Diagnosis:
Extramammary Paget's disease is a rare intraepidermal adenocarcinoma that arises in areas rich in apocrine sweat glands, most commonly the vulva, scrotum, perianal region, and axilla.

Exclusions:
Bowen’s disease (A) is also an intraepidermal malignancy, but it represents squamous cell carcinoma in situ (derived from keratinocytes) rather than an adenocarcinoma.
EMQ-3. This is an ulcerated skin lesion of a few months' duration, with a raised, everted edge and an indurated inflamed surrounding area. A minority may have enlarged regional lymphadenopathy from metastasis:
  • A. Bowen’s disease
  • B. Extramammary Paget’s disease
  • C. Basal cell carcinoma (BCC)
  • D. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)
  • E. Malignant melanoma (MM)
Diagnosis:
Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) is characterized by a relatively rapid onset (months) and clinically presents as an ulcerated nodule or plaque with an everted (turned-outward) margin and tissue induration. Unlike BCC, it carries a distinct risk of regional lymphatic metastasis.

Exclusions:
Basal cell carcinoma (C) presents with pearly, rolled-in borders rather than everted edges, and metastatic spread is extremely rare.
EMQ-4. There is a pigmented skin lesion on the scalp that has recently changed in color and has become itchy and started to bleed. There are a few small black spots irregularly scattered around the lesion:
  • A. Bowen’s disease
  • B. Extramammary Paget’s disease
  • C. Basal cell carcinoma (BCC)
  • D. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)
  • E. Malignant melanoma (MM)
Diagnosis:
This presentation highly suggests Malignant Melanoma (MM). Key indicators include the dynamic ABCDE changes (evolving color, pruritus, bleeding) and the presence of adjacent "satellite" pigmented spots (irregularly scattered black spots).

Exclusions:
Other choices like Bowen’s disease (A) or BCC (C) do not classically present with rapid color shifts, spontaneous bleeding, and scattered dark satellite macules.
EMQ-5. There is a slowly enlarging erythematous scaly patch on the dorsum of the right hand of an elderly male:
  • A. Bowen’s disease
  • B. Extramammary Paget’s disease
  • C. Basal cell carcinoma (BCC)
  • D. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)
  • E. Malignant melanoma (MM)
Diagnosis:
Bowen’s disease (Squamous Cell Carcinoma in situ) typically presents as a slowly enlarging, well-demarcated, erythematous, and scaly plaque. It occurs frequently on sun-damaged skin, such as the dorsum of the hands of elderly individuals.

Exclusions:
Extramammary Paget’s disease (B) presents similarly as an erythematous plaque but is found in apocrine-rich intertriginous and genital sites rather than sun-exposed extremities like the hand.
169. A 29-year-old female swimmer develops a pigmented lesion on the right thigh. With reference to a pigmented lesion, there is an increased risk of developing melanoma if it is identified with which of the following?
  • a. Hutchinson freckle (lentigo maligna)
  • b. Freckle involving basal layer of skin
  • c. Congenital nevocellular nevi
  • d. Hemangioma
Diagnosis:
Congenital nevocellular nevi (particularly medium- to giant-sized variants) carry a well-documented risk of malignant transformation into melanoma over a patient's lifetime.

Exclusions:
Hutchinson freckle (a) → This is lentigo maligna, which is already a form of melanoma in situ, rather than a precursor benign lesion carrying a risk of *developing* melanoma.
Freckle (b) & Hemangioma (d) → These are benign lesions with no independent malignant potential.
170. A 45-year-old male with human papilloma virus (HPV) presents to clinic to discuss his care after being diagnosed with Bowen disease of the anus. Which of the following is true regarding his condition?
  • a. This is considered an invasive cancer.
  • b. Wide local excision should be performed.
  • c. It can be managed initially with imiquimod.
  • d. HPV 6 and 11 are the most common subtypes leading up to this condition.
  • e. Negative margins prevent local recurrence
Diagnosis:
Bowen's disease of the anus (anal intraepithelial neoplasia, or AIN III) represents squamous carcinoma in situ. Since it is non-invasive, initial conservative management with topical immunomodulatory therapy (such as imiquimod) is a standard option to avoid the high morbidity and recurrence rates associated with wide surgical excision.

Exclusions:
Invasive cancer (a) → It is limited to the intraepithelial layer (in situ).
HPV 6 & 11 (d) → High-risk oncogenic subtypes HPV 16 and 18 are the primary causative agents of anal intraepithelial neoplasia.
171. Precancerous skin lesions include all the following, except:
  • a. Bowen’s disease
  • b. xeroderma pigmentosa
  • c. Giant hairy nevus in children
  • d. Keratoacanthoma (molluscum sebaceum)
Diagnosis:
Keratoacanthoma is a rapidly growing epidermal tumor that historically undergoes spontaneous involution. It behaves as a low-grade or pseudomalignant lesion rather than a classic progressive premalignant precursor lesion.

Exclusions:
Bowen’s disease (a) & Giant hairy nevus (c) → These are classic, well-recognized precancerous lesions with a defined risk of malignant progression.
Xeroderma pigmentosa (b) → Although a hereditary multi-system genetic disorder rather than a single "lesion," it is classified clinically as a severe precancerous condition because of deficient UV DNA repair.
172. A giant hairy nevus is excised mainly because of:
  • a. The risk of malignant transformation
  • b. The unsightly appearance
  • c. The frequent bleeding on contact with clothing
  • d. The frequent attacks of infection
Diagnosis:
Giant congenital melanocytic nevi carry a substantial lifetime risk (approximately 5% to 10%) of transforming into cutaneous melanoma, which serves as the primary medical indication for prophylactic surgical excision.

Exclusions:
Cosmesis (b), bleeding (c), and infection (d) → While these factors represent valid secondary concerns, they do not dictate the core surgical indication of oncologic risk reduction.
173. Ganglion cyst is most commonly located at:
  • a. Anterior aspect of the wrist.
  • b. Posterior aspect of the wrist.
  • c. Dorsum of the hand.
  • d. Palmar surface of the hand.
Diagnosis:
Approximately 60% to 70% of all ganglion cysts in the upper extremity are located on the dorsal (posterior) aspect of the wrist, typically arising from the scapholunate ligament.

Exclusions:
Anterior aspect (a) → Volar wrist ganglia represent only about 20% of cases and are less common than dorsal lesions.
174. Among the following, which is the most common type of basal cell carcinoma (BCC)?
  • a. The superficial spreading type.
  • b. The pigmented type.
  • c. The nodular type.
  • e. None of the above.
Diagnosis:
Nodular BCC is the most common clinical and histological subtype of basal cell carcinoma, accounting for roughly 60% of cases.

Exclusions:
Superficial spreading (a) → This is a classic histological type of melanoma, not basal cell carcinoma.
175. What is the safety margin at the time of excision of a 2.5 mm thick melanoma?
  • a. 1 cm.
  • b. 2 cm.
  • c. 3 cm.
  • d. 4 cm
Diagnosis:
Current consensus guidelines from major dermatologic oncology groups recommend a 2 cm surgical margin for invasive melanomas with a Breslow thickness greater than 2.0 mm.

Exclusions:
1 cm (a) → Reserved for thin melanomas (≤ 1.0 mm in Breslow thickness).
176. Which type of melanoma has the best 5-year survival?
  • a. Nodular type.
  • b. Superficial spreading type.
  • c. Lentigo-maligna.
  • d. Acral lentiginous.
Diagnosis:
Lentigo maligna melanoma typically presents with a prolonged, indolent radial growth phase and is diagnosed at a thinner stage, giving it the most favorable 5-year survival rate among invasive melanomas.

Exclusions:
Nodular (a) & Acral lentiginous (d) → These subtypes have a dominant vertical growth phase or are diagnosed late, leading to a much poorer prognosis.
177. A 75-year-old farmer complained of a scaly, plaque-like skin lesion on his forearm with recent development of ulceration. Biopsy reveals invasive squamous carcinomas within actinic keratosis. Definitive treatment is:
  • a. Local wound care until the ulcer heals; then wide excision and repair
  • b. Excision of the lesion with frozen section determined free margins and repair
  • c. Wide excision; split-thickness skin graft and axillary node dissection
  • d. Wide excision; split-thickness graft and radiation therapy
  • e. Wide excision; split-thickness graft and chemotherapy
Diagnosis:
Surgical excision is the standard of care for localized invasive cutaneous squamous cell carcinoma. Performing the excision utilizing frozen section allows real-time margin control to ensure complete oncologic clearance before surgical closure.

Exclusions:
Axillary node dissection (c) → Unnecessary as regional physical exam reveals no clinical evidence of lymph node enlargement.
178. A 65-year-old light-complexioned male presents with a solitary scaly plaque like lesion on his forearm present for many years. The lesion is 0.5 cm in diameter. Shave biopsy reveals intra-epithelial squamous cell carcinoma (Bowen’s disease) incompletely excised. Further treatment includes:
  • a. Wide excision of the lesions and sentinel node biopsy
  • b. Referral for local radiation therapy
  • c. Excision and repair of this area, ensuring clear surgical margins
  • d. No further treatment indicated
  • e. Local application of 5-fluorouracil(5-FU) cream
Diagnosis:
An incompletely excised intraepithelial squamous cell carcinoma (Bowen's disease) must undergo definitive re-excision with clear negative margins to prevent recurrence and avoid any potential progression to invasive squamous cell carcinoma.

Exclusions:
Sentinel node biopsy (a) → In situ disease has no metastatic potential, making sentinel node mapping inappropriate.
179. A 45-year-old soccer player presents with a 6-month history of an ulcerative nodular lesion, 1.5 cm in diameter in the region of the right oral commissure. Biopsy reveals basal cell carcinoma. The preferred treatment is:
  • a. Mohs micrographic surgery and subsequent reconstruction
  • b. Excision with a clinical margin and local flap repair
  • c. Topical 5-FU
  • d. Local radiation therapy
  • e. Cryotherapy
Diagnosis:
The oral commissure is in the facial "H-zone" (high risk area). Mohs micrographic surgery (MMS) is the preferred treatment here to achieve complete microscopic margin verification while maximally conserving critical lip tissue to maintain function and cosmesis.

Exclusions:
Topical 5-FU (c) & Cryotherapy (e) → Inadequate for larger, deep-seated, or high-risk lesions in critical cosmetic regions.
180. A 67-year-old business executive and tennis player has a basal cell carcinoma removed from the right cheek. What is TRUE of basal cell carcinoma?
  • a. It may show a flat ulcer.
  • b. It may metastasize to lymph nodes.
  • c. It may metastasize to remote skin areas.
  • d. It is found exclusively in the head and neck.
  • e. It is best treated by topical 5-FU.
Diagnosis:
Basal cell carcinoma has diverse morphologic appearances; an ulcerated variant (often referred to as a "rodent ulcer") classically presents as a flat ulceration surrounded by a raised, pearly border.

Exclusions:
Metastasis (b, c) → Metastasizing basal cell carcinoma is exceedingly rare (<0.1%).
Exclusively head and neck (d) → Can develop on any hair-bearing skin, including the trunk and upper/lower extremities.
181. A 38-year-old female undergoes removal of a 21-cm skin lesion shown to be a melanoma. It is reported as Clark level 1, which implies what?
  • a. It is superficial to the basement membrane.
  • b. It is 1 mm in thickness.
  • c. It has nodal involvement.
  • d. It involves the papillary layer.
  • e. It involves the reticular dermis.
Diagnosis:
Clark level 1 signifies melanoma in situ, meaning that atypical melanocytes are entirely restricted to the epidermis and remain superficial to the basement membrane.

Exclusions:
Papillary dermis (d) → This represents Clark level 2.
Reticular dermis (e) → This represents Clark level 4.
182. Which of the following descriptions of ulcer edges denote malignancy?
  • a. Sloping
  • b. Overhanging
  • c. Everted
  • d. Punched out
  • e. Rolled
Diagnosis:
An everted (rolled-outward) edge is a classic morphological feature of rapidly proliferating malignant epithelial neoplasms, such as squamous cell carcinoma.

Exclusions:
Sloping (a) → Characteristic of healing benign venous stasis ulcers.
Punched out (d) → Typically points to trophic neuropathic or arterial ischemic ulcers.
183. Which of the following are not types of malignant melanoma?
  • a. Superficial spreading
  • b. Salmon patch
  • c. Nodular
  • d. Giant congenital pigmented naevus
  • e. Lentigo maligna
  • f. Acral lentiginous
Diagnosis:
A salmon patch is a benign, congenital vascular ectasia (capillary malformation) and is entirely unrelated to melanoma.

Exclusions:
Superficial spreading (a), Nodular (c), Lentigo maligna (e), & Acral lentiginous (f) → These represent the four standard histological variants of primary cutaneous malignant melanoma.
184. Which of the following statements are false?
  • a. The vast majority of malignant melanoma form in pre-existing naevi.
  • b. Change in color, shape, size and surface in a naevus should be looked upon with suspicion.
  • c. Incision biopsy should be done in all suspicious lesions.
  • d. Superficial spreading melanoma is the most common type.
  • e. Exposure to ultraviolet rays is a major cause of malignant melanoma.
Diagnosis:
Only about 20% to 30% of melanomas arise within a pre-existing nevus; the vast majority (approx. 70%) develop de novo from normal skin.

Exclusions:
Superficial spreading type (d) → This is a true statement; superficial spreading melanoma is the most common subtype.
185. A 45-year-old male presents with a rapidly growing painless mass in the right upper leg. It measures 4 cm in diameter. He is otherwise healthy. The initial step in workup is:
  • a. Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI)
  • b. High resolution computed tomography (CT)
  • c. Incisional biopsy
  • d. Excisional biopsy
  • e. Core needle biopsy (CNB)
Diagnosis:
For a suspected soft tissue sarcoma of the extremity, an MRI with and without contrast is the gold standard initial step. This defines anatomical margins and relationship to neurovascular structures before any tissue sampling is performed.

Exclusions:
Biopsy (c, d, e) → Performing a biopsy before staging imaging can disrupt tissue planes, compromise surgical margins, and complicate future limb-salvage reconstruction.
186. Which of the following is least likely to affect prognosis in patients with melanoma?
  • a. Level of invasion
  • b. Regional lymph node involvement
  • c. Ulceration
  • d. Mitotic rate
  • f. Serum lactate dehydrogenase (LDH)
Diagnosis:
Clark's level of invasion (anatomical level of skin layer reached) has been replaced by Breslow thickness (measured in millimeters) in the AJCC staging system because it lacks the same independent prognostic weight.

Exclusions:
Lymph node status (b), Ulceration (c), and Mitotic rate (d) → These are highly significant independent predictors of overall and disease-free survival.
187. Which of the following is true regarding dermatofibrosarcoma protuberans (DFSP)?
  • a. Gross clinical margins are helpful in guiding width of excision.
  • b. The tumor is not radiosensitive.
  • c. If it occurs on the neck, wide local excision is the surgical treatment of choice.
  • d. Local recurrence rate is lower with Mohs micrographic surgery compared with wide local excision.
Diagnosis:
Because DFSP often has asymmetric, finger-like microscopic extensions beyond its gross clinical borders, Mohs micrographic surgery (MMS) achieves significantly lower local recurrence rates than standard wide local excision.

Exclusions:
Clinical margins (a) → Because DFSP spreads microscopically beyond visible borders, simple gross clinical margin assessment is highly unreliable.
188. Which of the following is true regarding SLNB in melanoma?
  • a. A 0.5-mm deep melanoma with ulceration does not require SLNB.
  • b. SLNB is unnecessary for melanoma that has more than a 4-mm thickness.
  • c. There is a survival benefit for completion lymphadenectomy following a positive SLNB.
  • d. Blue dye used for lymph node mapping should be injected outside of the planned wide local excision.
  • e. All nodes whose radioactivity count is greater than or equal to 10% of that of the hottest node should be removed.
Diagnosis:
The sentinel lymph node identification protocol (the "10% rule") dictates that any lymph node with an in vivo radioisotope count ≥ 10% of the count of the hottest sentinel node must be harvested.

Exclusions:
Completion lymphadenectomy (c) → The MSLT-II trial demonstrated no overall survival benefit for routine completion lymph node dissection following a positive SLNB.
189. A 51-year-old female presents with complaints of night sweats, fevers, and weight loss. On exam, she has enlarged cervical (2 cm), axillary (2 cm), and inguinal lymph nodes (3 cm). She is otherwise healthy. Which of the following would be the next step in establishing a diagnosis?
  • a. Fine-needle aspirate (FNA) of inguinal lymph node
  • b. Surgical biopsy of cervical lymph node
  • c. Core needle biopsy of inguinal lymph node
  • d. FNA of cervical lymph node
  • e. Surgical biopsy of axillary lymph node
Diagnosis:
For suspected lymphoma (suggested by "B symptoms" of fever, night sweats, and weight loss with generalized lymphadenopathy), an excisional/surgical biopsy of an entire node is required to preserve nodal architecture. Cervical nodes are preferred because inguinal lymph nodes are frequently chronically inflamed/reactive, reducing diagnostic yield.

Exclusions:
FNA (a, d) & Core biopsy (c) → Fine-needle aspirates destroy tissue architecture, which is critical for the accurate diagnosis and typing of lymphomas.
190. Which of the following is true regarding sarcoma?
  • a. Kaposi sarcoma is a common cause of death in patients with AIDS.
  • b. Embryonal subtype is a rare childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.
  • c. Embryonal subtype has the worst prognosis in childhood rhabdomyosarcoma.
  • d. Osteosarcoma arises from stromal cells.
  • e. Osteosarcoma is one of the rarest malignant bone tumors.
Diagnosis:
Osteosarcoma is a malignant bone tumor characterized by the direct production of osteoid or immature bone by malignant mesenchymal/stromal osteoblast precursor cells.

Exclusions:
Osteosarcoma rarity (e) → False; osteosarcoma is the most common primary malignant bone tumor.
191. A 19-year-old autistic male presents with severe pain in the second digit of the right hand. He has a fever of 103°F. His mother states that he has recently been biting his nails. On exam, he is tender lateral to the nail fold of the digit, and it appears swollen and red. Which of the following is the best management?
  • a. Warm compresses and oral antibiotic coverage for skin flora
  • b. Incision and drainage at the mid-digital pulp
  • c. Incision at lateral nail fold
  • d. Incision at lateral nail fold plus oral antibiotic coverage for skin flora
  • e. Incision at lateral nail fold plus oral antibiotic coverage for skin flora and anaerobic bacteria
Diagnosis:
The patient has severe acute paronychia with systemic infection (fever 103°F). Because he is a chronic nail-biter, oral pathogens (including anaerobes like *Eikenella corrodens*) are introduced. Thus, treatment must include incision and drainage at the lateral nail fold plus broad antibiotics covering skin flora and oral anaerobes.

Exclusions:
Warm compresses only (a) → Inadequate due to the presence of systemic symptoms and clear local infection requiring drainage.
Mid-digital pulp incision (b) → Pulp incision is used for felons, not paronychias.
192. A 3-mm basal cell carcinoma (BCC) of the skin should be treated with:
  • a. Biopsy and gross total excision
  • b. Dermatologic laser vaporization
  • c. Excision with 2- to 4-mm normal margin
  • d. Electrodesiccation
Diagnosis:
Standard guidelines recommend surgical excision with a 2- to 4-mm margin of normal skin for small (≤ 2 cm), low-risk primary basal cell carcinomas.

Exclusions:
Dermatologic laser (b) → Laser vaporization is not standard therapy for invasive basal cell carcinoma because it does not allow for histologic margin verification.
193. More than half of patients treated for BCC will experience a recurrence within:
  • a. 6 months
  • b. 1 years
  • c. 2 years
  • d. 3 years
Diagnosis:
Long-term clinical follow-up data show that more than 50% of BCC recurrences occur within 3 years of initial treatment.

Exclusions:
6 months (a) & 1 year (b) → Most recurrences take longer to manifest clinically due to the slow-growing nature of basal cell carcinoma.
194. The primary risk factor for the development of squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) is:
  • a. UV exposure
  • b. Cigarette smoking
  • c. Chemical agents
  • d. Chronic, nonhealing wounds
Diagnosis:
Cumulative exposure to ultraviolet (UV) radiation from sunlight or tanning beds is the single most important environmental risk factor for the development of cutaneous SCC.

Exclusions:
Cigarette smoking (b) & Nonhealing wounds (d) → While these are indeed recognized risk factors (especially for mucosal SCC or Marjolin’s ulcer), they are much less common than UV radiation-induced cases.
195. In the ABCDE of melanoma, the D stands for diameter greater than:
  • a. 2mm
  • b. 4mm
  • c. 6mm
  • d. 8mm
Diagnosis:
In the standard ABCDE rule for cutaneous melanoma, "D" stands for diameter greater than 6 mm (about the size of a pencil eraser).

Exclusions:
2mm (a) & 4mm (b) → Although early melanomas can be smaller, 6 mm remains the clinical screening threshold.
196. The most common site of distant metastasis for melanoma is:
  • a. Brain
  • b. Lung
  • c. Gastrointestinal tract
  • d. Distant skin
Diagnosis:
The lungs are the most common initial site of hematogenous/visceral distant metastasis for patients with cutaneous melanoma.

Exclusions:
Brain (a) → Brain metastases are highly common and serious in advanced melanoma, but the lung remains the most common first visceral site.
197. The most common subtype of melanoma is:
  • a. Lentigo maligna
  • b. Acral lentiginous
  • c. Superficial spreading
  • d. Nodular
Diagnosis:
Superficial spreading melanoma is the most common histological subtype of cutaneous melanoma, accounting for approximately 70% of all cases in light-complexioned individuals.

Exclusions:
Acral lentiginous (b) → The least common subtype overall, although it is the most common subtype in individuals of Asian or African descent.
198. Ocular melanoma:
  • a. Exclusively metastasizes to the lungs
  • b. Exclusively metastasizes to the brain
  • c. Exclusively metastasizes to regional lymph nodes
  • d. Exclusively metastasizes to the liver
Diagnosis:
Uveal/ocular melanoma has a unique, strong hematogenous tropism for the liver, which is the site of initial metastasis in over 90% of metastatic cases.

Exclusions:
Regional lymph nodes (c) → The eye lacks lymphatic drainage, meaning regional lymphatic metastasis is extremely rare except in cases of conjunctival involvement.
199. What is the most common melanoma in patients with dark skin?
  • a. Nodular
  • b. Superficial spreading
  • c. Acral lentiginous
  • d. Lentigo maligna
Diagnosis:
Acral lentiginous melanoma (arising on the soles, palms, and nail beds) is the most common melanoma subtype diagnosed in patients with dark skin tones (African, Asian, Hispanic populations).

Exclusions:
Superficial spreading (b) → Highly common in fair-complexioned populations with a history of intermittent UV exposure, but less common in dark-skinned individuals.
200. Kaposi sarcoma:
  • a. Excision is the treatment of choice
  • b. Is predominantly found on the skin
  • c. Appears as rubbery, blue nodules
  • d. Is most often seen in patients in their fifth decade of life
Diagnosis:
For single, small, isolated, symptomatic skin lesions of Kaposi's sarcoma (KS), surgical excision can be considered a curative and symptomatic localized therapy option.

Exclusions:
Predominantly skin (b) → While skin is the most visible site, classic and HIV-associated KS often involves systemic viscera (lungs, GI tract, lymph nodes).
201. The following is NOT a prognostic indicator for patients with a sentinel node containing metastatic melanoma:
  • a. Patient age
  • b. Site of metastasis
  • c. Number of positive nodes
  • d. Thickness, mitotic rate, and ulceration of primary tumor
Diagnosis:
The anatomical location/basin (site) of the sentinel lymph node metastasis itself has no independent prognostic value compared to powerful predictors such as age, tumor burden (number of positive nodes), and primary tumor parameters (Breslow thickness, ulceration, mitotic rate).

Exclusions:
Positive nodes count (c) & Mitotic rate/ulceration (d) → These are validated independent prognostic factors directly dictating overall survival.
202. A patient with a 5-mm deep melanoma of the thigh and no clinically positive nodes should undergo which procedure?
  • a. Resection of the primary only
  • b. Superficial femoral node resection
  • c. Superficial and deep femoral node resection
  • d. Resection of femoral and inguinal nodal basins
Diagnosis:
In patients with thick melanomas but clinically negative lymph nodes, elective lymph node dissection (therapeutic nodal resection) is not indicated as it does not improve overall survival. The standard of care is resection of the primary only (wide local excision), typically coupled with staging via sentinel lymph node biopsy.

Exclusions:
Nodal resection (b, c, d) → Complete nodal basin dissections are therapeutic procedures reserved for patients with biopsy-proven or clinically positive nodal disease.
203. A 65-year-old patient who spends winters in Florida presents with a painless, ulcerated lesion on his right cheek. The lesion has been present for 1 year. Physical examination of the patient’s neck reveals no lymph node enlargement. The most likely diagnosis is:
  • a. Melanoma
  • b. BCC
  • c. secondaries
  • d. Sebaceous cyst
Diagnosis:
A slowly growing, painless, ulcerated cheek lesion in an elderly patient with a history of significant sun exposure (Florida winters) and no regional lymphadenopathy is highly characteristic of Basal Cell Carcinoma (BCC / rodent ulcer).

Exclusions:
Sebaceous cyst (d) → Usually a fluctuating subcutaneous nodule with a punctum, not a chronically ulcerated skin lesion.

Section 6: Surgical Infection (Q204–Q251)

204. Patient in the ICU has been on ventilator support for 3 weeks. He has a new onset elevated WBC count, fever, and consolidation seen on chest X-ray. What is an appropriate next step?
  • a. Exchange endotracheal tube and change respiratory circuit.
  • b. Obtain bronchoalveolar lavage.
  • c. Start treatment with empiric penicillin G.
  • d. Obtain chest CT.
Diagnosis: Ventilator-Associated Pneumonia (VAP)
  • Clinical Presentation → Mechanical ventilation > 48h + new/progressive infiltrate + leukocytosis/fever.
    • Action → Obtain quantitative lower respiratory tract cultures via bronchoalveolar lavage (BAL) or endotracheal aspirate before altering/starting empiric antibiotics.
  • Exclusions:
    • Tube exchange (a) → Unnecessary, increases aspiration risk.
    • Penicillin G (c) → Insufficient; typical VAP pathogens are resistant Gram-negative bacilli or S. aureus.
    • Chest CT (d) → Unnecessary; clinical signs and chest X-ray are sufficient for diagnosis.
205. Which of the following is most suggestive of a necrotizing soft tissue infection and would mandate immediate surgical exploration?
  • a. A small amount of grayish, cloudy fluid from a wound
  • b. Red, swollen extremity which is tender to palpation
  • c. Soft tissue infection with a fever > 40ºC
  • d. Induration with pitting edema on the trunk
Clinical Signs: Necrotizing Soft Tissue Infection (NSTI)
  • Pathognomonic sign → "Dishwater pus" (thin, grayish, cloudy fluid).
    • Significance → Indicates liquefactive fascial necrosis, which is a surgical emergency requiring immediate debridement.
  • Exclusions:
    • Erythema/tenderness (b) → Highly typical of superficial cellulitis; non-specific.
    • Fever (c) & Induration (d) → Can be present in non-necrotizing cellulitis or simple abscesses; do not selectively mandate emergency operative exploration over "dishwater" fluid.
206. Which factor does NOT influence the development of surgical site infections (SSIs)?
  • a. Duration of procedure
  • b. Degree of microbial contamination of the wound
  • c. Malnutrition
  • d. General anesthesia
Pathophysiology: SSI Development Factors
  • SSI variables → Interplay of bacterial contamination, procedure length, and host factors (malnutrition, immune status).
    • Anesthesia effect → Choice of anesthesia (general vs. local) does not independently alter SSI risk.
  • Exclusions:
    • Duration (a) → Longer procedures expose tissue to airborne pathogens.
    • Contamination (b) → Directly correlates with wound class and risk.
    • Malnutrition (c) → Blunts host immune responses and tissue remodeling.
207. What is NOT an early goal in treatment of severe sepsis?
  • a. Mean arterial pressure > 65 mm Hg
  • b. Central venous pressure 8 to 12 mm Hg
  • c. Urine output > 0.5 cc/kg/h
  • d. Serum lactate < 2 mmol/L
Clinical Management: Early Goal-Directed Therapy (EGDT)
  • Sepsis targets (first 6 hours) → MAP > 65 mmHg, CVP 8-12 mmHg, Urine output > 0.5 mL/kg/h.
    • Lactate clearance → While lowering lactate is targeted, achieving absolute < 2 mmol/L is a delayed metabolic clearance target, not an acute goal.
  • Exclusions:
    • MAP (a) → Assures vital organ perfusion pressure.
    • CVP (b) → Confirms adequate intravascular volume/preload.
    • Urine output (c) → Indirect marker of renal perfusion.
208. All of the following statements about surgical site infections (SSIs) are true except:
  • a. Infection in the musculofascial tissues is known as deep SSI.
  • b. The patient may have systemic signs in a minor SSI.
  • c. Infection causing delay in hospital discharge is a major SSI.
  • d. Surveillance for surgical site infection should be done for a year after implanted joint surgery.
Clinical Definition: Classification of SSIs
  • Minor SSI features → Localized wound erythema/discharge without systemic findings (fever, chills).
    • Interpretation → Stating systemic signs are present in minor SSI is incorrect.
  • Exclusions:
    • Musculofascial (a) → Definition of deep incisional SSI.
    • Major SSI (c) → Defined by systemic illness and prolonged hospital stays.
    • Surveillance (d) → Standard protocol requires 1 year follow-up when joint prostheses are placed.
209. All of the following is a cause of secondary (or exogenous) infection except:
  • a. Poor hand-washing technique
  • b. Perforated diverticular disease
  • c. Anastomotic leak
  • d. Inadequate air filtration in the theatre.
Pathophysiology: Endogenous vs. Exogenous Infection
  • Endogenous (Primary) infection → Organisms originate inside the host (e.g., gut flora leakage).
    • Perforated diverticular disease → Classic endogenous infection resulting from the escape of native colon flora.
  • Exclusions:
    • Poor hand-washing (a) & Air filtration (d) → Classic vectors for exogenous pathogen entry.
    • Anastomotic leak (c) → Frequently leads to secondary localized infection, though flora is native.
210. All of the following statements regarding abscesses are true except:
  • a. Staphylococcus aureus is one of the most common causative organisms.
  • b. The abscess wall is composed of epithelium.
  • c. Antibiotics are indicated if there is evidence of cellulitis.
  • d. Actinomyces can cause a chronic abscess.
Pathophysiology: Abscess Wall Composition
  • Pyogenic wall → Formed by a fibrous/fibrinous capsule (pyogenic membrane) and vascular granulation tissue, not epithelial cells.
  • Exclusions:
    • S. aureus (a) → Most common cause of cutaneous abscesses.
    • Antibiotics (c) → Indicated specifically when surrounding cellulitis/erysipelas is present.
    • Actinomyces (d) → Classical cause of chronic, slow-growing "sulfur granule" abscesses in jaw/cervical areas.
211. Which of the following is associated with endotoxin?
  • a. Streptococcus pyogenes
  • b. Bacteroides fragilis
  • c. Clostridium tetani
  • d. Staphylococcus aureus
  • e. Clostridium perfringens
Pathophysiology: Endotoxins
  • Endotoxin location → Specifically found in the outer membrane (lipopolysaccharide) of Gram-negative bacteria.
    • Bacteroides fragilis → Gram-negative obligate anaerobic bacillus, thus possessing endotoxin.
  • Exclusions:
    • Streptococcus (a), Clostridium (c, e), Staphylococcus (d) → Gram-positive organisms that do not have outer membrane endotoxins.
212. Which areas likely do NOT contain resident microorganisms?
  • a. Terminal ileum
  • b. Oropharynx
  • c. Main pancreatic duct
  • d. Nares
Anatomy & Microbiology: Sterile Body Sites
  • Sterility → Deep exocrine glands and their ducts (e.g., main pancreatic duct, biliary tract) are normally sterile in healthy hosts.
  • Exclusions:
    • Terminal ileum (a) → Highly populated with enteric bacteria.
    • Oropharynx (b) & Nares (d) → Heavily colonized with permanent microflora (Streptococcus, Staphylococcus).
213. Which is NOT a component of systemic inflammatory response syndrome (SIRS)?
  • a. Temperature
  • b. White blood cell (WBC) count
  • c. Blood pressure
  • d. Heart rate
Clinical Definition: SIRS Criteria
  • Criteria targets → 1) Temp (>38 or <36ºC), 2) HR (>90), 3) RR (>20), 4) WBC (>12k or <4k).
    • Exclusion → Blood pressure is not a criteria point for diagnosing SIRS (it defines shock/severity).
  • Exclusions:
    • Temp (a), WBC (b), HR (d) → Core diagnostic variables of standard SIRS.
214. The most appropriate treatment of a 4-cm hepatic abscess is
  • a. Antibiotic therapy alone
  • b. Aspiration for culture and antibiotic therapy
  • c. Percutaneous drainage and antibiotic therapy
  • d. Operative exploration, open drainage of the abscess,
Clinical Management: Pyogenic Liver Abscess
  • Lesions > 3 cm → Poor response to medication alone due to diffusion limits.
    • Best approach → US/CT-guided percutaneous catheter drainage + systemic antibiotics.
  • Exclusions:
    • Antibiotics alone (a) → High risk of therapeutic failure for 4 cm cavities.
    • Aspiration (b) → Simple needle aspiration has a high re-accumulation/recurrence rate.
    • Operative drainage (d) → Unnecessary unless the patient has multiple, failed percutaneous, or ruptured lesions.
215. All of the following statements regarding cellulitis are true except:
  • a. This is non-suppurative invasive infection of tissues.
  • b. It is poorly localized.
  • c. It is commonly caused by Clostridium perfringens.
  • d. Systemic signs are not present.
  • e. Blood culture is usually positive.
Clinical Signs: Cellulitis Features
  • Invasiveness → Cellulitis spreads extensively in subdermal tissues.
    • Consequence → Highly typical to present with systemic signs (fever, systemic toxicity).
  • Exclusions:
    • Non-suppurative (a) & Poorly localized (b) → True; distinguishes cellulitis from abscess.
    • C. perfringens (c) → True; causes anaerobic cellulitis.
    • Blood culture (e) → Standard diagnostic step (though often clinically negative, it is listed as a true MCQ statement).
216. What is FALSE regarding intravascular catheter infections?
  • a. Selected low-virulence infections can be treated with a prolonged course of antibiotics.
  • b. In high-risk patients, prophylactic antibiotics infused through the catheter can reduce the rate of catheter infections.
  • c. Bacteremia with gram-negative bacteria or fungi should prompt catheter removal.
  • d. All of the above
Clinical Management: Catheter Infection Prophylaxis
  • Prophylactic infusions → Infusing antibiotics routine-style through the catheter is contraindicated because it promotes resistance.
    • Verification → Statement (b) is therefore false.
  • Exclusions:
    • Low-virulence (a) → Coagulase-negative Staph lines can sometimes be salvaged.
    • Gram-negative/Fungal (c) → Mandatory line removal due to high complication rates.
217. During a laparoscopic appendectomy, a large bowel injury was caused during trocar placement with spillage of bowel contents into the abdomen. What class of surgical wound is this?
  • a. Class I (clean)
  • b. Class II (clean/contaminated)
  • c. Class III (contaminated)
  • d. Class IV (dirty)
Clinical Definition: Wound Classification
  • Class III (Contaminated) definition → Fresh, open traumatic wounds, major breaks in aseptic technique, or gross gastrointestinal spillage.
    • Clinical scenario → Trocar perforation of the colon with bowel leakage is Class III.
  • Exclusions:
    • Class I (a) → No entry into respiratory/digestive tract.
    • Class II (b) → Controlled, uncomplicated entry into hollow viscera.
    • Class IV (d) → Purulent infection or perforated viscus encountered *prior* to skin incision.
218. Regarding postoperative urinary tract infections (UTIs):
  • a. Are usually treated with a 7- to 10-day course of antibiotics.
  • b. Initial therapy should be directed by results of urine culture.
  • c. are established by >10^4 CFU/mL of bacteria in urine culture in asymptomatic patients.
  • d. Can be reduced by irrigating indwelling Foley catheters daily.
Clinical Management: Postoperative UTIs
  • Antibiotic Stewardship → Directing treatment by culture results minimizes resistance.
    • Standard choice → (b) is the correct answer.
  • Exclusions:
    • 7-10 days (a) → Uncomplicated UTIs are safely treated with short courses (3-5 days).
    • CFU limit (c) → Diagnosis in asymptomatic patients requires >10^5 CFU/mL.
    • Catheter irrigation (d) → Daily irrigation increases contamination and UTI rates.
219. Transferrin plays a role in host defense by:
  • a. Sequestering iron, which is necessary for microbial growth
  • b. Increasing the ability of fibrinogen to trap microbes
  • c. Direct injury to the bacterial cell membrane
  • d. Direct injury to the bacterial mitochondria
Pathophysiology: Nutritional Immunity
  • Mechanism → Transferrin binds free ferric iron in blood with high affinity, preventing pathogens from acquiring it.
    • Importance → Iron is critical for microbial proliferation.
  • Exclusions:
    • Fibrinogen (b) → Unrelated to transferrin.
    • Direct cell membrane/mitochondria injury (c, d) → Done by defensins and oxidative burst, not transferrin.
220. A patient with necrotizing pancreatitis undergoes computed tomography (CT)-guided aspiration, which results in growth of Escherichia coli on culture. The most appropriate treatment is
  • a. Culture-appropriate antibiotic therapy
  • b. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography with sphincterotomy
  • c. CT-guided placement of drain(s)
  • d. Exploratory laparotomy
Clinical Management: Infected Pancreatic Necrosis
  • Definitive Therapy → Documentation of infected pancreatic necrosis (E. coli on FNA) classically demands open surgical debridement.
    • Note → While modern pathways support "step-up" drain strategies first, exploratory laparotomy remains the classic gold standard answer.
  • Exclusions:
    • Antibiotics alone (a) → Cannot clear necrotic collections.
    • ERCP (b) → Used for biliary obstruction/cholangitis.
    • CT-drain (c) → Part of step-up, but classically surgery (d) is final.
221. The first step in the evaluation and treatment of a patient with an infected bug bite on the leg with cellulitis, bullae, thin grayish fluid draining from the wound, and pain out of proportion to the physical findings is
  • a. Obtain C-reactive protein
  • b. CT scan of the leg
  • c. Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the leg
  • d. Operative exploration
Clinical Management: Necrotizing Fasciitis
  • Clinical Clues → "Pain out of proportion," "bullae," and "grayish fluid" are diagnostic.
    • First step → Emergency operative surgical exploration and direct debridement.
  • Exclusions:
    • CRP (a) → Delays crucial therapy.
    • CT (b) & MRI (c) → Useful to find fascial gas but must never delay immediate surgery.
222. Which of the following can be seen in SIRS?
  • a. Hypothermia ( <36 º C)
  • b. White cell count (WCC) < 4 _ 1000/dL
  • c. Tachycardia ( >90/min)
  • d. Tachypnoea ( >20/min).
Clinical Definition: SIRS Criteria
  • SIRS components → Standard criteria include (a), (b), (c), and (d).
    • Note → Tachypnea (d) is highlighted in the source PDF key as the correct choice.
  • Exclusions:
    • All options are valid, but (d) is designated in the key.
223. A 62-year-old man is postoperative day 6 from an elective laparoscopic sigmoid colectomy for recurrent diverticulitis. He is now complaining of tenesmus and urinary retention. Which of the following represents the most appropriate next step in management?
  • a. Transition to nonnarcotic pain medications
  • b. Bladder scan and in-and-out catheterization as needed
  • c. Abdominal radiography
  • d. Computed tomography (CT)
  • e. Diagnostic laparoscopy
Diagnosis: Postoperative Pelvic Abscess
  • Clinical Picture → Tenesmus + urinary retention + low fevers on post-op day 6 suggests a pelvic fluid collection compressing rectum and bladder.
    • First step → Abdominal/pelvic CT scan to locate the abscess and guide drainage.
  • Exclusions:
    • Pain meds (a) → Ignores underlying infection.
    • Bladder scan (b) → Addresses retention but misses the compressive cause.
    • X-ray (c) → Insufficient to diagnose soft tissue pelvic fluid.
    • Laparoscopy (e) → Overly invasive initial diagnostic step.
224. all of the following statements regarding bacteria in surgical infection are true except:
  • a. Beta-hemolytic Streptococcus is always associated with infection.
  • b. All streptococci are sensitive to penicillin and erythromycin.
  • c. Staphylococci are normally resident in the nasopharynx of up to 15 per cent of the population.
  • d. Gram-negative bacilli are a major cause of infection related to urethral catheterization.
Pathophysiology: Surgical Microbiology
  • Colonization → Beta-hemolytic Streptococcus can colonize vagina/skin without clinical disease.
    • Verification → Statement (a) is therefore false.
  • Exclusions:
    • Streptococci (b) → Historically true in exam keys (though clinical resistance is emerging).
    • Staph nasopharynx (c) → True; ~15% are healthy nasal carriers.
    • Gram-negative bacilli (d) → True; E. coli/Pseudomonas dominate catheter UTIs.
225. All of the following statements about types of wounds are true except:
  • a. The infection rate in a ‘clean wound’ is between 1 and 2 per cent.
  • b. The wound after biliary surgery is classified as ‘contaminated’.
  • c. A ‘clean-contaminated wound’ has an infection rate of less than 10 per cent.
  • d. The role of prophylactic antibiotics in non-prosthetic clean surgery is controversial.
Clinical Definition: Wound Classification
  • Biliary classification → Elective cholecystectomy without active infection is Class II (Clean-Contaminated), not Contaminated.
    • Verification → Statement (b) is therefore false.
  • Exclusions:
    • Clean wounds (a) → Expected SSI rate is 1-2%.
    • Clean-contaminated (c) → Expected SSI rate is < 10%.
    • Clean prophylaxis (d) → Use in simple repairs without mesh is debated.
226. Which of the following is least likely to contribute to a surgical site infection?
  • a. American Society of Anesthesiologists physical status
  • b. Length of operation
  • c. Serum glucose level
  • d. Body temperature
  • e. Hemoglobin level
Pathophysiology: SSI Risks
  • Hemoglobin vs SSI → Mild-to-moderate low Hb (anemia) without hypoxemia does not directly increase infection rate.
    • Note → Perfusion is more critical than absolute Hb level.
  • Exclusions:
    • ASA (a) & Length (b) → Strong independent risk factors.
    • Hyperglycemia (c) → Impairs neutrophil function.
    • Hypothermia (d) → Vasoconstricts wound bed, lowering oxygen delivery.
227. The organism most commonly associated with acute mesenteric lymphadenitis is:
  • a. Campylobacter jejuni
  • b. Escherichia coli
  • c. Enterococcus
  • d. Yersinia enterocolitica
Pathophysiology: Mesenteric Lymphadenitis
  • Pathogen profile → Yersinia enterocolitica infects mesenteric lymph nodes, presenting as RLQ pain that mimics appendicitis (pseudoappendicitis).
  • Exclusions:
    • C. jejuni (a) → Typically causes bloody diarrhea.
    • E. coli (b) / Enterococcus (c) → Cause urinary or peritonitis abscesses, not isolated adenitis.
228. What is the estimated risk of transmission of HIV from a needlestick from a source with HIV-infected blood?
  • a. <0.5%
  • b. 1%
  • c. 5%
  • d. 10%
Clinical Safety: HIV Needle Transmission
  • Statistics → The average transmission risk after percutaneous needle injury involving HIV-infected blood is ~0.3% (<0.5%).
  • Exclusions:
    • 1% (b), 5% (c), 10% (d) → Overestimate real transmission rates (Contrast with Hep B which can be up to 30%).
229. Which of the following is true regarding tetanus?
  • a. It is highly contagious.
  • b. Trismus is usually the first sign.
  • c. It is caused by a gram-negative anaerobic rod.
  • d. Immunization is not required if there is a previous history of surviving tetanus.
  • e. The diagnosis is established by demonstrating the organisms in a wound.
Clinical Signs: Tetanus
  • Symptom progression → Trismus (lockjaw) is the earliest and most classic presenting sign.
  • Exclusions:
    • Contagious (a) → False; tetanus does not spread person-to-person.
    • Gram-negative (c) → False; Clostridium tetani is Gram-positive.
    • Surviving infection (d) → False; natural disease does not provide immunity.
    • Diagnosis (e) → False; diagnosis is purely clinical.
230. Forty-eight hours after total mastectomy, high fever, diarrhea, vomiting, redness of skin of entire body, and hypotension develop in a 30-year-old patient. The mastectomy incision appears unremarkable. The following day diffuse desquamation develops. The most likely etiology is:
  • a. Clostridium perfringens
  • b. Clostridium difficile
  • c. β-Hemolytic Streptococcus
  • d. Staphylococcus aureus
  • e. Staphylococcus epidermidis
Diagnosis: Toxic Shock Syndrome (TSS)
  • Presentation → Sudden onset fever, erythroderma (sunburn-like rash), hypotension, and subsequent skin peeling (desquamation).
    • Pathophysiology → Mediated by TSST-1 toxin released by Staphylococcus aureus.
  • Exclusions:
    • C. perfringens (a) → Causes gas gangrene.
    • C. difficile (b) → Causes colitis without desquamating rash.
    • S. epidermidis (e) → Lacks ability to secrete TSST-1 toxin.
231. A 60-year-old man presents with gas gangrene of his left leg requiring below-knee amputation. Wound cultures were positive for Clostridium septicum. Additional workup should include:
  • a. Head CT scan
  • b. Bronchoscopy
  • c. Colonoscopy
  • d. HIV serology
  • e. Chest CT scan
Clinical Management: Clostridium septicum Association
  • Oncological correlation → C. septicum bacteremia or tissue infection is strongly associated with colon cancer.
    • Next step → Urgent colonoscopy to screen for occult GI malignancies once stable.
  • Exclusions:
    • Head CT (a), Bronchoscopy (b), Chest CT (e) → Unrelated to C. septicum pathophysiology.
232. Possible exposure to anthrax should be initially treated with
  • a. Colistin
  • b. Ciprofloxacin or doxycycline
  • c. Amoxicillin
  • d. Observation
Clinical Management: Anthrax Prophylaxis
  • Standard regimen → Oral Ciprofloxacin or Doxycycline for 60 days is recommended for post-exposure prophylaxis.
  • Exclusions:
    • Colistin (a) → Active only against Gram-negatives (Bacillus is Gram-positive).
