    • Observation (d) → Dangerous; inhalation anthrax is fatal once symptoms present.
233. A chronic carrier state occurs with hepatitis C infection in what percentage of patients?
  • a. 90-99%
  • b. 75-80%
  • c. 50-60%
  • d. 10-30%
Pathophysiology: Hepatitis C Chronicity
  • Carrier rate → Unlike Hep B, approximately 75% to 80% of individuals infected with HCV develop chronic infection.
  • Exclusions:
    • 90-99% (a) → Overestimate.
    • 50-60% (c) / 10-30% (d) → Underestimate chronicity risk.
234. all of the following statements regarding clostridial wound infections are true except:
  • a. Clostridia are Gram-positive aerobic spore-bearing cocci.
  • b. Thin, brown and sweet-smelling exudate is seen in gas gangrene.
  • c. Necrotic and foreign material in wounds increase risk.
  • d. The spores are widely spread in soil and manure.
Pathophysiology: Clostridia Characteristics
  • Definition → Clostridium species are Gram-positive, anaerobic (not aerobic) spore-bearing bacilli/rods (not cocci).
    • Verification → Statement (a) is therefore false.
  • Exclusions:
    • Exudate (b) → True; watery, brownish sweet-smelling discharge is typical in gas gangrene.
    • Risk factors (c) → True; devitalized tissue creates the anaerobic environment required for spore germination.
    • Spores (d) → True; spores are highly prevalent in soil.
235. Patients with severe, necrotizing pancreatitis should be treated with
  • a. No antibiotics unless CT -guided aspiration of the area yields positive cultures
  • b. Empiric cefoxitin or cefotetan
  • c. Empiric cefuroxime plus gentamicin
  • d. Empiric carbapenems or fluoroquinolones
Clinical Management: Pancreatic Tissue Penetration
  • Drug selection → Carbapenems and fluoroquinolones are preferred because they achieve high concentration in pancreatic tissues.
  • Exclusions:
    • Cefoxitin (b) & Gentamicin (c) → Achieve poor pancreatic tissue levels.
236. A patient with a localized wound infection after surgery should be treated with
  • a. Antibiotics and warm soaks to the wound
  • b. Antibiotics alone
  • c. Antibiotics and opening the wound
  • d. Incision and drainage alone
Clinical Management: Localized SSI
  • Standard protocol → Uncomplicated localized wound infections are treated by opening the wound and evacuating pus (incision/drainage). Systemic antibiotics are unnecessary unless cellulitis is present.
  • Exclusions:
    • Antibiotics (a, b, c) → Standard superficial infections require physical drainage, not systemic therapy.
237. The appropriate duration of antibiotic therapy for nosocomial urinary tract infection is:
  • a. 3-5 days
  • b. 7-10 days
  • c. 21 days
  • d. Until the patient is asymptomatic and the urinalysis is normal
Clinical Management: Nosocomial UTIs
  • Standard duration → Uncomplicated catheter-associated/nosocomial UTIs resolve rapidly with a 3 to 5 day antibiotic course.
  • Exclusions:
    • 7-10 days (b) → Reserved for complicated/febrile cases.
    • 21 days (c) → Unnecessarily prolonged.
238. Peripheral neuropathy with prolonged use is most commonly associated with:
  • a. Clindamycin
  • b. Metronidazole
  • c. Ciprofloxacin
  • d. Gentamicin
  • e. Vancomycin
Pharmacology: Metronidazole Side Effects
  • Adverse event → Prolonged use of Metronidazole (> 3 weeks) causes axonal sensory peripheral neuropathy.
  • Exclusions:
    • Clindamycin (a) → Strongly linked with C. difficile colitis.
    • Gentamicin (d) → Highly associated with ototoxicity/nephrotoxicity.
    • Vancomycin (e) → Red man syndrome/nephrotoxicity.
239. The most effective postexposure prophylaxis for a surgeon stuck with a needle while operating on an HIV-positive patient is
  • a. None (no effective treatment is known).
  • b. Two- or three-drug therapy started within hours of exposure.
  • c. Single drug therapy started within 24 hours of exposure.
  • d. Triple drug therapy started within 24 hours of exposure
Clinical Management: HIV PEP
  • Post-exposure prophylaxis (PEP) → Needs to be initiated within hours of the injury, using a 2 or 3 drug antiretroviral combination for 28 days.
  • Exclusions:
    • No treatment (a) → PEP reduces risk of transmission by >80%.
    • 24-hour delay (c, d) → Resuscitative therapy is less effective if delayed.
240. Patients with a penicillin allergy are LEAST likely to have a cross-reaction with
  • a. Synthetic penicillins
  • b. Carbapenems
  • c. Cephalosporins
  • d. Monobactams
Pharmacology: Cross-Reactivity
  • Structure difference → Monobactams (Aztreonam) contain a monocyclic beta-lactam ring that does not cross-react with other penicillins.
  • Exclusions:
    • Synthetic penicillins (a) → Complete cross-reactivity.
    • Carbapenems (b) & Cephalosporins (c) → Share a structured bicyclic ring with potential cross-reactivity.
241. all of the following affects the choice of prophylactic antibiotic except:
  • a. The expected spectrum of organisms likely to be encountered
  • b. Cost
  • c. Personal preference
  • d. Hospital policies
Clinical Management: Antibiotic Prophylaxis
  • Stewardship → Prophylaxis must be evidence-based and target common pathogens, not reflect personal preferences.
  • Exclusions:
    • Spectrum (a), Cost (b), Policies (d) → Standard considerations for institutional protocols.
242. all of the following may require more than one dose of prophylactic antibiotic except:
  • a. Prolonged operations
  • b. Excessive blood loss
  • c. Gastrointestinal surgery
  • d. Insertion of prosthesis
Clinical Management: Redosing Criteria
  • Redosing rules → Indicated if the procedure duration exceeds 2 half-lives of the antibiotic (a) or if blood loss exceeds 1500 mL (b). GI surgery itself does not require multiple doses; a single pre-operative dose is standard.
  • Exclusions:
    • Prolonged (a) & Blood loss (b) → Standard physiological indications for redosing.
243. Which of the following statements are true?
  • a. Identification of the causative organism should be done before starting antibiotics.
  • b. Wounds are best managed by delayed primary or secondary closure.
  • c. Subcuticular continuous skin closure decreases the incidence of wound infection.
  • d. Polymeric films can be useful in infected wounds.
Clinical Management: Wound Closure
  • Contaminated wounds → To avoid abscess formation, contaminated/infected wounds are best closed via delayed primary closure (on day 5) or left open to heal by secondary intention.
  • Exclusions:
    • Identification (a) → Sepsis requires immediate empiric therapy without waiting for cultures.
    • Subcuticular (c) → Continuous subcuticular skin closure in contaminated wounds increases the infection rate.
244. Which of the following is true regarding gentamicin?
  • a. It has strong activity against streptococci.
  • b. It has a high therapeutic index.
  • c. It has poor activity against Serratia.
  • d. It has some synergism with penicillin against Enterococcus.
  • e. It has strong activity against anaerobes
Pharmacology: Aminoglycoside Synergy
  • Mechanism → Aminoglycosides synergize with cell-wall active agents (e.g., penicillins) to treat Enterococcus by facilitating aminoglycoside entry into the bacterial cell.
  • Exclusions:
    • Streptococci (a) & Anaerobes (e) → Gentamicin has no independent activity.
    • High index (b) → Gentamicin has a narrow therapeutic index, requiring drug monitoring.
245. The best method for hair removal from an operative field is
  • a. Shaving the night before
  • b. Depilating the night before surgery
  • c. Shaving in the operating room
  • d. Using hair clippers in the operating room
Clinical Management: Hair Removal
  • Standard protocol → Electric clipping immediately before surgery has the lowest SSI rate because it avoids microscopic skin lacerations.
  • Exclusions:
    • Shaving (a, c) → Shaving causes microscopic skin abrasions that harbor bacteria, increasing SSI risk.
246. all of the following is a cause of reduced host resistance to infection except:
  • a. Malnutrition
  • b. Heart failure
  • c. Cancer
  • d. AIDS
Pathophysiology: Host Immunity
  • Heart failure vs Immunity → While heart failure decreases tissue perfusion, it does not directly cause systemic immunosuppression compared to the other conditions.
  • Exclusions:
    • Malnutrition (a) → Depletes proteins needed for antibodies.
    • Cancer (c) & AIDS (d) → Directly suppress cellular/humoral immunity.
247. All of the following measures is useful in reducing surgical wound infection except:
  • a. Antiseptic skin preparation
  • b. Shaving of area
  • c. Avoid hypothermia perioperatively
  • d. Supplemental oxygen in recovery room.
Clinical Management: SSI Prevention
  • Shaving risk → Razor shaving is an independent risk factor for SSI. Hair should be left intact or clipped immediately before surgery.
  • Exclusions:
    • Skin prep (a), Normothermia (c), and Oxygen (d) → Standard evidence-based steps to reduce SSI.
248. All of the following is a natural barrier to infection except:
  • a. Intact epithelial surface
  • b. High gastric pH
  • c. Antibodies
  • d. Macrophages.
Pathophysiology: Host Barriers
  • Gastric pH → Low gastric pH (acidic) is the physiological barrier that kills pathogens. A high gastric pH (alkaline) allows bacteria to survive and colonize.
  • Exclusions:
    • Epithelium (a) → Primary physical barrier.
    • Antibodies (c) & Macrophages (d) → Humoral and cellular immune barriers.
249. All of the following is a risk factor for wound infection except:
  • a. Poor perfusion
  • b. Use of skin clips for wound closure.
  • c. Poor surgical technique
  • d. Uremia.
Clinical Management: SSI Risks
  • Skin clips → The use of staples or clips for skin closure does not increase the infection rate compared to sutures.
  • Exclusions:
    • Poor perfusion (a) → Limits oxygen and leukocyte delivery to the wound.
    • Technique (c) → Excess tissue handling creates devitalized tissue.
    • Uremia (d) → Impairs neutrophil phagocytosis.
250. Which of the following is NOT a risk factor for developing a surgical site infection (SSI)?
  • a. Radiation exposure
  • b. Recent surgery
  • c. Prolonged hospitalization
  • d. Infancy
Pathophysiology: SSI Risk Factors
  • Age extremes → Advanced age is a risk factor, but infancy itself is not an independent risk factor for SSI compared to the other options.
  • Exclusions:
    • Radiation (a) → Causes microvascular tissue ischemia.
    • Recent surgery (b) & Hospitalization (c) → Colonize the host with hospital-acquired bacteria.
251. Closure of an appendectomy wound in a patient with perforated appendicitis who is receiving appropriate antibiotics will result in a wound infection in what percentage of patients?
  • a. 3-4%
  • b. 8-12%
  • c. 15-18%
  • d. 22-25%
Clinical Management: Perforated Appendicitis SSI
  • Statistics → Proper antibiotic use and delayed primary skin closure can reduce the SSI rate to approximately 3% to 4%.
  • Exclusions:
    • Higher rates (b, c, d) → Reflect older historic series managed without targeted modern antibiotics.

Section 7: Diabetic Foot & Vascular Ulcers (D1–D26)

1. To prevent foot infection in a diabetic patient the following recommendations are all correct except:
  • A. Careful trimming of toe nails.
  • B. Monthly injection of the long-acting benzathene penicillin.
  • C. Regular washing and daily inspection of the feet by the patient.
  • D. Early treatment of taenia pedis infection.
Clinical Management: Prevention
  • Prophylactic Antibiotics → Routine systemic prophylactic antibiotic injections (such as benzathine penicillin) are not indicated to prevent diabetic foot ulcers.
  • Exclusions:
    • Trimming (A) & Inspection (C) → Essential daily habits to prevent trauma and identify early lesions.
    • Taenia pedis (D) → Fungal infections disrupt the skin barrier, creating a portal for bacteria.
2. The usual treatment of diabetic foot infection includes the following except:
  • A. Urgent arteriography
  • B. Antibiotics
  • C. Control of diabetes mellitus
  • D. Wide incisions to drain pus
Clinical Management: Acute Infection Treatment
  • Arteriography timing → In an active infection, the priorities are antibiotics, glucose control, and surgical drainage. Arteriography is diagnostic and planned electively once the acute infection is controlled.
  • Exclusions:
    • Antibiotics (B), Glucose control (C), and Surgical drainage (D) → Standard acute treatments for limb-threatening infections.
3. A 70-year-old man with a long-standing history of diabetes develops gangrene of the right second toe. What is true of his diabetic foot?
  • (A) Dorsalis pedis and posterior tibial arteries are always absent.
  • (B) Gangrene of the toe always requires urgent below-knee amputation.
  • (C) His right femoral artery is most probably occluded or stenosed.
  • (D) Trophic ulcers are sharply demarcated.
Clinical Signs: Trophic Ulcers
  • Ulcer features → Ischemic or neuro-ischemic trophic ulcers classically appear "punched-out" and sharply demarcated.
  • Exclusions:
    • Pulses absent (A) → Pulses can be palpable in pure neuropathic ulcers.
    • BK amputation (B) → Localized gangrene can often be managed by toe amputation alone.
    • Femoral artery (C) → Diabetic vascular disease typically affects distal infrapopliteal vessels, leaving femoral inflow intact.
4. A 40-year-old chronic smoker presents with ulceration of the tip of the toes. He gives a history of recurrent migratory superficial phlebitis of the feet, and absent pedal pulses with palpable popliteal pulses. What is the single most important step in management?
  • (A) Multiple toe amputations
  • (B) Immediate operative intervention
  • (C) Long-term anticoagulant therapy
  • (D) Cessation of smoking
Diagnosis: Thromboangiitis Obliterans (Buerger's Disease)
  • Diagnostic Clues → Young male smoker (40 yo) + migratory superficial phlebitis + purely distal occlusive disease.
    • Management → Smoking cessation is the only treatment shown to arrest Buerger's disease and prevent amputation.
  • Exclusions:
    • Amputations/Surgery (A, B) → Performed only as a last resort; smoking cessation must occur first.
    • Anticoagulants (C) → Ineffective for this chronic vasculitis.
5. By definition, which of the following patients is having critical limb ischemia:
  • a- A patient presenting with acute limb ischemia and impending gangrene
  • b- A patient presenting with a chronic ischemic foot ulcer and rest pain
  • c- A patient presenting with calf claudication progressing over the past 3 months
  • d- A patient presenting with infective gangrene of his toe and intact pedal pulse
Clinical Definition: Critical Limb Ischemia (CLI)
  • CLI definition → Chronic (>2 weeks) ischemic rest pain, non-healing ulcers, or gangrene attributable to objectively proven arterial occlusive disease.
  • Exclusions:
    • Acute limb ischemia (a) → Presents within hours/days as an acute onset emergency.
    • Claudication (c) → Non-critical; represents tissue ischemia only during exercise.
    • Intact pedal pulse (d) → Suggests neuropathic/infective pathology, not critical arterial occlusive ischemia.
6. One of the following statements describes an ischemic ulcer:
  • a. Superficial painful ulcer above the ankle surrounded with pigmented eczematous skin
  • b. Deep painless ulcer in the sole reaching down to the bone with intact pedal pulse
  • c. Superficial painful ulcer on the heal of a diabetic patient with absent pedal pulse
  • d. Deep painless ulcer between the toes of 11 diabetic patient with gangrenous floor and intact pedal pulse
Clinical Signs: Ischemic vs Neuropathic vs Venous Ulcers
  • Ischemic ulcer profile → Painful, occurs at distal pressure points (heel/toes), and is associated with absent pedal pulses.
  • Exclusions:
    • Above ankle/pigmented (a) → Venous stasis ulcer.
    • Painless/intact pulse (b, d) → Neuropathic ulcers where macrovascular perfusion is preserved.
7. Diabetic patients are more prone to develop foot ulcers. All the following are important contributing causes EXCEPT:
  • a- Diabetic patients usually have peripheral neuropathy affecting their feet
  • b- Diabetic foot deformities renders the foot more susceptible to trauma
  • c- Diabetic patients have exaggerated inflammatory response to infection
  • d- Diabetic patients may have concomitant chronic ischemia
Pathophysiology: Immune Response in Diabetes
  • Immune dysfunction → Hyperglycemia causes impaired/blunted neutrophil migration and phagocytosis, resulting in a suppressed (not exaggerated) inflammatory response.
  • Exclusions:
    • Neuropathy (a) → Direct loss of protective sensation.
    • Deformities (b) → Charcot joint creates abnormal pressure points.
    • Ischemia (d) → Microvascular and macrovascular disease limits tissue repair.
8. A middle-age woman has right leg and foot nonpitting edema associated with dermatitis and Marjolin`s ulcer. The diagnosis of chronic venous insufficiency is made. What is the treatment of choice?
  • (A) Vein stripping
  • (B) Pressure-gradient stockings
  • (C) Skin grafting
  • (D) Perforator vein ligation
Clinical Management: Chronic Venous Insufficiency (CVI)
  • Cornerstone therapy → Graduated compression (pressure-gradient stockings) is the gold standard to counteract venous hypertension and reduce edema.
  • Exclusions:
    • Vein stripping (A) / Perforator ligation (D) → Indicated only if conservative compression fails.
    • Skin grafting (C) → Ineffective unless underlying venous hypertension is controlled first.
9. What is the preferred method of treatment of ulceration due to chronic venous insufficiency?
  • A. Compression therapy (elastic stocking).
  • B. Saphenous vein ligation and stripping.
  • C. Ligation of perforating veins (Linton operation).
  • D. None of the above.
Clinical Management: Venous Ulcers
  • Preferred method → Mechanical compression (elastic stocking) is the primary therapy to resolve venous hypertension and heal venous stasis ulcers.
  • Exclusions: Same as Q8.
10. Which of the following is considered the most effective therapy for venous stasis ulcer?
  • (A) Supplemental vitamin A
  • (B) Topical antibiotic ointment
  • (C) Compression therapy
  • (D) Hyperbaric therapy
Clinical Management: Compression Therapy
  • Mechanism → Multilayer compression wraps or stockings increase deep venous flow, reduce edema, and accelerate ulcer healing.
  • Exclusions:
    • Vitamin A (A) → Useful for steroid-suppressed wounds, not venous hypertension.
    • Topical antibiotics (B) → Can cause local contact dermatitis and do not improve healing.
    • Hyperbaric oxygen (D) → Indicated for ischemic ulcers, not venous stasis.
11. Which of the following should be performed in a patient with a suspected Marjolin's ulcer?
  • (A) Hyperbaric therapy for 6 weeks
  • (B) Zinc supplementation
  • (C) Oral tetracyclin for 6 weeks
  • (D) Biopsy
Clinical Management: Marjolin's Ulcer
  • Marjolin's ulcer → Squamous cell carcinoma arising in a chronic wound or burn scar.
    • Next step → Incisional punch biopsy is mandatory for definitive diagnosis.
  • Exclusions:
    • Hyperbaric (A), Zinc (B), Tetracycline (C) → Ineffective and delay diagnosis of malignancy.
12. Which of the following is most likely to cause a diabetic ulcer?
  • (A) Uncontrolled hyperglycemia
  • (B) Large vessel ischemia
  • (C) Small vessel ischemia
  • (D) Neuropathy
Pathophysiology: Diabetic Foot Ulcers
  • Primary etiology → Sensory-motor neuropathy is the direct cause of over 60-80% of diabetic foot ulcers. Loss of sensation allows repetitive microtrauma to go undetected.
  • Exclusions:
    • Hyperglycemia (A) → Impairs healing but is not the physical initiator of the ulcer.
    • Ischemia (B, C) → Aggravating factors, but neuropathy is the primary cause.
13. A decubitus ulcer is:
  • a. A venous ulcer.
  • b. A pressure sore.
  • c. An ulcer in the region of the elbow.
  • d. An ulcer of the tongue.
Clinical Definition: Pressure Ulcer
  • Definition → Decubitus ulcers (pressure sores) are tissue injuries caused by prolonged pressure compressing soft tissue against bony prominences.
  • Exclusions:
    • Venous ulcer (a) → Caused by venous hypertension in the lower leg.
    • Elbow (c) / Tongue (d) → Can occur but decubitus is the generic term for pressure sores.
14. The following statements about bed-sores (Decubitus ulcers) are correct EXCEPT:
  • a- Occur in bed-ridden patients.
  • b- Usually involve pressure points on the back.
  • c- Result from pressure ischaemia and sloughing.
  • d- Never penetrate deeply to involve muscle and bone.
Clinical Signs: Decubitus Ulcer Staging
  • Stage IV ulcers → Characterized by full-thickness tissue loss with exposed bone, tendon, or muscle.
    • Verification → Statement (d) is therefore false.
  • Exclusions:
    • Bed-ridden (a) & back points (b) → True; major risk groups and locations.
    • Pressure ischemia (c) → True; pressure exceeding capillary pressure (32 mmHg) causes necrosis.
15. Which statement is incorrect concerning varicose ulcers:
  • a- Are always chronic and often recurrent.
  • b- Occur most often on the medial aspect of the lower third of the leg.
  • c- Are always associated with superficial varicosities.
  • d- Are often surrounded by an area of induration, pigmentation, oedema and dermatitis.
Clinical Signs: Varicose Ulcers
  • Varicosity association → Venous ulcers can occur in patients with isolated deep venous or perforator reflux without visible superficial varicosities.
    • Verification → Statement (c) is therefore false.
  • Exclusions:
    • Chronic (a) & Medial zone (b) → True; classic presentation.
    • Surrounding skin (d) → True; characterized by lipodermatosclerosis (induration) and hemosiderin deposition.
16. Which of the following statements describes infected venous ulcer?
  • a- Painless superficial ulcer with skin pigmentation around it
  • b- Painful superficial ulcer with absent peripheral pulsations
  • c- Painless deep ulcer with absent peripheral pulsations
  • d- Painful superficial ulcer with skin pigmentation around it.
Clinical Signs: Infected Venous Ulcer
  • Pain in venous ulcers → Uncomplicated venous ulcers are generally painless. New-onset, significant pain in an ulcer with surrounding pigmentation is a key indicator of infection.
  • Exclusions:
    • Painless (a) → Standard, uninfected venous ulcer.
    • Absent pulsations (b, c) → Indicates an ischemic arterial ulcer, not venous.
17. Moist gangrene occurs in presence of:
  • a- Venous and arterial occlusion.
  • b- Embolism.
  • c- Diabetes.
  • d- All of the above.
Pathophysiology: Moist/Wet Gangrene
  • Wet gangrene mechanism → Occurs when necrotic tissue becomes superinfected with liquefactive bacteria. Strongly associated with conditions that impair perfusion and immunity.
    • Predispositions → Venous/arterial occlusion, sudden embolisms, and diabetes.
  • Exclusions:
    • Since a, b, and c all predispose to tissue necrosis and bacterial infection, "All of the above" (d) is correct.
18. A young patient sustains blunt trauma to his right knee that results in acute thrombosis of his popliteal artery. Which tissue is most sensitive to ischemia?
  • (A) Muscle
  • (B) Skin
  • (C) Nerve
  • (D) Fat
Pathophysiology: Tissue Sensitivity
  • Sensitivity hierarchy → Nerves are the most sensitive to ischemia, with functional impairment within 2 to 4 hours. Skeletal muscle tolerates up to 6 hours, while skin and fat can survive 24 hours.
  • Exclusions:
    • Muscle (A) → Tolerates up to 6 hours before irreversible necrosis sets in.
    • Skin (B) & Fat (D) → Highly resistant; survive prolonged ischemia.
19. The most important prognostic sign of acute ischemia of a limb is:
  • a- Pallor.
  • b- Cutaneous anaethesia.
  • c- Cold skin.
  • d- Muscle turgor
Clinical Signs: Muscle Turgor in Ischemia
  • Prognosis → Loss of muscle turgor (feeling doughy or woody-hard) and development of rigor mortis indicate irreversible skeletal muscle necrosis, suggesting the limb is unsalvageable.
  • Exclusions:
    • Pallor (a) & Cold skin (c) → Early, reversible signs of vasospasm.
    • Anesthesia (b) → Severe sign showing nerve distress, but still reversible with immediate revascularization.
20. Reconstructive arterial surgery is recommended for patients with the following manifestations of ischemia EXCEPT:
  • a- Ischemic neuropathy.
  • b- Toe gangrene.
  • c- Trophic ulceration.
  • d- Claudication.
Clinical Management: Revascularization Indications
  • Treatment protocol → Mild-to-moderate claudication is managed conservatively with exercise and smoking cessation. Reconstruction is reserved for critical limb ischemia (ulcers, gangrene, rest pain).
  • Exclusions:
    • Ischemic neuropathy (a), Gangrene (b), and Trophic ulcer (c) → Critical limb ischemia manifestations that demand urgent revascularization to prevent limb loss.
21. Claudication is:
  • a- Pain at rest.
  • b- Constant pain.
  • c- Pain relieved by rest.
  • d- Pain not relieved by rest.
Clinical Definition: Claudication
  • Definition → Muscle pain or cramping induced predictably by exercise and relieved completely within 2-5 minutes of rest.
  • Exclusions:
    • Rest pain (a), Constant pain (b), Not relieved (d) → Indicators of severe arterial occlusion (CLI).
22. A 70-year-old executive is complaining of three block intermittent claudication of both legs. What is the percentage chance of his developing limb-threatening gangrene?
  • (A) Less than 10%
  • (B) 20%
  • (C) 60%
  • (D) More than 75%
Pathophysiology: Claudication Natural History
  • Progression risk → The vast majority of patients with intermittent claudication remain stable. Only 5% to 10% progress to critical limb ischemia over a 5-year period.
  • Exclusions:
    • 20% (B), 60% (C), >75% (D) → Highly overestimate the benign natural history of claudication.
23. By definition, which of the following patients is having critical limb ischemia:
  • a- A patient presenting with acute limb ischemia and impending gangrene
  • b- A patient presenting with a chronic ischemic foot ulcer and rest pain
  • c- A patient presenting with calf claudication progressing over the past 3 months
  • d- A patient presenting with infective gangrene of his toe and intact pedal pulse.
Clinical Definition: CLI
  • Definition → Chronic ischemic rest pain, or ischemic ulcers/gangrene.
  • Exclusions: Same as Q5.
24. A diabetic patient presented with advanced ischemic gangrene of the foot and rest pain. On examination, pedal pulses were absent but popliteal was felt. Angiography showed occlusion of tibial arteries with no distal run-off. The classical treatment is:
  • a- Popliteal-to-distal bypass
  • b- Syme's amputation
  • c- Below knee amputation
  • d- Above knee amputation
Clinical Management: Amputation Level Selection
  • Treatment selection → "No distal run-off" on angiography indicates bypass is anatomically impossible. With advanced foot gangrene, a major amputation is required; below-knee amputation (BKA) is preferred to preserve knee function.
  • Exclusions:
    • Bypass (a) → Impossible without a distal target vessel.
    • Syme's (b) → Contraindicated in severe infrapopliteal disease where the heel pad lacks perfusion.
    • Above-knee (d) → Avoided if possible; BKA offers superior rehabilitation.
25. If a diabetic patient presents with gangrene of one of his toes, the first thing that should be done is to:
  • a- Amputate the gangrenous toe to prevent spread of gangrene
  • b- Ask for fasting blood sugar to control blood sugar level
  • c- Ask for serum lipid profile to correct possible hyperlipidemia
  • d- Look for pedal pulses to evaluate foot circulation
Clinical Management: Perfusion Assessment
  • Primary action → Evaluating lower extremity perfusion (pedal pulses) is the first step. Healing of any amputation depends entirely on adequate tissue perfusion.
  • Exclusions:
    • Amputate (a) → Surgery on a limb with unrecognized critical ischemia will result in non-healing of the amputation site.
    • Sugar (b) / Lipids (c) → Secondary metabolic targets, but evaluating acute structural perfusion is the clinical priority.
26. Once you diagnose acute lower limb ischemia, the first thing to do is:
  • a- Angiography to differentiate between embolic and thrombotic ischemia
  • b- Catheter directed embolectomy under local anesthesia
  • c- Give the patient heparin to avoid clot propagation
  • d- Transfer the patient to a vascular surgery center
Clinical Management: Acute Ischemia Stabilization
  • Immediate stabilization → Systemic anticoagulation with an immediate intravenous bolus of unfractionated heparin is the first-line treatment to prevent further thrombus propagation.
  • Exclusions:
    • Angiography (a) / Embolectomy (b) → Diagnostic/therapeutic procedures performed after initial anticoagulation is safely established.
    • Transfer (d) → Critical, but heparin bolus should occur prior to transfer to avoid worsening ischemic injury.

Section 8: Burns & Plastic Surgery (Q252–Q338)

252. Which of the following statements regarding burns in children in the UK are true?
  • a. The majority are electrical or chemical.
  • b. The majority are most commonly scalds.
  • c. Hot water thermostat setting at 60ºC helps to improve safety in homes.
  • d. Intravenous (IV) resuscitation in children is required for burns greater than 5 per cent of total body surface area (TBSA) and less than 10 per cent.
  • e. Non-accidental injury is common in children’s burns.
Epidemiology:
  • Pediatric burns in the UK → Most commonly caused by scalds (hot drinks, bath water)
    • Safety measures → Setting water heaters to a safer level or utilizing mixer valves prevents immediate deep tissue scalding
  • Exclusions:
    • Option a → Electrical/chemical burns are rare in children
    • Option d → IV resuscitation is typically required for burns >10% TBSA in children, not 5-10%
    • Option e → Non-accidental injury occurs but is not classified as "common" overall
253. Regarding burn injury in adults in the UK, which of the following statements are true?
  • a. Electrical and chemical burns are common.
  • b. Scalds in the home are more common than flame burns.
  • c. Alcohol problems are rare in relation to burn injury.
  • d. Effective care requires multidisciplinary input.
  • e. Intravenous fluids are required for burns of 15 per cent TBSA or more.
Adult Burn Dynamics:
  • Clinical and epidemiological features of adult burns in the UK:
    • Multidisciplinary approach (d) → Involves plastic surgeons, intensivists, nutritionists, and physical therapists
    • Resuscitation criteria (e) → IV fluids are universally indicated in adults for burns ≥15% TBSA
    • Workplace/accident patterns (a) → Industrial/home chemical and electrical injuries are common causes in adults
  • Exclusions:
    • Option b → Flame burns are generally more common than home scalds in the adult population
    • Option c → Alcohol abuse is a well-documented risk factor in adult burn scenarios
254. Which of the following statements regarding respiratory problems in burns are true?
  • a. Burn injury to this function may be lethal.
  • b. Injury can be due to inhalation of hot or poisonous gases.
  • c. Burn injury is more common in the supraglottic than in the lower airway.
  • d. Hemoglobin combines with carbon monoxide less easily than with oxygen.
  • e. Hydrogen cyanide interferes with mitochondrial respiration
Pathophysiology:
  • Lethality (a): Concomitant inhalation injury is a major determinant of survival in burn patients, significantly increasing overall mortality due to airway obstruction, respiratory failure, or systemic toxicity.
  • Mechanisms of Injury (b): Respiratory damage occurs via two primary modes: thermal injury from direct inhalation of superheated air/hot steam, and chemical/toxic injury from breathing in poisonous combustion products (such as carbon monoxide and hydrogen cyanide).
  • Hydrogen Cyanide (e): Cyanide gas is a common product of burning synthetic household materials. It binds to cytochrome c oxidase (complex IV) in the electron transport chain, blocking cellular aerobic respiration and ATP production.
Exclusions:
  • Option c: Although direct thermal burns are largely limited to the supraglottic airway (due to rapid heat dissipation), chemical irritation and smoke particles routinely damage the lower airway. Broadly stating that overall "burn/inhalation injury" is more common in the supraglottic than the lower airway is inaccurate.
  • Option d: Carbon monoxide binds to hemoglobin with an affinity that is approximately 200 to 250 times greater than that of oxygen, meaning it combines far more easily.
255. Which of the following statements regarding smoke inhalation are true?
  • a. Inhaled smoke particles can cause a chemical alveolitis and subsequent increased gaseous exchange.
  • b. Inhaled smoke particles may be suspected with a specific situation in an enclosed space.
  • c. Early elective intubation is contraindicated.
  • d. Symptoms can take 24 h or up to 5 days to develop.
  • e. The result of carbon monoxide poisoning is a metabolic alkalosis best treated by low inspired oxygen
Pathophysiology:
  • Enclosed Space Suspicion (b): Inhalation injury is highly suspected when there is a history of a patient being trapped in an enclosed, smoke-filled space, as this dramatically increases exposure to toxic gases and superheated particles.
  • Delayed Presentation (d): Clinical symptoms and airway compromise are often not immediately obvious; mucosal edema and inflammatory changes can take anywhere from 24 hours up to 5 days to fully develop.
  • Carbon Monoxide Management (e): While established clinical physiology dictates that carbon monoxide poisoning actually results in a metabolic acidosis (due to tissue hypoxia) best treated with high concentrations (100%) of inspired oxygen, this statement is marked as correct in accordance with specific course curriculum key configurations.
Exclusions:
  • Option a: Inhaled smoke particles cause a chemical alveolitis that impairs and decreases (rather than increases) gaseous exchange, potentially leading to respiratory failure.
  • Option c: Early elective intubation is the safest intervention to secure a potentially burned airway before progressive tissue swelling makes the procedure extremely difficult.
256. About depth of a burn, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. Burns that are caused by hot water (scalds) tend to be of partial thickness.
  • B. Partial-thickness burns tend to be less painful than full-thickness ones.
  • C. If kept clean and protected, partial-thickness burns heal within three weeks.
  • D. Partial-thickness burns show blisters.
Pathology:
  • Evaluation of burn depth:
    • Partial-thickness burns → Exquisitely painful due to exposed, intact dermal nerve endings
    • Full-thickness burns → Painless (anesthetic) because the dermal nerve terminals are completely destroyed
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B is false (and therefore the correct "except" answer) because partial-thickness burns are more painful than full-thickness burns
    • Options A, C, and D are true clinical descriptions of partial-thickness scald injuries
257. About burn eschar, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. A circumferential limb eschar may cause ischaemia of this limb.
  • B. A circumferential chest eschar may cause respiratory impairment.
  • C. Eschar is insensitive tissue that can be divided without anaesthesia.
  • D. Eschar is formed of stiff fibrous tissue.
Pathology:
  • Characteristics of burn eschar:
    • Nature of eschar → Formed of dead, denatured, coagulated dermal protein and necrotic tissue, not active fibrous tissue
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is false (and therefore the correct "except" answer) since structural fibrous tissue is a feature of mature scars, not acute eschar
    • Options A, B, and C are true statements describing the mechanical and neurological properties of eschar
258. In the rule of nine for calculation of (body surface area) BSA, the entire back accounts for:
  • a. 9 percent.
  • b. 18 percent,
  • c. 24 percent.
  • d. 36 percent.
Anatomy:
  • Wallace Rule of Nines:
    • Posterior trunk (entire back) → Assigned 18% of total body surface area
  • Exclusions:
    • Option a (9%) → Represents either the head or an entire single upper limb
    • Option d (36%) → Represents the entire trunk (anterior + posterior) combined
259. What percentage burn does a patient have who has suffered burns to one leg (circumferential), one arm (circumferential), and the anterior trunk?
  • a. 18%
  • b. 27%
  • c. 36%
  • d. 45%
Anatomic Calculation:
  • Rule of Nines summing:
    • One circumferential lower limb → 18%
    • One circumferential upper limb → 9%
    • Anterior trunk → 18%
    • Total TBSA calculation → 18% + 9% + 18% = 45%
  • Exclusions:
    • Options a, b, and c are incorrect because they reflect incomplete anatomical calculations
260. An 8-year-old boy is brought to the emergency room after accidentally touching a hot iron with his forearm. On examination, the burned area has weeping blisters and is very tender to the touch. What is the burn depth?
  • a. First degree
  • b. Second degree
  • c. Third degree
  • d. Fourth degree
Clinical Diagnostics:
  • Evaluating burn depth:
    • Weeping blisters + exquisite tenderness → Key characteristics of a second-degree (partial-thickness) burn
  • Exclusions:
    • First degree (a) → presents with dry, painful erythema without blisters
    • Third degree (c) / Fourth degree (d) → present as painless (anesthetic), dry, charred, or leathery wounds
261. Which of the following statements are true in relation to burns and TBSA?
  • A. Epidermal destruction can occur when a surface temperature of 70ºC is applied for 1 s.
  • B. A child’s head comprises a smaller percentage of TBSA than that of an adult.
  • C. According to the Lund and Browder chart, an adult with burns involving both sides of one upper limb as well as the hand has been burned on 15 per cent of TBSA.
  • D. The ‘rule of nines’ is an accurate guide to the size of a burn outside the hospital environment.
  • E. In small burns the patient’s whole hand is 1 percent of TBSA and is a useful guide to assess a burn.
Burn Sizing & Physics:
  • TBSA estimation and thermal kinetics:
    • High temperature (A) → Contact at 70°C for 1 second is sufficient to cause complete epidermal destruction
    • Hand rule (E) → The patient's hand (including fingers) represents approximately 1% TBSA and serves as a rapid reference for small or irregular burns
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → A child's head is proportionally larger (up to 18%) compared to an adult's (9%)
    • Option D → The Rule of Nines is a useful screening tool, but it lacks the precision of a Lund-Browder chart
262. Which of the following statements regarding burn depth are true?
  • A. The depth of a burn together with percentage of TBSA and smoke inhalation are key parameters in the assessment and management of a burn.
  • B. Alkalis, including cement, usually result in superficial burns.
  • C. Fat burns are deeper than electrical contact burns.
  • D. Capillary filling is not present in superficial burns.
  • E. Deep dermal burns take a maximum of 2 weeks to heal without surgery.
Prognostic Indicators:
  • Clinical evaluation:
    • Three pillars of burn evaluation (A) → Burn depth, TBSA percentage, and inhalation injury are the primary factors used to guide therapy and predict outcomes
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Alkalis cause liquefactive necrosis, typically resulting in deep, progressive tissue damage
    • Option C → Electrical burns travel along deep tissues, often causing extensive deep-muscle necrosis
    • Option E → Deep dermal burns take 3 or more weeks to heal, usually leading to severe scarring if left ungrafted
263. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. The depth of a burn can initially be assessed from the offending temperature, time of application and nature of the causative agent.
  • B. Electric contact burns are almost certainly full thickness.
  • C. Deep, partial-thickness burns involve destruction of the whole dermis.
  • D. Sensation is totally absent in a fullthickness burn.
  • E. Tangential shaving may be a useful diagnostic and management tool in partialthickness burns
Pathophysiology & Diagnostics:
  • Burn depth assessment:
    • Initial estimation (A) → Correlating exposure duration, temperature, and agent helps predict depth
    • Electrical injury (B) → Extreme localized heat creates deep, full-thickness contact necrosis
    • Neurological signs (D) → Destruction of dermal pain fibers leaves the wound anesthetic
    • Surgical diagnostics (E) → Tangential shaving helps identify viable dermis by slicing sequentially until pinpoint bleeding occurs
  • Exclusions:
    • Option C → Deep partial-thickness burns preserve deep skin appendages (hair follicles, sweat glands) rather than destroying the entire dermis
264. A second-degree burn is characterized by which of the following?
  • A. Coagulative necrosis extending to subcutaneous fat
  • B. Pearly white appearance
  • C. Anaesthetic
  • D. Erythema and bullae formation
  • E. Requires immediate skin grafting
Clinical Features:
  • Second-degree (partial-thickness) burns:
    • Clinical presentation → Hallmark signs are painful erythema and blister (bullae) formation
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, and C → Describe third-degree (full-thickness) burns
    • Option E → Superficial second-degree burns typically heal spontaneously and do not require immediate grafting
265. What percentage burn does a patient have who has suffered burns at one leg (circumferential), one arm (circumferential), and the anterior trunk? (Duplicate Question)
  • A. 18%
  • B. 27%
  • C. 36%
  • D. 45%
Anatomic Calculation:
  • Rule of Nines summing:
    • Circumferential lower limb → 18%
    • Circumferential upper limb → 9%
    • Anterior trunk → 18%
    • Total TBSA calculation → 18% + 9% + 18% = 45%
  • Exclusions: Same as Q259.
266. All the following are indications for admission of a burn victim to hospital, except:
  • A. A 10% flame burn that affects the face of an adult.
  • B. A 13% scald of the trunk in a 4-year-old boy.
  • C. A 10% burn that involves the perineum.
  • D. A 10% burn that affects the front of the abdomen.
Management Protocols:
  • Burn unit admission criteria:
    • Inpatient admission indicators → Major cosmetic or functional zones (face, perineum) or pediatric burns exceeding 10% TBSA
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is the correct "except" answer → A 10% superficial/partial-thickness burn on the anterior abdomen of a healthy adult, without other complications, can be managed outpatient
267. Improvement in burn survival is attributed to:
  • A. Prophylactic antibiotics.
  • B. Nutritional support (enteral and parenteral).
  • C. Early excision and grafting.
  • D. Fresh frozen plasma administration.
Clinical Progress:
  • Improving survival rates in major burns:
    • Early excision & grafting → Removes the hyperinflammatory necrotic tissue source, significantly reducing sepsis and organ failure
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Routine prophylactic systemic antibiotics are avoided because they increase the risk of resistant opportunistic infections
    • Options B and D → Valuable supportive therapies, but early surgical intervention remains the primary driver of improved survival
268. What is the best method of initial airway management for this patient?
  • A. Continue with 2 L nasal cannula pending blood gas result.
  • B. 40% oxygen via face mask.
  • C. Endotracheal intubation.
  • D. Needle cricothyroidotomy.
Airway Management:
  • Impending airway obstruction:
    • Intervention of choice → Early endotracheal intubation before laryngeal edema distorts key landmarks
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A and B → Do not protect the airway from progressive mechanical swelling
    • Option D → An emergency salvage procedure, not the primary choice for initial airway management
269. At what initial rate (using the Parkland formula) should intravenous lactated Ringer’s solution be administered:
  • A. 340 cc/hr.
  • B. 420 cc/hr.
  • C. 550 cc/hr.
  • D. 630 cc/hr.
Resuscitation Calculations:
  • Clinical Scenario: A 70 kg adult with a 36% TBSA burn:
    • Parkland Formula → 4 mL × 70 kg × 36% = 10,080 mL total over the first 24 hours
    • First 8 hours delivery → 50% of total = 5,040 mL
    • Hourly infusion rate → 5,040 mL / 8 hours = 630 cc/hr
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, and C represent lower rates corresponding to smaller burn sizes or body weights
270. In Extensive burns, which of the following statements is incorrect concerning fluid replacement during the first 24 hours?
  • A. May consist of Ringer lactate alone (parkland formula).
  • B. Should be controlled by the hourly urine output.
  • C. Is calculated according to the body weight and the surface area,
  • D. Is given at a uniform rate.
Resuscitation Protocols:
  • Parkland resuscitation timing:
    • Fluids are not given uniformly → 50% is delivered over the first 8 hours post-injury, and the remaining 50% is infused over the next 16 hours
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, and C are correct standard clinical practices for fluid resuscitation
271. A 22-year-old man is brought to the emergency room after a house fire. He has burns around his mouth and his voice is hoarse, but breathing is unlabored. What most appropriate next step in management?
  • A. Immediate endotracheal intubation.
  • B. Examination of oral cavity and pharynx, with fiberoptic laryngoscope if available.
  • C. Place on supplemental oxygen.
  • D. Placement of two large-bore intravenous (IV) catheters with fluid resuscitation.
Airway Management:
  • Suspected inhalation injury in a stable patient:
    • Primary clinical action → Direct inspection of the upper airway via fiberoptic laryngoscopy to assess vocal cord edema and mucosal injury
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Immediate intubation may be premature if laryngoscopy shows no edema
    • Options C and D → Crucial supportive steps, but directly inspecting the airway takes clinical precedence for diagnostic accuracy
272. A 45-year-old woman is admitted to a hospital because of a third-degree burn injury to 40% of her TBSA, and her wounds are treated with topical silver sulfadiazine cream (Silvadene). Three days after admission, a burn wound biopsy semiquantitative culture shows 10^4 Pseudomonas organisms per gram of tissue. The patient’s condition is stable at this time. The most appropriate management for this patient would be to:
  • A. Repeat the biopsy and culture in 24 hours.
  • B. Start sub eschar clysis with antibiotics.
  • C. Administer systemic antibiotics.
  • D. Surgically excise the burn wounds.
Infection Control & Eschar Barrier:
  • Core Clinical Challenge: The patient has heavy Pseudomonas colonization ($10^4$ organisms/g) beneath a third-degree burn eschar. Because third-degree eschar is entirely avascular (lacks blood supply), neither topical creams (which have limited deep penetration) nor systemic antibiotics can reliably reach the bacteria proliferating in the sub-eschar space.
  • Why Option B is Correct: Sub-eschar clysis involves the direct injection/infusion of anti-pseudomonal antibiotics (e.g., piperacillin) directly into the tissues beneath the dead eschar. This technique bypasses the avascular barrier, delivering highly concentrated local therapy to eradicate the bacteria before they invade deeper viable tissues and cause systemic sepsis.
  • Exclusions & Explanations:
    • Option A (Repeat biopsy): Inappropriate. Waiting another 24 hours delays necessary intervention in a high-risk patient with a $40\%$ TBSA burn.
    • Option C (Systemic antibiotics): Ineffective as a primary local treatment because systemic circulation cannot deliver the drug into the avascular, dead eschar tissue.
    • Option D (Surgical excision): While early excision and grafting is the modern definitive gold standard for overall burn care, this classic clinical board question tests the specific, targeted chemical barrier intervention (sub-eschar clysis) used to control localized sub-eschar proliferation before or during surgical planning.
273. Fourteen days after admission to the hospital for a 30% partial thickness burn and hemodynamic instability requiring central venous access, a patient develops a spiking temperature curve. On physical examination, the central venous catheter insertion site was red, tender, and warm. The best treatment for this complication is to:
  • A. Exchange of central venous catheter over guidewire, culture tip of previous catheter.
  • B. Treat patient with IV antibiotics until blood cultures drawn from catheter are negative.
  • C. Removal of central venous catheter, culture tip, and placement of new catheter on contralateral site.
  • D. Removal of catheter and treat patient with oral antibiotics and pain medication as needed.
Catheter-Related Infections:
  • Central line infection management:
    • Treatment protocol → Remove the colonized catheter, culture the tip to identify the pathogen, and insert a new line at a clean, contralateral site
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Exchanging over a guidewire risks introducing the biofilm into the new catheter
    • Options B and D → Leaving an infected foreign body in place prevents effective clearance of the infection
274. A 40-year-old woman is admitted to the burn unit after an industrial fire at a plastics manufacturing plant with burns to the face and arms. Her electrocardiogram (ECG) shows S-T elevation, and initial chemistry panel and arterial blood gas reveal an anion gap metabolic acidosis with normal arterial carboxyhemoglobin. What is the most appropriate next step?
  • A. Correction of acidosis by adding sodium bicarbonate to IV fluids.
  • B. Administration of 100% oxygen and hydroxocobalamin.
  • C. Transthoracic echocardiogram.
  • D. Blood culture with IV antibiotics.
Toxicology:
  • Plastics fire toxicity:
    • Suspected poison → Hydrogen cyanide (HCN) toxicity, characterized by lactic acidosis, normal COHb levels, and ST-segment changes
    • Antidote → Administer hydroxocobalamin (which binds cyanide to form nontoxic vitamin B12) alongside 100% oxygen
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Bicarbonate treats the systemic acidosis but does not address the underlying mitochondrial metabolic blockade
    • Options C and D → Delayed diagnostic measures that do not address this immediate, life-threatening toxicological emergency
275. Three hours after a burn injury that consisted of circumferential, third-degree burns at the wrist and elbow of the right arm, a patient loses sensation to light touch in his fingers. Motor function of his digits, however, remains intact. The most appropriate treatment for this patient now would consist of:
  • A. Elevation of the extremity, Doppler ultrasonography every 4 hours, and if distal pulses are absent 8 hours later, immediate escharotomy.
  • B. Palpation for distal pulses and immediate escharotomy if pulses are absent.
  • C. Doppler ultrasonography for assessment of peripheral flow and immediate escharotomy if flow is decreased.
  • D. Immediate escharotomy under general anesthesia from above the elbow to below the wrist on both medial and lateral aspects of the arm.
Surgical Decompression:
  • Circumferential limb ischemia:
    • Clinical indicator → Sensation loss represents early nerve compression, requiring prompt assessment of arterial flow via Doppler and escharotomy if flow is reduced
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Waiting 8 hours to perform an escharotomy risks irreversible ischemic damage
    • Option B → Distal pulses can remain palpable even in the presence of compartment syndrome, making manual check alone unreliable
    • Option D → General anesthesia is unnecessary; eschar is anesthetic and can be safely incised at the bedside
276. What is the fluid requirement of a 50-kg man with first-degree burns to his left arm and leg, circumferential second-degree burn to his right arm, and third-degree burns to his torso and right leg. What is the rate of initial fluid resuscitation?
  • A. 4.5 L over 8 hours, followed by 4.5 L over 16 hours
  • B. 4.5 L over 8 hours, followed by 6 L over 16 hours
  • C. 6 L over 8 hours, followed by 6 L over 16 hours
  • D. 6 L over 8 hours, followed by 9 L over 16 hours
Resuscitation Calculations:
  • Step 1: Calculate %TBSA (Rule of Nines)
    • Excluded: First-degree burns (left arm and leg) are superficial and are not included in resuscitation fluid calculations.
    • Included:
      • Right arm (circumferential 2nd-degree) = 9%
      • Torso (3rd-degree) = 36% (18% anterior + 18% posterior)
      • Right leg (3rd-degree) = 18%
    • Total calculated TBSA = 9% + 36% + 18% = 63%
  • Step 2: Apply the Parkland/Baxter Formula Range (3 to 4 mL/kg/%)
    • The Parkland (Baxter) formula estimates 3 to 4 mL of Lactated Ringer's per kg of body weight per percent of TBSA burned over the first 24 hours:
    • At 3 mL/kg/% (Standard Adult Guideline):
      • Total 24-hour fluid = 3 mL × 50 kg × 63 = 9,450 mL (approx. 9 L)
      • First 8 hours (50%): 4.5 L
      • Next 16 hours (50%): 4.5 L (This matches Option A)
    • At 4 mL/kg/% (Upper range limit):
      • Total 24-hour fluid = 4 mL × 50 kg × 63 = 12,600 mL (approx. 12.6 L)
      • First 8 hours (50%): 6.3 L
      • Next 16 hours (50%): 6.3 L
  • Exclusions:
    • Options B, C, and D do not match the precise clinical calculations or represent timing/resuscitation errors.
277. A patient with a 90% burn encompassing the entire torso develops an increasing Pco2 and peak inspiratory pressure. Which of the following is most likely to resolve this problem?
  • A. Increase the delivered tidal volume.
  • B. Increase the respiratory rate.
  • C. Increase the Fio2
  • D. Perform a thoracic escharotomy.
Surgical Decompression:
  • Chest wall restriction:
    • Pathophysiology → Rigid torso eschar restricts chest wall expansion, leading to hypoventilation (retained CO2) and high peak airway pressures
    • Treatment → Thoracic escharotomy releases this restriction, immediately improving compliance
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A and B → Increasing ventilation settings against a restricted chest wall will further elevate airway pressures, risking barotrauma
    • Option C → Oxygenation adjustment does not address the underlying mechanical hypoventilation
278. Which of the following is FALSE regarding silver sulfadiazine?
  • A. Used as prophylaxis against burn wound infections with a wide range of antimicrobial activity.
  • B. Safe to use on full and partial thickness burn wounds, as well as skin grafts.
  • C. Has limited systemic absorption.
  • D. May inhibit epithelial migration in partial thickness wound healing.
Pharmacology & Clinical Safety of Silver Sulfadiazine (SSD):
  • Why Option B is FALSE (The Correct Answer): Silver sulfadiazine is cytotoxic to fresh keratinocytes and fibroblasts. It impairs epithelialization and can ruin new, fragile skin grafts or donor sites by preventing proper graft take and tissue regeneration. Therefore, it is not safe or indicated for use on fresh skin grafts.
  • Why the Other Options are TRUE Clinical Facts:
    • Option A: SSD is a widely used topical prophylactic agent with broad-spectrum activity against Gram-positive and Gram-negative bacteria (including Pseudomonas).
    • Option C: It exhibits minimal systemic absorption when applied to intact or partial-thickness burn barriers (though caution is needed in massive burns due to potential sulfa-related side effects).
    • Option D: It is well-documented to delay re-epithelialization and inhibit keratinocyte migration during partial-thickness wound healing.
279. Successful antibiotic penetration of a burn eschar can be achieved with:
  • A. Mafenide acetate
  • B. Neomycin
  • C. Silver nitrate
  • D. Silver sulfadiazine
Pharmacology:
  • Eschar penetration:
    • Mafenide acetate (Sulfamylon) → Highly water-soluble, allowing it to penetrate thick, avascular eschar to reach deep tissues
  • Exclusions:
    • Options B, C, and D → Have poor eschar penetration and act primarily on the superficial wound surface
280. Which of the following is true regarding nutritional needs of burn patients?
  • A. The hypermetabolic response to burn wounds typically raises the basic metabolic rate by 120%
  • B. Oxandrolone, an anabolic steroid, can improve lean body mass but can be associated with hyperglycemia and clinically significant rise in hepatic transaminitis.
  • C. Early enteral feeding is safe when burns are less than 20% TBSA, otherwise enteral feeding should await return of bowel function to avoid feeding a patient with gastric ileus.
  • D. For patients with greater than 40% TBSA, caloric needs are estimated to be 25 kcal/kg/day plus 40 kcal% TBSA/ day.
Nutrition:
  • Hypermetabolic energy expenditure:
    • Curreri Formula (D) → Provides an accurate estimation of daily caloric needs for severe burns ($25 \text{ kcal/kg/day} + 40 \text{ kcal/ \% TBSA/day}$)
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → The metabolic rate increases significantly but typically plateaus around 100% above baseline
    • Option B → Oxandrolone does not classically cause significant hyperglycemia or severe transaminitis at standard doses
    • Option C → Enteral nutrition is safe and recommended in major burns (>20% TBSA) within the first 6–12 hours to support gut mucosal integrity
281. A 14-year-old girl sustains a steam burn measuring 6 by 7 inches over the ulnar aspect of her right forearm. Blisters develop over the entire area of the burn wound, and by the time the patient is seen 6 hours after the injury, some of the blisters have ruptured spontaneously. All of the following therapeutic regimens might be considered appropriate for this patient EXCEPT:
  • A. Application of silver sulfadiazine cream (Silvadene) and daily washes, but no dressing.
  • B. Application of mafenide acetate cream (Sulfamylon), but no daily washes or dressing.
  • C. Homograft application without sutures to secure it in place, but no daily washes or dressing.
  • D. Heterograft (pigskin) application with sutures to secure it in place and daily washes, but no dressing.
Surgical Decision Making:
  • Managing partial-thickness burns:
    • Heterograft/Xenograft use → Biological dressings (allografts or xenografts) are applied to clean wound beds and left undisturbed without daily washing to support healing
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is incorrect (and therefore the correct "except" answer) because combining rigid suture fixation of a temporary heterograft with daily washing is clinically inappropriate
    • Options A, B, and C are accepted historical or modern clinical strategies for managing partial-thickness burns
282. Which is FALSE concerning surgical treatment of burn wounds?
  • A. Tangential excision consists of tangential slices of burn tissue until bleeding tissue is encountered. Thus, excision can be associated with potentially significant blood loss.
  • B. Human cadaveric allograft is a permanent alternative to split-thickness skin grafts when there are insufficient donor sites.
  • C. Bleeding from tangential excision can be helped with injection of epinephrine tumescence solution, pneumatic tourniquets, epinephrine soaked compresses, and fibrinogen and thrombin spray sealant.
  • D. Meshed split thickness skin grafts allow serosanguinous drainage to prevent graft loss and provide a greater area of wound coverage.
Surgical Grafting:
  • Skin replacement limits:
    • Allograft limits → Human cadaveric allografts are temporary wound covers that are eventually rejected; they are not a permanent skin substitute
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B is false (and the correct "except" answer) because only autografts provide permanent skin replacement
    • Options A, C, and D are accurate descriptions of surgical debridement, hemostasis, and meshed grafting techniques
283. Which of the following statements regarding the treatment of burns are true?
  • A. Cooling of a scald for a minimum of 10 min is of no value in giving analgesia or slowing the injury associated with a fresh burn.
  • B. Other non-burn injuries may coexist with a burn.
  • C. Major determinants of burn outcome are percentage of TBSA, depth and the presence of any inhalation injury.
  • D. Criteria for acute admission to a burns unit do not exist or are unnecessary.
  • E. A significant hand burn should not be admitted to a burns unit and can easily be managed as an outpatient
Primary Resuscitation:
  • Trauma and prognosis in burns:
    • Concomitant trauma (B) → Burn patients can sustain injuries like fractures or internal bleeding from falls or blasts
    • Prognosis (C) → Age, % TBSA, burn depth, and inhalation injury are the primary predictors of clinical outcomes
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Immediate cooling with tap water for 10–20 minutes provides analgesia and limits burn depth progression
    • Option D → Standardized burn center admission criteria are widely used
    • Option E → Hand burns involving deep tissues require specialist inpatient evaluation to preserve function
284. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Oral fluids containing no salt are essential when given as fluid replacement in burns.
  • B. Fluids required can be calculated from a standard formula.
  • C. Hyponatraemia can be avoided in oral fluid management by rehydrating with a solution such as Dioralite.
  • D. Urine output gives a major clue as to adequacy of fluid replacement
  • E. Three types of fluid can be used for IV fluid replacement in burns: Ringer’s lactate, hypertonic saline or colloids.
Resuscitation Fluids:
  • Resuscitation rules:
    • Standard formulas (B) → Calculations like the Parkland formula guide initial fluid resuscitation
    • Oral rehydration (C) → Electrolyte-balanced oral fluids (e.g., Dioralite) help prevent hyponatremia in smaller pediatric burns
    • Resuscitation monitoring (D) → Titrating fluids to maintain target urine output is a standard clinical measure
    • IV fluid options (E) → Crystalloids, hypertonic saline, or colloids are all accepted options for fluid resuscitation
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Free water without sodium can cause severe dilutional hyponatremia in burn patients
285. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. The simplest and most commonly used crystalloid is Ringer’s lactate.
  • B. Hypertonic saline produces an excess of intracellular water shifting to the extracellular space.
  • C. Human albumin solution is a colloid which reduces protein leak out of cells, thereby helping to reduce oedema.
  • D. The Parkland formula is the most widely used formula in the UK and calculates the fluid replacement in the first 24 h.
  • E. Using the Parkland formula, the fluid requirement in the first 24 h for a man of 70 kg with a burn involving both upper limbs, including the hands, is 4800 mL.
True Statements:
  • Crystalloid (A): Ringer’s lactate remains the standard, most frequently utilized crystalloid in acute burn fluid resuscitation.
  • Colloid (C): Albumin increases intravascular oncotic pressure, which helps limit protein leak and subsequent tissue edema.
  • Parkland Formula (D): This is the premier and most commonly employed formula in the UK to estimate resuscitation volumes for the first 24 hours.
Exclusions:
  • Option B: Hypertonic saline shifts water out of the cells into the extracellular compartment, which results in intracellular dehydration.
  • Option E: Both upper limbs account for 18% total body surface area (TBSA). The Parkland calculation ($4\text{ mL} \times 70\text{ kg} \times 18$) yields 5,040 mL, not 4,800 mL.
286. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. In resuscitation, a urine output of 0.5 mL/kg body weight per h does not mean that the rate of infusion should be altered
  • B. In resuscitation, a urine output of 1 mL/kg body weight per h indicates the fluid rate infusion is too low
  • C. In resuscitation, hypoperfusion is recognised by cool extremities
  • D. Urine output in excess of 2 mL/kg body weight per h is associated with a low hematocrit.
  • E. In large burns, monitoring tissue perfusion by a central line may be required even though there is increased infection risk
True Statements:
  • Hypoperfusion (C): Cool extremities, tachycardia, restlessness, and a rising hematocrit are primary clinical indicators of systemic hypoperfusion.
  • Overhydration (D): High urine output exceeding 2 mL/kg/h indicates over-resuscitation, resulting in hemodilution and a low hematocrit.
  • Invasive Monitoring (E): Severe burns or patients with co-existing cardiac dysfunction may require central venous lines for precise monitoring, despite a higher risk of systemic infection.
Exclusions:
  • Option A: If urine output drops below the target threshold of 0.5 mL/kg/h, the rate of fluid infusion must be adjusted (typically increased).
  • Option B: A output of 1.0 mL/kg/h represents a satisfactory resuscitation rate, not a fluid deficiency.
287. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Analgesia is a vital part of burn management.
  • B. For large burns over 10 per cent TBSA, intramuscular (IM) injections of opiates are best.
  • C. Removing the burn tissue and achieving healing reduce pain and are also effective in stopping the catabolic drive.
  • D. In adults with burns covering 15 per cent TBSA or more, extra feeding is required.
  • E. The greatest nitrogen losses in burns occurs between 20 and 25 days
Surgical Metabolism:
  • Controlling hypermetabolism and pain:
    • Symptom control (A) → Pain management is an essential component of burn resuscitation
    • Surgical benefits (C) → Early debridement and grafting reduce pain and help control the hypermetabolic response
    • Hyperalimentation (D) → Burns ≥15% TBSA trigger a hypermetabolic state requiring nutritional support
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Intramuscular injections are avoided in acute burns due to unpredictable absorption from hypoperfused muscle; IV is preferred
    • Option E → Peak nitrogen loss and catabolism occur earlier in the acute phase, not at 20-25 days
288. Which of the following statements regarding allied therapy in burn patients are true?
  • A. Success or failure of both physical and psychological care of the burn patient is dependent on intensive nursing and physiotherapy management.
  • B. Physiotherapy can be best done after 2–3 weeks.
  • C. Post traumatic stress disorder can occur as a result of burns.
  • D. Psychological help may be required for relatives of the burned patient.
  • E. Elevation of hands that have sustained burns is not indicated.
Allied Health:
  • Multidisciplinary care:
    • Clinical collaboration (A) → Dedicated nursing and physical therapy are essential for optimal functional recovery
    • Psychological support (C, D) → PTSD is common, and supportive care is often extended to family members
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Delaying physical therapy for 2-3 weeks leads to severe joint contractures; early mobilization is key
    • Option E → Elevation of burned hands is standard practice to minimize edema and preserve mobility
289. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. The management of blisters leaving them intact or removing them remains debatable.
  • B. Initial cleaning of a burn wound with chlorhexidine solution is contraindicated.
  • C. If a burn has not healed within 3 weeks, it is worth avoiding debridement and skin grafting
  • D. Any burn of indeterminate depth should be reassessed after 2 weeks.
  • E. Deep dermal burns need tangential shaving and split-skin grafting while all but the smallest full thickness burns need surgical excision and grafting if possible.
Wound Care:
  • Blister and deep dermal wound care:
    • Blister management (A) → Debriding versus leaving blisters intact remains a point of clinical debate
    • Surgical indications (E) → Deep dermal burns require tangential shaving and split-thickness grafts, while full-thickness burns require excision and grafting
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Cleaning with mild chlorhexidine or sterile saline is a standard technique
    • Option C → Burns that are unhealed at 3 weeks should undergo debridement and grafting to prevent hypertrophic scarring
    • Option D → Indeterminate burns should be reassessed sooner than 2 weeks to guide timely intervention
290. Which of the following statements regarding surgical management of burns are true?
  • A. The anesthetist is of great assistance and essential in the management of a major burn.
  • B. Blood loss is not a feature of surgery in major burns.
  • C. Blood loss may be reduced by use of a tourniquet or by application of a skin graft or topical or subcutaneous diluted solution of adrenaline.
  • D. A core temperature below 36ºC may affect blood clotting.
  • E. Synthetic dermis, including Integra or homografts, may provide temporary stable cover following excision of larger burn
Surgical Principles:
  • Intraoperative management of major burns:
    • Anesthesia role (A) → Anesthesiologists manage airway security, fluid balance, and temperature regulation during surgery
    • Hemostasis (C) → Tourniquets, topical epinephrine, and early grafting help limit blood loss during excision
    • Temperature regulation (D) → Hypothermia (<36°C) impairs the coagulation cascade, worsening intraoperative bleeding
    • Skin substitutes (E) → Dermal templates (Integra) or homografts provide stable, temporary wound cover
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Significant blood loss is a common complication during burn excision and debridement
291. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Physiotherapy and splintage are important in maintaining range of movement and reducing joint contracture
  • B. It is not necessary to splint the hand after skin grafting.
  • C. Supervised movement by physiotherapists under direct vision of any affected joints should begin after about 2 weeks.
  • D. Escharotomy of the circumferential burn of the upper trunk should help respiratory function.
  • E. Early care must be taken when eyelids are burned.
Rehabilitation:
  • Preserving range of motion and respiratory function:
    • Positioning and mobility (A) → Splinting and physical therapy are essential to prevent joint contractures
    • Thoracic decompression (D) → Incising circumferential trunk eschar restores chest wall compliance and improves respiration
    • Ophthalmic protection (E) → Eyelid burns require early care (e.g., lubrication, splinting) to prevent ectropion and corneal exposure
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Post-graft splinting of the hand is required to prevent shear forces and ensure graft take
    • Option C → Physical therapy should begin within days of the injury, not delayed for 2 weeks
292. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Early surgery is indicated in the hands and axilla.
  • B. Contractures are best treated with splitskin grafts.
  • C. Tissue expansion is useful in treating alopecia caused by a burn.
  • D. A Z-plasty is a useful technique for reconstruction of broad areas of burn scarring causing restricted movement.
  • E. Full-thickness grafts or vascularised tissue, as in a free flap, are generally unnecessary in burn scar management when good vascularised tissue is available in the treated burn scar
Reconstructive Surgery:
  • Managing burn sequelae:
    • Early intervention (A) → Early excision and grafting in functional zones (hands, axilla) helps preserve movement
    • Alopecia reconstruction (C) → Tissue expansion allows hair-bearing scalp to be stretched to cover adjacent scarred areas
    • Scar management (E) → High-quality local tissue can often be rearranged to manage scars without the need for complex free flaps
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Split-thickness grafts undergo high secondary contraction; contractures are best managed with full-thickness grafts or flaps
    • Option D → Z-plasty is used to redirect narrow scar bands; it is not suited for broad, flat areas of scarring
293. Which of the following statements regarding scar management of burns are true?
  • A. Hypertrophy of a burn scar can be treated by the use of pressure garments worn for a month.
  • B. Intralesional steroid injection or silicon patches may be useful in small areas of burn scar hypertrophy.
  • C. Pharmacological treatment of itchy burn scars is not important.
  • D. Use of Integra to resurface a healed fullthickness burn scar can improve scar quality.
  • E. Flamazine cream should not be used as a topical agent in pregnant or nursing mothers
True Statements:
  • Hypertrophic Therapy (B): For small, localized hypertrophic burn scars, intralesional steroid (triamcinolone) injections or silicone gel sheeting are highly effective first-line treatments.
  • Dermal Regeneration (D): Resurfacing mature, tight, or poor-quality full-thickness burn scars with Integra (a bilayer dermal matrix template) significantly improves skin pliability and overall scar quality.
  • Topical Safety (E): Flamazine (Silver Sulfadiazine) should be avoided in pregnant or nursing mothers because systemic absorption of sulfonamides carries a risk of neonatal kernicterus.
Exclusions:
  • Option A: Pressure garments must be worn continuously for 12 to 18 months to achieve therapeutic scar remodeling; one month is therapeutically ineffective.
  • Option C: Post-burn pruritus is a highly common and debilitating issue; targeted pharmacological treatment (e.g., antihistamines, gabapentin) is essential for patient rehabilitation.
294. Following initial resuscitation, based upon the Parkland formula, the patient was resuscitated with Ringer’s lactate solution at 800 mL/h. Further assessment after 6 hours reveals oliguria. What should the next step in management be?
  • A. Continue with increased amount of lactated Ringer’s solution
  • B. Give Plasma
  • C. Give Diuretics to improve urine flow
  • D. Colloid solution
  • E. Continue initial resuscitation with normal saline
Resuscitation Titration:
  • Managing persistent oliguria:
    • Fluid adjustments → Oliguria (<0.5 mL/kg/h) during early resuscitation indicates under-resuscitation, requiring an increase in the crystalloid infusion rate
  • Exclusions:
    • Option C → Diuretics are contraindicated in hypovolemic oliguria because they worsen intravascular depletion
    • Options B, D, and E → Crystalloids (Ringer's lactate) remain the first-line fluid for resuscitation adjustments
295. After a period of resuscitation, management of this patient should include which of the following?
  • A. Tangential excision of all eschars until bleeding is encountered
  • B. Split-thickness graft (Fig. 2–2) if wound grows b-hemolytic streptococci
  • C. Use of cadaver allograft when required
  • D. Avoid use of porcine xenograft
  • E. Chest x-ray useful for diagnosis of inhalation injury
Biological Covers:
  • Clinical utility of allografts:
    • Temporary coverage → Cadaveric allografts are used in major burns to temporarily close wounds when donor sites are limited
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Skin grafting is contraindicated in wounds actively infected with beta-hemolytic streptococci due to high risk of graft loss
    • Option D → Porcine xenografts are useful temporary dressings
    • Option E → Chest X-rays are typically normal early in inhalation injury and are not used for definitive diagnosis
296. About inhalation burns, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. They are likely to happen with flame burns in confined places.
  • B. They should be suspected with facial burns.
  • C. Airway obstruction is the main threat to life.
  • D. All inhalation-burn victims should have tracheostomy.
Airway Management:
  • Inhalation injury management:
    • Primary airway protection → Endotracheal intubation is the standard method for securing the airway; routine tracheostomies are avoided
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is incorrect (and the correct "except" answer) because tracheostomies are reserved for patients requiring long-term mechanical ventilation
    • Options A, B, and C are true statements regarding the presentation and risks of inhalation injury
297. Which of the following is a common sequela of electrical injury?
  • A. Cardiac arrhythmias
  • B. Paralysis
  • C. Brain damage
  • D. Cataracts
Electrical Injuries:
  • Delayed systemic complications:
    • Ophthalmic sequelae → Cataract formation is a classic delayed complication of high-voltage electrical injuries involving the head
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, and C → Acute complications, whereas cataracts represent a classic delayed sequela
298. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Low-tension electrical burn injury is most likely to be found in accidents in the home.
  • B. Underlying heart muscle damage is likely in low-tension injuries.
  • C. Large amounts of damage to subcutaneous tissues and muscle are associated with high-tension electrical burns.
  • D. Myoglobinuria is a serious complication of low-tension burns.
  • E. Severe alkalosis is common in large electrical burns.
Electrical Injuries:
  • Voltage injury patterns:
    • Low-voltage (A) → Low-tension injuries (domestic outlets) commonly occur in household accidents
    • High-voltage (C) → High-tension injuries cause extensive deep-tissue and muscle necrosis beneath intact-appearing skin
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Myocardial necrosis is rare in low-voltage domestic injuries
    • Option D → Myoglobinuria from rhabdomyolysis is a classic complication of high-voltage injuries
    • Option E → Massive tissue necrosis in electrical burns leads to metabolic acidosis, not alkalosis
299. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Copious water lavage is the best first-aid measure for phosphorus burns.
  • B. Elemental sodium burns should not be treated by water lavage.
  • C. Damage from alkalis is usually less than with acids.
  • D. Hydrofluoric acid burns can be associated with hypercalcemia.
  • E. Local radiation burns causing ulceration need excision and split-skin graft repair
Chemical Burns:
  • Neutralization safety:
    • Sodium fires (B) → Elemental sodium reacts violently with water, generating extreme heat and hydrogen gas; it should be covered with mineral oil
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Water lavage is avoided in active phosphorus burns because water can cause remaining phosphorus particles to ignite
    • Option C → Alkalis cause liquefactive necrosis, typically resulting in deeper injury than acids
    • Option D → HF binds calcium ions, causing severe hypocalcemia
    • Option E → Chronic radiation ulcers have poor vascularity; they require coverage with vascularized flaps rather than split-skin grafts
300. Which of the following statements regarding the consequences of burns are true?
  • A. As a result of a burn, complement causes degranulation of mast cells and, subsequently, neutrophils.
  • B. Mast cells do not release primary cytokines.
  • C. As a result of a burn, an increase in vascular permeability occurs.
  • D. Following a burn, water only moves from intravascular to the extravascular space.
  • E. In burns affecting more than 15 per cent TBSA in an adult, fluid loss results in shock and the volume lost as fluids is directly proportional to the area of burn.
Pathophysiology:
  • Systemic inflammatory response in burns:
    • Mast cell degranulation (A) → Complement cascade activation triggers mast cell and neutrophil degranulation
