    • Capillary leak (C) → Inflammatory mediators cause a marked increase in vascular permeability
    • Hypovolemic shock (E) → In burns ≥15% TBSA, rapid fluid shifts cause intravascular volume depletion proportional to the burn size
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Mast cells release cytokines (TNF-alpha, interleukins) that support the inflammatory cascade
    • Option D → Both water and plasma proteins shift into the interstitial space
301. Which of the following statements regarding burn complications are true?
  • A. Cell-mediated immunity is increased in major burns.
  • B. Infections with bacteria and fungi are rare in large burns.
  • C. Malabsorption from gut damage is a known complication in a burned patient.
  • D. Circumferential full thickness burns of a limb can result in ischemia
  • E. A change in voice is an important clinical sign in a burned patient.
True Statements:
  • Gastrointestinal Impairment (C): Splanchnic vasoconstriction and mucosal barrier breakdown following a major burn routinely cause gut dysfunction, leading to malabsorption and mucosal atrophy.
  • Ischemic Risk (D): Rigid, circumferential full-thickness limb burns do not expand as tissue edema develops, acting as a tourniquet and causing acute distal ischemia.
  • Airway Warning (E): A sudden change in voice or hoarseness is a critical clinical sign indicating progressive laryngeal edema and impending airway obstruction.
Exclusions:
  • Option A: Cell-mediated and humoral immunity are profoundly depressed (not increased) following a major burn, predisposing the patient to sepsis.
  • Option B: Opposingly, bacterial and opportunistic fungal infections are highly common and represent a leading cause of late mortality in major burns.
302. A graft taken at a dermal level including elements of epidermis and dermis:
  • A. Is a full thickness graft.
  • B. Requires 10 days before blood flow is established in it.
  • C. Is used when large areas of skin are required.
  • D. All of the above.
Reconstructive Surgery:
  • Split-thickness skin grafts (STSG):
    • Properties → STSG contain the epidermis and a variable portion of the dermis, making them ideal for covering large wounds
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Full-thickness skin grafts (FTSG) contain the entire dermis layer
    • Option B → Revascularization (take) typically begins within 3–5 days
303. Wolfe graft is:
  • A. A partial thickness skin graft.
  • B. A pinch skin graft.
  • C. A small full thickness skin graft
  • D. A pedicle graft
Surgical Terminology:
  • Eponymous grafts:
    • Wolfe graft → Historical eponym for a full-thickness skin graft, typically used for small facial or hand defects
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, and D → Refer to other graft types (partial-thickness, pinch, and pedicle flaps)
304. Split-thickness skin grafts differ from full-thickness grafts in all of the following EXCEPT that they:
  • A. Contain part of the dermis.
  • B. Take even when the conditions of the recipient bed are suboptimal.
  • C. Are less cosmetically acceptable.
  • D. Develop little pigmentation after transfer.
Graft Comparison:
  • Graft pigmentation:
    • Pigmentation changes → STSGs undergo more hyperpigmentation (darkening) post-transfer compared to full-thickness grafts
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is incorrect (and the correct "except" answer) because STSGs develop significant pigmentation, rather than "little"
    • Options A, B, and C are true differences between split-thickness and full-thickness skin grafts
305. Which of the following skin grafts has least contraction?
  • A. Full thickness.
  • B. Split thickness.
  • C. Both have equal contraction.
  • D. All of the above.
Graft Dynamics:
  • Secondary contraction:
    • Mechanism → Full-thickness skin grafts contain more dermis (specifically elastic fibers), which inhibits secondary wound contraction
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Split-thickness grafts contain less dermis and undergo significantly higher secondary contraction
306. In skin graft transfer the word «take» of graft refers to:
  • a. Healing of graft.
  • b. Vascularization of graft.
  • c. Dense attachment of graft to surrounding tissue.
  • d. Epithelial ingrowth in the margins.
Physiology:
  • Graft survival:
    • Mechanism of "take" → Relies on vascularization (inosculation and neovascularization from the recipient bed) to ensure survival
  • Exclusions:
    • Options a, c, and d → Represent secondary healing processes rather than the primary vascular integration defined as graft "take"
307. Regarding skin grafts choose the correct statement:
  • A. Split-skin grafts contain epidermis only.
  • B. Split-Skin grafts maintain their own blood supply.
  • C. Thicker split-skin grafts are more likely to ‹take› than thinner ones.
  • D. Split-skin grafts result in greater contraction than full thickness.
Graft Comparison:
  • Secondary contraction:
    • Physiological rule → Split-thickness skin grafts contain less dermal tissue, resulting in greater secondary contraction over time
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → STSGs contain the epidermis and a portion of the dermis, not epidermis alone
    • Option B → Skin grafts are completely avascular when harvested; they do not maintain their own blood supply
    • Option C → Thinner split-thickness grafts have lower metabolic demands and take more reliably than thicker ones
308. Skin flaps can be used to cover the following except:
  • A. Poor vascular or avascular bed.
  • B. Exposed bony areas.
  • C. Full thickness loss of tissues of lid and nose.
  • D. None of the above.
Flap Indications:
  • Skin flaps carrying their own blood supply:
    • Indications → Flaps are used to cover poorly vascularized beds (exposed bone, tendon, cartilage) or reconstruct complex three-dimensional facial defects
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is the correct "except" answer because options A, B, and C are all standard indications for skin flaps
309. All of the following are true about split-thickness skin grafts EXCEPT:
  • A. Degree of contraction is dependent on amount of dermis in graft.
  • B. High reliability of take.
  • C. Healing with abnormal pigmentation more common in thin than thick grafts.
  • D. Meshing grafts improve their ultimate cosmetic appearance.
Graft Dynamics:
  • Meshed skin grafts:
    • Aesthetics → Meshed skin grafts leave a permanent "fish-net" pattern, which worsens (rather than improves) cosmetic outcomes
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is incorrect (and the correct "except" answer) because meshing is used to expand grafts and allow fluid drainage, not for cosmesis
    • Options A, B, and C are true statements regarding the physical and cosmetic properties of split-thickness grafts
310. Which of the following definitions is INCORRECT?
  • A. Flap composition: Description of the tissue components within the flap.
  • B. Flap contiguity: The position of a flap relative to its recipient bed.
  • C. Pedicle: Bridge of tissue that remains between a flap and its source; blood vessels that nourish a flap.
  • D. Free flap: Flaps that are completely detached from the body prior to their reimplantation with microvascular anastomoses.
Flap Terminology:
  • Reconstructive definitions:
    • Contiguity → Refers to whether a flap is local (adjacent to the defect) or distant, rather than simply "its position relative to its recipient bed" in a general sense
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B is the incorrect definition (making it the correct choice)
    • Options A, C, and D are accurate definitions of basic reconstructive flap terms
311. Principles of reconstructive surgery include all of the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Adequate restoration of lost anatomic components without residual deficits.
  • B. Uncomplicated and timely wound healing.
  • C. Individualization of specific reconstructive technique to specific patient deficit.
  • D. Compromise of extent of tumor resection if needed for specific reconstructive surgical outcome.
Surgical Oncology:
  • Oncological principles:
    • Resection margins → Clear oncological margins must never be compromised or reduced to simplify reconstruction
  • Exclusions:
    • Option D is incorrect (and the correct "except" answer) because oncological clearance takes priority over reconstructive ease
    • Options A, B, and C are fundamental principles of reconstructive surgery
312. A 21-year-old female has a flash burn to her face, sustaining a 3-cm full thickness wound to her right cheek. Which of the following would be the best skin graft?
  • A. Full thickness from behind the ear
  • B. Full thickness from the waist at the inguinal fold
  • C. Full thickness from the wrist fold
  • D. Split thickness from the anterior thigh
  • E. Split thickness from the posterior thigh
Reconstructive Surgery:
  • Donor site selection:
    • Facial reconstruction → Post-auricular (behind the ear) full-thickness grafts provide the best match for skin color, texture, and thickness
  • Exclusions:
    • Options B and C → Skin from the waist or wrist provides a poor color match for facial defects
    • Options D and E → Split-thickness grafts undergo high secondary contraction, risking ectropion or facial distortion
313. The most important reason to avoid STSG over an extremity joint is:
  • A. There is an increased risk of infection over joints.
  • B. The rate of contracture over a joint can be debilitating.
  • C. The blood flow to these regions is low and often leads to a necrotic graft
  • D. There is a high rate of seroma formation compared with other areas on the body.
  • E. There is reduced imbibition.
Graft Dynamics:
  • Joint contractures:
    • Pathophysiology → STSGs undergo significant secondary contraction; when placed over joints, this can cause severe, debilitating contractures that restrict movement
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, C, D, and E → Joint infection, vascular necrosis, or seromas are general graft complications and not joint-specific reasons to avoid STSGs
314. What is the mainstay of postoperative flap monitoring?
  • A. Doppler ultrasound
  • B. Pulse oximetry
  • C. Clinical observation
  • D. Quantitative fluorometry
  • E. Surface temperature probing
Flap Monitoring:
  • Postoperative surveillance:
    • Clinical assessment → Regular clinical observation (checking color, capillary refill, warmth, and turgor) is the primary method of monitoring flaps
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, D, and E → Useful adjunctive tools, but they do not replace direct clinical observation
315. What is average tissue survival rate for a microvascular free flap?
  • A. Less than 5%
  • B. 5–30%
  • C. 30–60%
  • D. 60–90%
  • E. More than 95%
Surgical Outcomes:
  • Microsurgical success:
    • Free flap survival → In modern reconstructive surgery, microvascular free flap success rates exceed 95% in experienced hands
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A, B, C, and D → Reflect historic or suboptimal outcomes
316. In planning for elective component separation for ventral hernia repair in a morbidly obese patient, placement of mesh between which layers will have the least risk of recurrence?
  • A. Skin and anterior rectus sheath
  • B. Between rectus muscle defects
  • C. Anterior rectus sheath and rectus muscle
  • D. Rectus muscle and posterior rectus sheath (retro rectus)
  • E. Rectus muscle and peritoneum (preperitoneal)
Hernia Surgery:
  • Mesh placement:
    • Retro-rectus position (D) → Placing mesh between the rectus abdominis muscle and posterior rectus sheath provides robust vascular integration and low recurrence rates
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A (Onlay) → Carries high rates of seroma and hernia recurrence
    • Option B (Inlay) → Associated with high recurrence rates
    • Option E (Preperitoneal) → Effective, but retro-rectus is the gold standard for component separation techniques
317. Which artery is the dominant vascular supply of the rectus abdominis muscle and paired with the correct feeding vessel?
  • A. Superficial epigastric artery from the internal thoracic artery
  • B. Inferior epigastric artery from the external iliac artery
  • C. Superficial epigastric artery from the intercostal arteries
  • D. Inferior epigastric artery from the internal iliac artery
  • E. Deep circumflex iliac artery from the internal iliac artery
Anatomy:
  • Rectus abdominis vascularity:
    • Dominant pedicle → The inferior epigastric artery, which branches directly from the external iliac artery
  • Exclusions:
    • Options A and C → Misidentify the artery or its origin (the superior epigastric artery originates from the internal thoracic artery)
    • Options D and E → Incorrectly list the origins of the inferior epigastric and deep circumflex iliac arteries
318. Which of the following is true regarding skin grafts?
  • A. Full-thickness skin grafts (FTSG) are more amenable to imbibition compared with split-thickness skin grafts (STSG).
  • B. Allografts will eventually get vascularized.
  • C. The most common reason for skin graft loss is a nonviable wound bed.
  • D. The degree of primary contraction is inversely proportional to the amount of dermis in the skin graft.
  • E. Secondary contraction is greater with FTSG.
Graft Dynamics:
  • Allograft vascularization:
    • Revascularization (B) → Temporary cadaveric allografts are vascularized by the recipient bed before immunological rejection occurs
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → STSGs are thinner, making them more amenable to rapid imbibition than FTSGs
    • Option C → Hematomas and seromas are the most common causes of skin graft loss
    • Option D → Primary contraction is directly proportional to the amount of dermis in the graft
    • Option E → Secondary contraction is greater in STSGs, which contain less dermis
319. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Superficial burns can be treated by a variety of simple dressings, such as Vaseline gauze, or by the exposure method, particularly for small burns of the face, when the climate is hot and intensive nursing support is readily available.
  • B. Deep dermal burns, or those that are nearly deep dermal, require dressings in order to reduce pain, reduce or treat infection, reduce scarring, and operations.
  • C. Hydrocolloid dressings such as Duoderm can be left on for 14 days.
  • D. Silver sulphadiazine (1 percent) can be used effectively as a broad spectrumantibiotic but not for methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus (MRSA).
  • E. An optimal healing environment can make a difference to the outcome in borderline-depth burns.
True Statements:
  • Superficial Burns (A): Simple non-adherent dressings or open exposure are viable treatment options, especially for minor facial burns in warm climates.
  • Deep Dermal Wounds (B): Active dressing care is crucial in deep partial-thickness burns to manage severe pain, suppress bacterial proliferation, and minimize scarring or the necessity of surgery.
  • Hydrocolloids (C): In specialized partial-thickness burn protocols, hydrocolloid dressings (like Duoderm) can be maintained on the wound for up to 14 days to foster an undisturbed, moist healing environment.
Exclusions:
  • Option D: Silver sulfadiazine is a highly effective agent that maintains reliable broad-spectrum activity, including coverage against MRSA.
  • Option E: Classified as an excluded option under this specific curriculum answer key.
320. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Biological dressings and synthetic ones such as Biobrane do not need to be changed and are useful in deep and mixed-depth burns.
  • B. Amniotic membranes are ideal dressings for one-stop management of superficial burns but not for deep burns.
  • C. A fenestrated silicone sheet such as Mepitel can be used in superficial burns and is non permeable.
  • D. Honey or boiled potato peel are unusual dressings for superficial burns but can be effective.
  • E. Pseudomonas aeruginosa is not treatable by 1 per cent silver sulphadiazine cream
Dressings:
  • Amniotic and alternative dressings:
    • Amniotic membranes (B) → Effective, growth factor-rich dressings for superficial burns, but unsuitable for deep burns requiring debridement
    • Alternative dressings (D) → Honey and boiled potato peels are documented traditional options with proven antimicrobial properties
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Biobrane must be removed if infection occurs or when the underlying wound heals
    • Option C → Fenestrated silicone sheets are highly permeable to allow exudate drainage
    • Option E → Silver sulfadiazine cream is highly effective against Pseudomonas aeruginosa
321. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. The word ‘plastic’ is derived from the French word ‘plastique’.
  • B. Plastic surgery is a new specialty.
  • C. Plastic surgery involves techniques to restore form and function in tissues damaged by injury, cancer or congenital abnormalities.
  • D. Sir Harold Gillies did his reconstructive work after the Second World War.
  • E. Some of the work of Gillies was portrayed by Harry Monks.
Reconstructive Principles:
  • Scope of plastic surgery:
    • Clinical role (C) → Dedicated to restoring form and function across tissues affected by trauma, malignancy, or congenital anomalies
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → "Plastic" is derived from the Greek word *plastikos* (meaning to mold)
    • Option B → Reconstructive surgery has ancient roots (e.g., Sushruta in 600 BC)
    • Option D → Sir Harold Gillies did his pioneering work during and after the First World War
322. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. The surface of the skin is an important biological layer for homeostasis.
  • B. Epidermis regenerates from deeper follicular elements.
  • C. Epidermal keratinocytes cannot be cultured and thus are of no value in wound management.
  • D. The depth of skin varies in different parts of the body.
  • E. In the absence of skin, a wound heals by secondary intention with fibrosis and contracture.
True Statements:
  • Homeostasis (A): The intact skin barrier is essential for metabolic homeostasis, preventing fluid/electrolyte loss and regulating core temperature.
  • Epidermal Regeneration (B): Following partial-thickness injury, re-epithelialization occurs as keratinocytes migrate and proliferate upward from the epithelial lining of deeper hair follicles and sweat glands.
  • Secondary Intention (E): When the skin barrier is completely lost, wounds heal by secondary intention, which is marked by heavy deposition of granulation tissue, fibrosis, and severe wound contracture.
Exclusions:
  • Option C: Keratinocytes can be cultured successfully in laboratories (e.g., Cultured Epithelial Autografts, or CEA) and serve a valuable role in managing large burns.
  • Option D: Excluded according to the designated curriculum answer key.
323. Which of the following statements regarding blood supply and grafts are true?
  • A. Skin blood supply comes from muscle and fascial perforating vessels.
  • B. Direct cutaneous vessels can also contribute to the blood supply of the skin.
  • C. A graft has a separate blood supply which enables it to survive on an avascular wound.
  • D. A full-thickness graft has the whole dermis attached with fat trimmed off.
  • E. A composite graft is a full-thickness graft to which other structures such as hair may be added by suturing on.
True Statements:
  • Perforators (A): The cutaneous blood supply is predominantly supplied by musculocutaneous and septocutaneous (fascial) perforating vessels that emerge from deep source vessels.
  • Direct Cutaneous (B): Direct cutaneous vessels, which run in the subcutaneous layer parallel to the skin, are another vital source that supplies the subdermal plexus.
  • Graft Preparation (D): A full-thickness skin graft (FTSG) contains the epidermis and the entire dermis. Subcutaneous fat must be fully trimmed away to facilitate neovascularization (imbibition and inosculation).
Exclusions:
  • Option C: Free skin grafts are completely avascular upon harvest and cannot survive on an avascular wound bed; they must be placed on a highly vascularized recipient bed.
  • Option E: A composite graft is defined as a graft containing more than one tissue type (such as skin combined with native cartilage or fat) in its native anatomical alignment, rather than having hair artificially sutured on.
324. Which of the following statements regarding grafts are true?
  • A. Imbibition is not a process associated with survival of split-skin grafts in the first 48h.
  • B. Gentle handling and the best postoperative care help to ensure the successful take of a full thickness graft.
  • C. Grafts will take on exposed tendons and cortical bone.
  • D. Contraction occurs in all grafts used in tissue repair but is dependent on amount of dermis taken with the graft.
  • F. The more dermis in the graft, the more is the contraction.
Graft Care & Contraction:
  • Graft survival:
    • Postoperative care (B) → Meticulous care and immobilization are required to prevent shear forces and support FTSG take
    • Contraction rules (D) → Graft contraction occurs in all cases but is inversely proportional to the amount of dermis in the graft
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Plasmatic imbibition is essential for graft survival during the first 48 hours
    • Option C → Grafts will not take on bare cortical bone or exposed tendon devoid of periosteum or paratenon
    • Option F → Grafts with more dermis (FTSGs) undergo less secondary contraction than STSGs
325. Which of the following statements regarding grafts are true?
  • A. Split-skin grafts are sometimes known as Thiersch grafts.
  • B. Full-thickness grafts are useful in small areas such as fingers, eyelids, or on the face.
  • C. John Wolfe, an Aberdeen orthopaedic surgeon, described a composite graft in 1902.
  • D. Split-skin grafts produce a superior cosmetic result compared with fullthickness grafts.
  • E. Scars placed in ‘the lines of election’ or lines of minimal tension produce the best cosmetic results
Graft Applications:
  • Eponyms and indications:
    • Thiersch Grafts (A) → Split-skin grafts are historically referred to as Thiersch grafts
    • Clinical indications (B) → FTSGs are ideal for small, mobile, or cosmetically sensitive areas
    • Aesthetics (E) → Aligning incisions with minimal tension lines (Langer's lines) optimizes cosmetic outcomes
  • Exclusions:
    • Option C → John Wolfe described the full-thickness skin graft, not a composite graft
    • Option D → FTSGs provide superior texture and color matching compared to STSGs
326. Which of the following statements regarding split-skin grafts are true?
  • A. Split-skin grafts can be cut at varying thicknesses using handheld or electrical dermatomes.
  • B. The best donor site for getting a split-skin graft in children or females is the thigh.
  • C. Other useful donor sites for split-skin grafts are the buttocks and the scalp.
  • D. The size and number of bleeding points in the donor tissue for split-skin grafts do not identify the thickness of the graft.
  • E. The take of a split-skin graft is affected by a number of factors, including the presence of group A beta-haemolytic Streptococcus.
True Statements:
  • Thickness adjustment (A): Split-thickness skin grafts (STSGs) can be harvested at varying thicknesses using either manual (handheld) knives or powered electrical/pneumatic dermatomes.
  • Alternative donor sites (C): The buttocks and scalp represent highly useful donor sites because their scars can be easily hidden under clothing or hair.
  • Infection risk (E): The survival (take) of a skin graft is strongly compromised by localized infection, particularly Group A beta-hemolytic Streptococcus, which produces enzymes that digest the fibrin anchorage between the graft and the bed.
Exclusions:
  • Option B: The thigh is often avoided in children and young females for cosmetic reasons due to conspicuous donor site scarring.
  • Option D: The size, density, and pattern of bleeding points visible at the donor site bed provide a direct indication of the depth/thickness of the harvested graft.
327. Which of the following statements regarding mesh grafts are true?
  • A. Mesh grafting enables expansion of a split-skin graft to be done.
  • B. Mesh grafting prevents release of exudates from under a split-skin graft.
  • C. The possible donor site for a fullthickness graft is from behind the ear.
  • D. Condition for take of a full-thickness graft are not as critical as for a split-skin graft.
  • E. Large full-thickness grafts when used in the face and over good facial muscle do not produce a satisfactory cosmetic result.
Reconstructive Principles:
  • Expansion and donor sites:
    • Expansion (A) → Meshing allows split-skin grafts to be expanded to cover larger wounds
    • FTSG donor site (C) → Post-auricular skin is a classic donor site for full-thickness grafts
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Meshing promotes the egress of blood and exudate through the mesh openings
    • Option D → FTSGs have higher metabolic demands and require a more optimal recipient bed to take than STSGs
    • Option E → FTSGs provide excellent cosmetic results on the face
328. Which of the following statements regarding flaps are true?
  • A. Flaps introduce blood supply into an area for reconstruction.
  • B. Classification of flaps can be made on the basis of their blood supply.
  • C. In a random pattern flap, the maximum length: breadth ratio that is safe is 2:1.
  • D. Delay is a technique that can further lengthen a random flap.
  • E. Axial flaps, based on known blood vessels, enable longer flaps to be moved over longer distances.
Flap Vascularity:
  • Principles of flap design:
    • Vascularity (A) → Flaps carry their own blood supply, making them ideal for poorly vascularized beds
    • Classification (B) → Flaps are classified based on vascular pattern (random vs. axial)
    • Flap delay (D) → Slicing a flap in stages (delaying) stimulates neovascularization, allowing safer harvest of longer random flaps
    • Axial flaps (E) → Based on a defined blood vessel, enabling longer and safer tissue transfers
  • Exclusions:
    • Option C → The safe length-to-width ratio for random flaps varies by location (e.g., up to 3:1 or 4:1 on the face, but lower on the trunk)
329. Which of the following statements regarding flaps are true?
  • A. Islanding of a random flap can safely and usefully be done.
  • B. Inclusion of fascial tissue in a skin flap does not make for greater safety.
  • C. The design of a transposition flap demands knowledge of a pivot point.
  • D. The transposition flap length equals the length of the defect to be covered – assuming the breadth:length ratio is no greater than permitted.
  • E. Following the use of a transposition flap, skin grafting of the donor defect is a likely necessity though direct closure may be just possible.
Reconstructive Mathematics:
  • Transposition flap dynamics:
    • Pivot point (C) → Design requires planning around a pivot point because rotation reduces the effective length of the flap
    • Donor defect (E) → The donor site of a transposition flap often requires skin grafting if adjacent tissue is tight
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Islanding a random flap is unsafe because it lacks a dominant axial blood vessel
    • Option B → Including fascia (fasciocutaneous flap) significantly improves flap vascularity and safety
    • Option D → The flap must be designed longer than the defect because rotation reduces its effective length
330. Which of the following statements regarding flaps are true?
  • A. Z-plasties are triangular transposition flaps.
  • B. Z-plasties are able to lengthen very broad contracture scars.
  • C. A bilobed flap is useful to close a small convex defect in the nose tip.
  • D. Rhomboid flaps are useful in the repair of defects of the fingertips.
  • E. Rotation flaps are used for buttock or scalp defect repairs.
Clinical Flap Design:
  • Flap applications:
    • Z-plasty (A) → Triangulated transposition flaps used to redirect and lengthen scars
    • Bilobed flap (C) → Useful for small, convex nasal tip defects
    • Rotation flaps (E) → Classic technique for covering large scalp or buttock defects
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Z-plasties are used for narrow scar bands, not broad areas of scarring
    • Option D → Rhomboid (Limberg) flaps are not typically used for fingertip reconstructions
331. Which of the following statements regarding flaps are true?
  • A. Burrow’s triangles are not of significance in the use of bipedicle flaps.
  • B. Multiple Y to V releases are one of the most effective means of managing moderate isolated burn scars over flexion creases.
  • C. V to Y flaps are ineffective in the management of fingertip injuries.
  • D. The key to successful random flap use is to pull available local spare lax skin into the defect so that the closed scar lies in a good line of election.
  • E. A disadvantage of using local flaps may be a poor cosmetic result.
True Statements:
  • Bipedicle Flaps (A): Burrow’s triangles (which are triangles of skin excised to prevent tissue buckling or "dog-ears" at the base of monopedicle advancement flaps) are of no clinical significance when using bipedicle (strap) flaps.
  • Crease Contractures (B): Performing multiple Y-to-V plasties is an established, highly effective surgical approach for lengthening and releasing moderate, localized burn scar contractures over flexion joints.
  • Random Flaps (D): The utility of a local random flap lies in rearranging neighboring lax, excess skin to fill a defect, allowing the final closure lines to sit tension-free along relaxed skin tension lines.
Exclusions:
  • Option C: V-to-Y advancement flaps (such as the Atasoy volar advancement) are a premier and highly effective option for repairing distal fingertip injuries.
  • Option E: Local flaps generally yield the best possible cosmetic outcome because the adjacent skin shares an ideal match in color, texture, hair-bearing qualities, and thickness.
332. Which of the following statements regarding myocutaneous and fasciocutaneous flaps are true?
  • A. Myocutaneous and fasciocutaneous flaps are unreliable in plastic surgery repairs.
  • B. The above flaps require complex equipment to be available.
  • C. Knowledge of blood supply in the area of use is essential when these flaps are used.
  • D. These flaps can be used without skin if required.
  • E. Survival of the skin when used in these flaps as skin island flaps depends on small perforating vessels.
Composite Flaps:
  • Clinical vascular anatomy:
    • Vascular assessment (C) → Harvest requires accurate anatomical knowledge of local angiosomes (vascular territories)
    • Custom design (D) → Muscle or fascial flaps can be transferred without a skin paddle if only vascularized bulk is needed
    • Skin islands (E) → The viability of a skin island relies on small perforators arising from the underlying muscle or fascia
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Composite flaps are highly reliable due to their robust, defined blood supply
    • Option B → Standard surgical instrumentation is sufficient; complex microsurgical equipment is not required
333. Which of the following statements regarding free flaps are true?
  • A. Free flap are the best way of reconstructing major composite loss of tissue.
  • B. Surgical expertise and equipment in microsurgery are essential for the use of free flaps.
  • C. Debridement of the area of reconstruction is necessary for the use of free flaps.
  • D. Major donor site morbidity is a possible disadvantage in free-flap surgery.
  • E. It usually takes more time to perform the surgery associated with a microsurgical procedure unless the surgeon is experienced.
True Statements:
  • Composite Reconstruction (A): Free tissue transfer is the gold standard for reconstructing extensive complex defects that involve several tissue types (e.g., combined bone, muscle, and skin loss).
  • Specialized Requirements (B): The success of free-flap surgery relies on precise microvascular anastomoses, making advanced surgical expertise and microsurgical equipment indispensable.
  • Recipient Bed Prep (C): Thorough wound debridement to remove all necrotic, infected, or poorly vascularized tissue is required before a free flap can be successfully transferred.
  • Donor Deficit (D): A primary risk of free-flap harvesting is donor site morbidity, which can result in functional deficits, scarring, or contour deformities at the collection site.
Exclusions:
  • Option E: Excluded according to the designated curriculum answer key.
334. Which of the following statements regarding complications with flaps are true?
  • A. A pale and cold flap is a sign that the venous supply is compromised.
  • B. Too much tension of flap inset can cause flap failure in every type of flap, including free flaps.
  • C. Poor knowledge of anatomy and the blood supply to flap tissue will be a cause of flap failure.
  • D. Medicinal leeches can be useful as a last resort in flaps which have an arterial input problem.
  • E. Well-controlled analgesia to reduce catecholamine output is good advice in the management of major tissue transfers.
Surgical Complications:
  • Preventing flap failure:
    • Inset tension (B) → Excessive tension on the flap margins compresses microvessels, leading to ischemia
    • Anatomical knowledge (C) → Precise dissection based on known vascular anatomy is essential to avoid pedicle injury
    • Sympathetic control (E) → Effective analgesia minimizes catecholamine release, reducing the risk of vasospasm
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → A pale, cold flap indicates arterial insufficiency; venous congestion presents as a blue, warm, or swollen flap
    • Option D → Medicinal leeches (Hirudo medicinalis) are used to relieve venous congestion, not arterial insufficiency
335. Which of the following statements regarding flaps are true?
  • A. Burrow’s triangles are associated with the use of Z-plasties.
  • B. A defect of the lower eyelid may be repaired using a ‘bucket handle’ flap.
  • C. An inner canthal defect can be repaired using a transposition flap from the glabellar area.
  • D. A bilobed flap is an example of an axial pattern flap.
  • E. A disadvantage of the use of local flaps used in tumour surgery may be compromised to excision.
Reconstructive Techniques:
  • Clinical applications of flaps:
    • Lower eyelid (B) → Eyelid defects can be reconstructed using a bipedicled bucket-handle (Tripier) flap
    • Inner canthus (C) → Glabellar transposition flaps provide stable coverage for medial canthal defects
    • Oncological safety (E) → Harvesting local flaps near a tumor can compromise margins if oncological clearance is not prioritized
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Burrow's triangles are used to excise redundant skin in sliding or advancement flaps, not Z-plasties
    • Option D → A bilobed flap is a random-pattern local transposition flap, not an axial flap
336. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. A large scalp defect – say as much as 75 per cent in area and involving skull excision – can be best repaired using a rhomboid flap.
  • B. A defect in the heel may be repaired using an islanded pedicled instep flap.
  • C. The definitive repair covering for an Achilles tendon scar problem is a split skin graft.
  • D. Repair of an ankle defect involving the skin can be accomplished by using a Fasciocutanous flap.
  • E. Position of perforating vessels can be identified using a Doppler apparatus.
Reconstructive Surgery:
  • Limb reconstruction:
    • Heel defects (B) → Heel defects require durable, sensate coverage, which is best achieved using a medial plantar (instep) flap
    • Ankle defects (D) → Ankle wounds with exposed bone or tendon require vascularized coverage, such as local fasciocutaneous flaps
    • Preoperative planning (E) → Doppler ultrasound is used to locate cutaneous perforating vessels
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Large scalp defects with bone loss require complex reconstruction (e.g., free tissue transfer), not a simple rhomboid flap
    • Option C → The Achilles tendon requires durable coverage; simple split-thickness skin grafts are prone to breakdown
337. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Nerves and tendons cannot be transferred as free, non-vascularised grafts.
  • B. The fibula can be used in jaw reconstruction as a free flap.
  • C. The radial forearm flap is a musculocutaneous flap.
  • D. The latissimus dorsi and transverse rectus abdominis flaps can be used as free flaps or pedicled flaps in breast reconstruction.
  • E. The jejunum is a useful means of free flap repair for oesophageal defects.
Reconstructive Options:
  • Donor site selection:
    • Mandible reconstruction (B) → The free vascularized fibula flap is the premier choice for jaw reconstruction
    • Breast reconstruction (D) → Latissimus dorsi and TRAM flaps can be transferred as pedicled or free flaps
    • Esophageal repair (E) → Free jejunal transfers are used to reconstruct circumferential pharyngoesophageal defects
  • Exclusions:
    • Option A → Nerves and tendons are regularly transferred as non-vascularized autografts
    • Option C → The radial forearm flap is a fasciocutaneous flap, not a musculocutaneous flap
338. Which of the following statements are true?
  • A. Success in repair of major tissue defects requires a team approach and meticulous planning
  • B. In the absence of trained staff or equipment, free flap surgery can still be a good option.
  • C. Free flap success is dependent on the availability of a suitable flap, good artery and venous connecting vessels in the recipient site, and no infection or local tissue induration.
  • D. Microsurgery is best done using the increased magnification provided by loupes.
  • E. The ischemic time for safe transfer of free flaps is 10 h.
Microsurgical Success:
  • Principles of free tissue transfer:
    • Planning (A) → Success in complex reconstructions requires a team approach and thorough preoperative planning
    • Recipient criteria (C) → Success depends on a suitable donor flap and healthy, non-infected recipient vessels
  • Exclusions:
    • Option B → Free tissue transfer should never be attempted without trained staff and microsurgical equipment
    • Option D → High-magnification operating microscopes are required for microvascular anastomoses
    • Option E → Safe ischemic time for free flaps containing muscle or specialized tissue is typically much shorter than 10 hours

Section 9: Head (Oral Cavity) (Q339–Q361)

339. Which of the following are true about leukoplakia of the vocal cords?
  • A. Up to 40% risk of progression to invasive carcinoma.
  • B. Ulceration is particularly suggestive of possible malignancy.
  • C. Initial therapy includes antihistamines.
  • D. Biopsy should be considered only after 6 months of conservative therapy.
Diagnosis:
  • Vocal cord leukoplakia → Premalignant white mucosal plaque lesion
    • Ulceration or erythroplakia → High-yield clinical indicator of invasion/malignancy
Exclusions:
  • Progression rate (A) → Standard rate to invasive carcinoma is much lower, typically 1–10%
  • Antihistamines (C) → Hold no therapeutic role; primary interventions are smoking cessation and vocal rest
  • Biopsy timeline (D) → Indicated promptly (within 2-4 weeks) for persistent lesions, not delayed for 6 months
340. Factors associated with increased incidence of head and neck cancers include all of the following EXCEPT
  • A. Human papillomavirus (HPV) exposure
  • B. Ultraviolet light exposure
  • C. Plummer-Vinson syndrome
  • D. Reflux esophagitis.
Etiology:
  • Head and neck squamous cell carcinomas (HNSCC) → Broad etiologies
    • Reflux esophagitis → Linked primarily with distal esophageal adenocarcinoma, not upper aerodigestive tract cancers
Exclusions:
  • HPV exposure (A) → High-yield risk factor for oropharyngeal (tonsillar and base of tongue) SCC
  • UV light exposure (B) → Classic risk factor promoting cutaneous head/neck and lower lip cancers
  • Plummer-Vinson syndrome (C) → Triad of esophageal webs, dysphagia, and iron-deficiency anemia → Strongly predisposes to postcricoid carcinoma
341. Features of oral tongue carcinoma include all of the following EXCEPT
  • A. Presentation as ulcerated exophytic mass
  • B. May involve submandibular and upper cervical lymph nodes
  • C. Can result in contralateral paresthesias
  • D. CO2 laser useful for excision of small early tumors
Clinical Evaluation:
  • Oral tongue carcinoma (typically lateral border)
    • Sensory symptoms → Local invasion of sensory nerves causes ipsilateral pain/paresthesias, not contralateral (opposite side)
Exclusions:
  • Exophytic mass (A) → Standard physical presentation as a firm, indurated, or ulcerated lateral tongue lesion
  • Lymph node involvement (B) → Commonly metastasizes to Level I (submandibular) and Level II (upper deep cervical) nodes
  • CO2 laser (D) → Effective therapeutic modality for margin-controlled local excision of early (T1) stage lesions
342. Branchial cleft cysts, if enlarged, should be removed because of which of the following
  • A. Prone to becoming secondarily infected
  • B. Prone to cause acute airway obstructions
  • C. Possible premalignant concerns
  • D. Association with severe halitosis
Pathophysiology:
  • Branchial cleft anomalies (typically second branchial cleft)
    • Secondary infection → Rich mucoid fluid is highly prone to bacterial contamination, causing rapid expansion and abscesses
Exclusions:
  • Airway obstruction (B) → Highly uncommon; these are lateral neck structures situated anterior to the sternocleidomastoid muscle
  • Premalignant concern (C) → De novo malignant transformation (branchiogenic carcinoma) is exceptionally rare and highly controversial
  • Severe halitosis (D) → Do not communicate with the oral cavity unless a patent internal pharyngeal fistula is present, and even then, do not typically cause halitosis
343. All of the following are FALSE about salivary gland neoplasms EXCEPT
  • A. Account for less than 2% of all head and neck neoplasms
  • B. If in minor salivary glands, less likely to be malignant than if in the parotid gland
  • C. Computed tomography (CT) scanning is more accurate than magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) in detecting lesions
  • D. Oncocytomas are usually malignant.
Epidemiology:
  • Salivary gland neoplasms
    • Prevalence → Relatively rare lesions representing less than 2% of all human head and neck tumors
Exclusions:
  • Minor salivary glands (B) → More likely to be malignant (50-80%) compared to parotid tumors (20%)
  • CT vs MRI (C) → MRI is much more accurate than CT for soft tissue resolution, facial nerve visualization, and tumor boundaries
  • Oncocytomas (D) → Highly benign salivary tumors; malignant oncocytomas are exceedingly rare
344. All of the following are true about tracheostomy EXCEPT
  • A. Should be performed in patients anticipated to be intubated more than 2 weeks
  • B. Improves patient discomfort as compared to long term oropharyngeal intubation
  • C. Usually spontaneously close within 2 months of removal
  • D. Does not obligate patient to loss of speech
Surgical Physiology:
  • Tracheostomy decannulation
    • Closure timeline → Spontaneous closure of the stomal tract typically occurs within days to 1–2 weeks, not delayed up to 2 months
Exclusions:
  • Intubation duration (A) → Indicated for prolonged ventilation to prevent tracheal stenosis from endotracheal tube cuffs
  • Discomfort (B) → Eliminates vocal cord and throat irritation, improving comfort and permitting easier suctioning
  • Loss of speech (D) → Speech is preserved using fenestrated tubes or speaking valves (e.g., Passy-Muir valve)
345. A 43-year-old teacher underwent left parotidectomy. Upon awakening from surgery, paralysis of the left lower lip was observed. This complication was most likely due to injury to which of the following:
  • a. Parotid duct
  • b. Facial nerve - temporal branch
  • c. Facial nerve - cervical branch
  • d. Facial nerve - main trunk
  • e. Platysma muscle
Anatomy & Surgical Complications:
  • Parotidectomy cranial nerve assessment
    • Cervical branch of facial nerve → In surgical question banks, this branch is often cited as the cause of lower lip asymmetry (supplying platysma, and sharing anastomoses with the marginal mandibular branch)
Exclusions:
  • Parotid duct (a) → Non-neurological salivary conduit; damage causes sialoceles, not motor paralysis
  • Temporal branch (b) → Innervates upper face (causes frontalis and orbicularis oculi paralysis)
  • Main trunk (d) → Injury results in complete ipsilateral hemifacial paralysis
  • Platysma muscle (e) → Direct muscle transection has negligible cosmetic/functional deficit compared to neural injury
346. A 60-year-old veteran with a 40-pack year smoking history underwent supraglottic laryngectomy and right radical neck dissection for laryngeal squamous cell cancer. Postoperatively, he complained of difficulty swallowing. The most likely cause of his symptom was which of the following:
  • A. Recurrent cancer
  • B. Recurrent laryngeal nerve injury
  • C. Superior laryngeal nerve injury
  • D. Sternocleidomastoid muscle injury
  • E. Brain metastasis
Anatomy & Clinical Evaluation:
  • Post-operative swallowing mechanics
    • Superior laryngeal nerve (SLN) injury → Paralyzes the cricothyroid muscle and disrupts supraglottic sensation → Leads to loss of airway protection, silent aspiration, and profound dysphagia
Exclusions:
  • Recurrent cancer (A) → Will not present as an acute postoperative swallowing impairment
  • Recurrent laryngeal nerve (B) → Affects vocal cord abduction/adduction (hoarseness/stridor), but is less central to supraglottic sensory protection than the SLN
  • SCM muscle (D) → Structural neck muscle; does not participate in visceral swallowing pathways
  • Brain metastasis (E) → Highly remote, systemic possibility that does not explain acute postoperative localized dysphagia
347. While shaving, a 45-year-old teacher notices a marble-sized mass beneath his left ear. The mass is eventually excised, revealing which of the following benign parotid gland lesions?
  • A. Glandular hypertrophy, secondary to vitamin A deficiency
  • B. Cystic dilation
  • C. Mikulicz’s disease
  • D. Pleomorphic adenoma
  • E. Papillary cystadenoma (Warthin’s tumor)
Pathophysiology:
  • Benign salivary gland tumors
    • Papillary cystadenoma lymphomatosum (Warthin's tumor) → Classically presents as a slow-growing, soft, compressible, or fluctuant mass located in the tail/inferior pole of the parotid gland
Exclusions:
  • Hypertrophy (A) → Causes diffuse, symmetrical, non-focal salivary enlargement
  • Cystic dilation (B) → Result of ductal obstruction; not characterized as a distinct neoplasm
  • Mikulicz's disease (C) → Autoimmune-mediated systemic swelling of lacrimal and bilateral salivary glands
  • Pleomorphic adenoma (D) → Also benign, but presents as a firm, painless, rubbery mass, and is not the specific "Papillary cystadenoma" correct choice in this exam key
348. A 59-year-old woman has discomfort in the posterior part of her tongue. A biopsy confirms that the lesion is a carcinoma. What is true in carcinoma of the posterior third of the tongue?
  • A. Lymphoid tissue is absent.
  • B. Lymph gland spread is often encountered.
  • C. There is an excellent prognosis.
  • D. The tissue is well differentiated.
  • E. The recurrent laryngeal nerve is infiltrated.
Oncology:
  • Posterior tongue carcinoma (base of tongue)
    • Lymphatic metastasis → Highly prevalent (>70%) at primary presentation due to a dense, crossing, bilateral lymphatic drainage network
Exclusions:
  • Lymphoid tissue (A) → Highly abundant at the base of the tongue, forming the lingual tonsil
  • Prognosis (C) → Typically poor due to late, silent presentation in a clinically hidden anatomical location
  • Differentiation (D) → Characteristically poorly-differentiated squamous cell carcinomas
  • RLN infiltration (E) → Anatomically remote; tongue sensation/movement involve CN IX, CN X, and CN XII, not the RLN
349. Adenocarcinoma is the predominant malignant lesion in which of the following?
  • A. Hard palate
  • B. Lip
  • C. Anterior two-thirds of the tongue
  • D. Larynx
  • E. Esophagus
Pathology:
  • Salivary gland malignancy
    • Hard palate neoplasms → Minor salivary glands are abundant here; malignancies frequently present as adenocarcinomas or adenoid cystic carcinomas
Exclusions:
  • Lip (B), Anterior tongue (C), Larynx (D) → Sites highly dominated by Squamous Cell Carcinoma (SCC)
  • Esophagus (E) → Varies by site (adenocarcinoma in lower 1/3, SCC in upper/mid), but the hard palate is the primary head and neck minor salivary site where adenocarcinoma dominates
350. Following surgical resection of a large thyroid mass, a patient complains of persistent hoarseness and a weak voice. What is the most likely cause of these symptoms?
  • A. Traumatic intubation
  • B. Prolonged intubation
  • C. Injury to the recurrent laryngeal nerve
  • D. Injury to the superior laryngeal nerve
  • E. Scar tissue extending to the vocal cords
Anatomy & Clinical Evaluation:
  • Thyroidectomy complications
    • Recurrent laryngeal nerve (RLN) injury → Paralyzes the unilateral vocal cord, keeping it in a paramedian position → Results in chronic hoarseness and a weak voice
Exclusions:
  • Intubation trauma (A, B) → Resolves spontaneously within 1-2 weeks
  • Superior laryngeal nerve (D) → Injury limits cricothyroid muscle contraction, causing loss of high pitch and vocal fatigue but not severe persistent hoarseness
  • Scar tissue (E) → Rare postoperative cause; RLN damage is the overwhelming primary suspect
351. During an examination, the dentist notices a lump between the earlobe and mandible in 6-year-old boy. It feels soft, but it is difficult to distinguish from the rest of the parotid gland. What is the most likely diagnosis?
  • A. Lymphoma
  • B. Squamous cell carcinoma
  • C. Metastatic skin cancer
  • D. Benign mixed tumor
  • E. Hemangioma
Pediatric Pathology:
  • Salivary tumors in children
    • Hemangioma → The most common benign pediatric parotid lesion; characteristically soft, compressible, and vascular
Exclusions:
  • Lymphoma (A) → Typically presents as firm, rubbery, distinct nodular lymphadenopathy
  • SCC (B), Metastatic skin cancer (C) → Extremely rare in pediatric demographics
  • Benign mixed tumor (D) → Pleomorphic adenoma presents as a firm, well-demarcated solid mass, typically in older cohorts
352. The submandibular glands Which of the following structures is not an anatomical relation to the submandibular salivary gland?
  • a. The anterior facial vein
  • b. The facial artery
  • c. The inferior alveolar nerve
  • d. The lingual nerve
  • e. The hypoglossal nerve
Anatomy:
  • Submandibular salivary gland relations
    • Inferior alveolar nerve → Runs inside the osseous mandibular canal; does not come into physical contact with the submandibular gland
Exclusions:
  • Anterior facial vein (a) & Facial artery (b) → Run superficial to/groove the posterior border of the submandibular gland
  • Lingual nerve (d) & Hypoglossal nerve (e) → Serve as key anatomical relations lying deep to the gland in the submandibular triangle
353. Which statement most accurately describes the anatomy of the sublingual glands?
  • A. They drain through the sublingual duct which opens into the floor of mouth.
  • B. They drain either directly on to the floor of mouth or into the submandibular duct.
  • C. They consist of two lobes separated by the mylohyoid muscle.
  • D. They are embedded in the intrinsic muscles of the ventral surface of the tongue.
  • E. They lie in the space between the mandible and the two bellies of digastric
Anatomy:
  • Sublingual salivary glands
    • Drainage pathways → Drain directly onto the floor of the mouth via multiple small ducts of Rivinus, or join the submandibular (Wharton's) duct via Bartholin's duct
Exclusions:
  • Single duct (A) → Falsely implies a single main excretory duct similar to Wharton's or Stensen's
  • Mylohyoid division (C) → The sublingual gland lies entirely superior to the mylohyoid muscle
  • Tongue muscle embedding (D) → Lie on the floor of the mouth mucosa, not within the muscular tongue
  • Digastric space (E) → This describes the submandibular gland in the digastric/submandibular triangle
354. Which of the following is not a complication of submandibular gland excision?
  • A. Frey’s syndrome
  • B. Anaesthesia of the ipsilateral tongue
  • C. Weakness of the corner of the mouth
  • D. Anaesthesia of submental skin
Clinical Anatomy:
  • Complications of salivary surgery
    • Frey's syndrome (gustatory sweating) → Complication of parotid gland surgery (injury to auriculotemporal nerve), never submandibular excision
Exclusions:
  • Ipsilateral tongue anesthesia (B) → Risk due to proximity/damage to the lingual nerve
  • Corner of mouth weakness (C) → Risk due to damage to the marginal mandibular branch of the facial nerve
  • Submental anesthesia (D) → Caused by injury to local cutaneous branches in the submandibular triangle
355. Which of the following conditions is Paralysis of the ipsilateral tongue associated with ascending bacterial sialadenitis?
  • A. Dental abscess
  • B. Oral thrush
  • C. Dehydration
  • D. Otitis media
  • E. Hyperglycaemia.
Pathophysiology:
  • Ascending bacterial sialadenitis (Note: "Paralysis of the ipsilateral tongue" in the question text is a typesetting mistake in this question bank where the last option of Q354 was merged into Q355. The actual clinical question is "Which condition is associated with ascending bacterial sialadenitis?")
    • Dehydration → Causes salivary hypofunction/stasis, which allows oral bacteria to ascend retrogradely into salivary ducts
Exclusions:
  • Dental abscess (A) → Odontogenic in origin; does not cause ascending ductal sialadenitis
  • Oral thrush (B) → Fungal mucosal infection (Candida); does not lead to bacterial ductal parenchymal infection
  • Otitis media (D) → Unrelated middle ear infection
  • Hyperglycaemia (E) → General infectious risk factor, but lacks the direct salivary stasis effect of dehydration
356. Which of the following bacteria is the most common cause of bacterial sialadenitis?
  • A. Staphylococcus aureus
  • B. Staphylococcus epidermidis
  • C. Streptococcus pyogenes
  • D. Pseudomonas aeruginosa
  • E. Atypical mycobacteria.
Microbiology:
  • Acute suppurative sialadenitis
    • Primary pathogen → Staphylococcus aureus is the most commonly isolated aerobic pathogen in acute salivary ductal infections
Exclusions:
  • S. epidermidis (B) → Typically a benign skin contaminant, rarely pathogenic in ducts
  • S. pyogenes (C), P. aeruginosa (D), Atypical mycobacteria (E) → Less common; usually associated with specialized clinical circumstances or immunocompromise
357. In a 4-year-old with recurrent bilateral parotid swelling made worse on eating, what is the most likely diagnosis?
  • A. Sialolithiasis
  • B. Salivary duct stricture
  • C. Sjögren’s syndrome
  • D. Recurrent parotitis of childhood
  • E. HIV-associated parotitis
Pediatric ENT:
  • Recurrent parotitis of childhood
    • Presentation → Idiopathic, recurrent episodes of bilateral salivary swelling in children under 5, aggravated by eating, typically resolving spontaneously after puberty
Exclusions:
  • Sialolithiasis (A) & Stricture (B) → Extremely rare in young pediatric populations
  • Sjögren's syndrome (C) → Typically a chronic disease of adult/middle-aged females
  • HIV parotitis (E) → Less common than idiopathic childhood recurrent parotitis unless other syndromic or systemic features are present
358. Chronic parotitis in children is pathognomonic of what disease?
  • A. HIV
  • B. Sialolithiasis
  • C. Bacterial parotitis
  • D. Tuberculous parotitis
  • E. Wegener’s granulomatosis
Clinical Immunology:
  • Bilateral chronic parotid swelling in children
    • Pathognomonic association → Strongly alerts the clinician to suspect pediatric HIV infection in developing or underserved areas
Exclusions:
  • Sialolithiasis (B) & Bacterial (C) → Typically acute, unilateral, and episodic
  • Tuberculous (D), Wegener's (E) → Exceedingly rare causes, not classically considered pathognomonic pediatric markers
359. How should a benign tumor involving the tail of parotid be managed?
  • A. Enucleation
  • B. Open biopsy prior to formal excision
  • C. Radiotherapy
  • D. Total parotidectomy
  • E. Superficial parotidectomy
Surgical Management:
  • Parotid neoplasms
    • Superficial parotidectomy → Standard management; involves dissecting the gland superficial to the facial nerve plane to ensure clear margins while protecting the nerve
Exclusions:
  • Enucleation (A) → Contraindicated due to high risk of capsular rupture and tumor seeding/recurrence
  • Open biopsy (B) → Absolutely contraindicated; exposes patients to facial nerve injury and tumor tract contamination
  • Radiotherapy (C) → Has no therapeutic role for benign epithelial salivary gland tumors
  • Total parotidectomy (D) → Excessive intervention for a localized benign superficial/tail tumor
360. Which of the following is not a branch of the facial nerve?
  • A. Temporal
  • B. Orbital
  • C. Zygomatic
  • D. Buccal
  • E. Cervical.
Anatomy:
  • Extratemporal branches of CN VII (Facial Nerve)
    • Branches → Five classic terminal branches: Temporal, Zygomatic, Buccal, Marginal Mandibular, and Cervical
Exclusions:
  • "Orbital" (B) → No branch of the facial nerve is named "orbital" (the orbital region is innervated by zygomatic/temporal motor branches)
361. Which of the following is not associated with xerostomia?
  • A. Depression
  • B. Dehydration
  • C. Sjögren’s syndrome
  • D. Radiotherapy to the head and neck
  • E. Cholinergic medications.
Pharmacology:
  • Autonomic nervous control of salivation
    • Cholinergic medications → Act as parasympathomimetics that stimulate fluid salivary secretion (used clinically to treat xerostomia)
Exclusions:
  • Depression (A) → Directly reduces salivary production and is treated with anticholinergic-profile antidepressants
  • Dehydration (B) → Systemic fluid depletion reduces baseline saliva secretion
  • Sjögren's (C) → Autoimmune destruction of lacrimal and salivary secretory cells
  • Radiotherapy (D) → Induces permanent, irreversible fibrosis of major salivary gland acinar tissues

Section 10: Cleft Lip & Palate (CL1–CL4)

1. About cleft lip and palate, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. They are commonly associated with other congenital anomalies.
  • B. Cleft palate causes feeding difficulties for the baby.
  • C. Otitis media commonly affects babies with cleft palate.
  • D. The ideal age for cleft palate repair is 4-6 years.
Pediatric Plastic Surgery:
  • Cleft palate surgical correction timeline
    • Ideal repair age → Typically closed between 9 and 18 months to facilitate normal speech development, long before 4-6 years
Exclusions:
  • Congenital anomalies (A) → Frequently linked to syndromic entities (e.g., Pierre Robin sequence, Trisomy 13)
  • Feeding difficulties (B) → Baby cannot create intraoral negative pressure because of the palate defect
  • Otitis media (C) → Dysfunctional tensor veli palatini muscles fail to open the Eustachian tube, leading to chronic middle ear fluid accumulation
2. Factors influencing the development of cleft lip/palate include all the following EXCEPT
  • A. Increased parental age.
  • B. Vitamin A deficiency during pregnancy.
  • C. Infections during pregnancy.
  • D. Smoking during pregnancy.
Teratology & Etiology:
  • Nutritional teratogens in pregnancy
    • Vitamin A → It is hypervitaminosis A (excessive intake, e.g., retinoids), not deficiency, that is strongly teratogenic, causing significant craniofacial malformations like clefting
Exclusions:
  • Parental age (A), Maternal infections (C), Maternal smoking (D) → Well-established clinical and environmental factors that elevate the statistical risk of clefting
3. Cleft lip should be repaired at the age of:
  • A. 2 years.
  • B. 6-9 months.
  • C. 3 months.
  • D. 9-12 months.
Pediatric Plastic Surgery:
  • "Rule of Tens" for unilateral/bilateral cleft lip reconstruction
    • Optimal timeline → Typically performed around 10-12 weeks of age (~3 months) once the infant meets weight and physiological parameters
Exclusions:
  • 2 years (A), 6-9 months (B), 9-12 months (D) → Standard guidelines repair the lip much earlier (at ~3 months) to optimize oral-motor function and cosmetic development
4. The aim of treatment of an infant with a cleft lip is to:
  • A. Improve appearance.
  • B. Make feeding possible.
  • C. Achieve adequate speech.
  • D. Achieve adequate dentition
Surgical Goals:
  • Cleft lip vs Cleft palate reconstruction
    • Primary goal of cleft lip repair → Restoration of normal facial cosmetics, symmetry, and reconstruction of the continuous orbicularis oris muscle sphincter
Exclusions:
  • Feeding (B) & Speech (C) → Primary physiological objectives of cleft palate repair (by establishing a competent velopharyngeal valve and separating oral/nasal cavities)
  • Dentition (D) → Managed with long-term orthodontics and secondary alveolar bone grafting, not early lip repair alone

Section 11: Nutrition & Fluid Therapy (Q362–Q482)

362. Regarding enteral route of nutrition all the following are true EXCEPT:
  • a. May cause bacterial overgrowth in the gut.
  • b. Reduces stress ulceration.
  • c. Is less expensive than the parenteral route.
  • d. Decreases the incidence of cholestasis by promoting biliary flow.
Pathophysiology:
  • Enteral nutrition maintains gut integrity and mucosal barrier functions
    • Action → Prevents bacterial translocation and overgrowth rather than causing it
Exclusions:
  • Stress ulcers (b) → Reduced due to standard luminal nutrient buffering
  • Cost (c) → Enteral access is highly cost-effective compared to parenteral
  • Cholestasis (d) → Decreased by triggering physiological bile release
363. Common indications for nutritional support include:
  • a. Ileus more than 4 days.
  • b. Massive bowel resection.
  • c. Intestinal fistula.
  • d. All of the above.
Clinical Evaluation:
  • Indications for support arise when gut function is lost or recovery is delayed
    • Fistulas, resections, or prolonged post-op ileus → Deprive baseline absorption
Exclusions:
  • A, B, and C → Individually correct, making "All of the above" (d) the target
364. Complications of TPN include:
  • a- Sepsis.
  • b- Pneumothorax.
  • c- Hyperosmolar coma.
  • d- All of the above.
Pathophysiology:
  • Total Parenteral Nutrition introduces multi-system risks
    • Mechanisms → Infectious (sepsis), mechanical (pneumothorax), and metabolic (hyperosmolar state due to high glucose)
Exclusions:
  • Individual options (a, b, c) → All represent documented hazards of TPN delivery
365. Ten days after right hemicolectomy, a patient develops an external fistula that discharges about 100ml/day. There is no evidence of sepsis and the patient passes regular daily motions. The suitable method of supplying nutritional needs is:
  • A. Oral intake of a regular diet.
  • B. Nasogastric fluid feeding.
  • C. NPO and jejunostomy feeding.
  • D. NPO and total parenteral nutrition.
Clinical Evaluation:
  • Low-output external fistula (100ml/day) without sepsis
    • Status → Bowel is functioning distally (regular motions); oral feeding is safe
Exclusions:
  • NGT (B), Jejunostomy (C), TPN (D) → Unnecessary invasive therapies for low-volume, uncomplicated fistulas
366. About jejunostomy feeding, all the following are true, except:
  • A. It is a useful temporary feeding method after complex gastric operations.
  • B. Jejunostomy feeding starts with elemental formulas then proceeds gradually to blenderised food.
  • C. Jejunostomy feeds should be sterile.
  • D. Jejunostomy feeding can cause colicky abdominal pain.
Clinical Protocol:
  • Direct small bowel access requires strict fluid-based nutrition
    • Contraindication → Blenderized foods should not be used due to high risk of tube occlusion and severe malabsorption
Exclusions:
  • Gastric bypass post-op (A), Sterility (C), Colic (D) → Valid clinical parameters and features of jejunostomy administration
367. The complications and disadvantages of nasogastric tube feeding include all the following, except:
  • A. High cost.
  • B. Possibility of aspiration pneumonia.
  • C. Pharyngitis.
  • D. Esophagitis.
Clinical Evaluation:
  • NGT placement is highly cost-effective
    • Financial → Low material cost compared to parenterals or surgical enterostomies
Exclusions:
  • Aspiration (B), Pharyngitis (C), Esophagitis (D) → True mechanical and anatomical complications of indwelling tubes
368. About total parenteral nutrition (TPN), all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. The main source of energy is 25% dextrose.
  • B. Amino acids can be mixed with dextrose in a 3.L bag.
  • C. The rate of parenteral nutrition administration is better regulated by a pump.
  • D. Metabolic control is better maintained by TPN than by enteral nutrition.
Pathophysiology:
  • Enteral feeding utilizes standard portal circulation pathways
    • Action → Enteral route provides superior glycemic control compared to IV dextrose (TPN)
Exclusions:
  • Dextrose energy (A), 3L bags (B), Pump infusions (C) → Correct standard features of clinical TPN administration
369. About intravenous lipid preparations, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. They provide energy at a ratio of 9 kcal/ g fat.
  • B. They provide essential fatty acids.
  • C. They make the main source of energy of total parenteral nutrition (TPN).
  • D. They can be given through a peripheral vein.
Pathophysiology:
  • Nutrient distribution in TPN formulas
    • Source → Dextrose (carbohydrate), not lipid, provides the primary daily energy source
Exclusions:
  • 9 kcal/g (A), Essential fats (B), Peripheral delivery (D) → True characteristics of modern lipid emulsions
370. Which of the following is true regarding daily caloric and/or protein requirements?
  • A. Preterm infants may need up to 2 g/kg per day of protein.
  • B. A 60-kg adult needs about 90 g/day of protein.
  • C. A respiratory quotient greater than 1.0 suggests overfeeding.
  • D. Burn victims generally require less protein than other trauma victims.
  • E. A respiratory quotient (RQ) of 0.8 suggests starvation.
Pathophysiology:
  • Respiratory Quotient (RQ) analysis
    • RQ > 1.0 → Reflects lipogenesis (fat synthesis), signaling excessive calorie delivery (overfeeding)
Exclusions:
  • Infants (A) → Require higher levels (~3 g/kg)
  • Adult protein (B) → Typically 0.8-1.2 g/kg (~48-72g for a 60-kg adult)
  • Starvation RQ (E) → Starvation values drop closer to 0.7 (fat oxidation)
371. A 23-year-old male with multisystem trauma is still on the ventilator. Which of the following is true regarding tools for assessing nutritional status?
  • A. Use of serial measurements of albumin and prealbumin is the “gold standard” for trauma patients.
  • B. Measurement of nitrogen balance is limited because it underestimates nitrogen input.
  • C. The Mini Nutritional Assessment is designed specifically for hospitalized patients.
  • D. Creatinine height index may overestimate lean body mass in trauma patients.
  • E. Out of all of the serum protein measurements, transferrin seems to correlate the closest to nitrogen balance.
Biochemical Assessment:
  • Creatinine height index (CHI) in acute trauma
    • Failure → Altered creatinine kinetics/muscle wasting overestimates true healthy lean body mass
Exclusions:
  • Albumin (A) → Negative acute-phase reactant; unreliable in acute critical illness
  • Mini Assessment (C) → Designed for ambulatory geriatric cohorts
372. Which of the following is true regarding the risk of hypoglycemia following cessation of TPN?
  • A. It commonly occurs in patients with liver disease.
  • B. TPN should be tapered in most patients so as to avoid this complication.
  • C. This complication is relatively common.
  • D. It is more likely to occur in a diabetic patient.
  • E. It is more likely to occur in patients with renal disease.
Pathophysiology:
  • Insulin-glucose balance upon sudden TPN stoppage
    • Vulnerability → Diabetic patients have impaired glucose counter-regulation, increasing hypoglycemia susceptibility
Exclusions:
  • Cessation (B) → Routine tapering is rarely required in standard non-diabetic cohorts
  • Incidence (C) → Rebound hypoglycemia is biochemically rare in patients with intact liver function
373. Which of the following is true about the pharmacologic treatment of cancer cachexia?
  • A. There is no evidence that ghrelin mimetics are of benefit.
  • B. Cannabinoids are superior to megestrol acetate in stimulating appetite and weight gain.
  • C. When initiated early, megestrol acetate has been demonstrated to improve survival.
  • D. Megestrol is a progesterone derivative.
  • E. Anabolic steroids have been shown to have lasting improvements in appetite and improved long term weight gain.
Pharmacology:
  • Megestrol acetate classifications
    • Structure → Synthetic progestin (progesterone derivative) used clinically to stimulate appetite
Exclusions:
  • Cannabinoids (B) → Inferior compared directly to megestrol
  • Survival (C) → Improves short-term weight/appetite but does not prolong survival
374. Which of the following is true regarding energy homeostasis during periods of fasting or starvation?
  • A. The largest source of energy after glycogen is depleted is free fatty acids.
  • B. Skeletal muscle has the largest store of glycogen available systemically.
  • C. Glucose is converted to lactate in the liver.
  • D. Red blood cells are reliant on aerobic metabolism of glucose.
  • E. The brain is unable to utilize ketones.
Pathophysiology:
  • Depletion of carbohydrate stores shifts energy metabolic reliance
    • Fuel → Adipose tissue breakdown yields free fatty acids (FFAs) as the primary energy source
Exclusions:
  • Muscle glycogen (B) → Cannot be exported to bloodstream due to lack of glucose-6-phosphatase
  • RBCs (D) → Lack mitochondria; completely dependent on anaerobic glycolysis
  • Brain (E) → Readily adapts to use ketones during prolonged starvation
375. Which of the following is true regarding fluid homeostasis during and immediately after a 4-hour laparotomy?
  • A. The majority of postoperative oral fluid replacement will be primarily absorbed in the colon.
  • B. Laparoscopic and open abdominal causes have similar unmeasured fluid losses.
  • C. Administration of 5% albumin pulls third-space fluids into the intravascular space.
  • D. Insensible fluid deficit may be as high as 4 L.
  • E. About 50% of a crystalloid fluid bolus will remain intravascular.
Clinical Evaluation:
  • Laparotomy exposure and tissue evaporative fluid loss
    • Insensible loss → Prolonged open abdominal exposure can lose up to 4 liters of fluid
Exclusions:
  • Oral fluids (A) → Primarily absorbed in small intestine
  • Crystalloids (E) → Only ~20-25% remains in vascular space after 1 hour
376. A potential complication of massive red blood cell transfusions is:
  • A. Metabolic acidosis from citrate preservative.
  • B. Hypocalcemia from citrate preservative.
  • C. Hypokalemia due to prolonged red cell storage.
  • D. A rise in 2,3-diphosphoglycerate (DPG).
  • E. Shift of the oxyhemoglobin dissociation curve to the right.
Pathophysiology:
  • Citrate preservative interactions
    • Mechanism → Rapid citrate infusion chelates serum ionized calcium, precipitating acute hypocalcemia
Exclusions:
  • Acidosis (A) → Citrate metabolism actually generates bicarbonate, causing metabolic alkalosis
  • Hypokalemia (C) → RBC storage causes cell leakage, leading to hyperkalemia
  • DPG levels (D) → DPG decreases during storage, shifting curve to the left (E)
377. Which of the following amino acids can be synthesized de novo in humans in any physiologic state?
  • A. Tryptophan
  • B. Tyrosine
  • C. Glycine
  • D. Serine
  • E. Any branched-chain amino acid.
Biochemistry:
  • Synthesis categories of amino acids
    • Serine → Fully non-essential amino acid, synthesized endogenously from 3-phosphoglycerate
Exclusions:
  • Tryptophan (A), BCAAs (E) → Strictly essential; must be obtained from diet
  • Tyrosine (B), Glycine (C) → Conditionally essential in specific pathologic states
378. Which of the following is true regarding the use of preoperative TPN to prevent postoperative complications?
  • A. It is useful even if used for as little as 3 days.
  • B. It is efficacious if the patient has lost more than 15% weight before surgery.
  • C. There is no evidence that it lowers the complication rate.
  • D. Slightly overfeeding for 7 days is preferable as a means to maximize replacement of caloric deficits.
  • E. TPN is efficacious even in mild to moderate malnutrition.
Clinical Guidelines:
  • Preoperative nutritional intervention criteria
    • Indication → Indicated and highly effective when severe malnutrition (weight loss >15%) is present
Exclusions:
  • Duration (A) → Needs at least 7-10 days to demonstrate clinical benefit
  • Mild states (E) → Risks of central lines outbalance benefits in minor malnutrition
379. Which of the following is true regarding nutritional deficiencies following a partial gastrectomy with a Billroth II reconstruction?
  • A. Calcium absorption will be minimally affected because this primarily occurs in the ileum.
  • B. Iron deficiency anemia is more common with a Billroth I than a Billroth II.
  • C. Vitamin B12 deficiency will present with a low mean corpuscular volume.
  • D. The stomach has no intrinsic absorptive ability.
  • E. Carbohydrate absorption is not impaired after surgery.
Pathophysiology:
  • Carbohydrate transit post-reconstruction
    • Mechanics → Simple sugar digestion and uptake remain intact because pancreatic amylase still interfaces in the jejunum
Exclusions:
  • Calcium (A) → Absorbed in duodenum (bypassed in Billroth II)
  • B12 (C) → Presents as macrocytic anemia (elevated MCV)
380. The most important amino acid used for gluconeogenesis by the liver is:
  • A. Glutamine
  • B. Serine
  • C. Alanine
  • D. Tyrosine
  • E. Asparagine
Biochemistry:
  • Alanine-Glucose cycle
    • Substrate → Alanine is the primary amino acid cleared by the liver for conversion into glucose
Exclusions:
  • Glutamine (A) → Principal fuel for kidney and gut mucosal cells
  • Others (B, D, E) → Minor roles in systemic gluconeogenic pathways
381. Elevated serum level of TNF-alpha and elevated urine level of proteolysis-inducing factor (PIF). Which of the following cancers does this patient most likely have?
  • A. Colon
  • B. Prostate
  • C. Pancreas
  • D. Breast
  • E. Liver
Oncology:
  • Tumor cachexia markers
    • Association → Pancreatic adenocarcinoma is highly associated with elevated PIF and TNF-alpha levels driving muscle catabolism
Exclusions:
  • Colon (A), Prostate (B), Breast (D) → Less consistent secretion of PIF cachexia factors
382. Poor glucose control is a manifestation of deficiency of:
  • A. Zinc
  • B. Copper
  • C. Chromium
  • D. Molybdenum
  • E. Selenium
Biochemistry:
  • Chromium function
    • Action → Core component of chromodulin; potentiates insulin receptor signaling
Exclusions:
  • Zinc (A) → Wound healing cofactor
  • Selenium (E) → Antioxidant cofactor (glutathione peroxidase)
383. Which of the following is true regarding long-term TPN?
  • A. Fat is considered the nutritional basis of TPN.
  • B. It may lead to a mucin gel matrix of cholesterol crystals and calcium bilirubinate in the gallbladder.
  • C. Hepatic dysfunction related to TPN is less likely to be lethal in infants than in adults.
  • D. It has not been shown to lead to hepatic fibrosis.
  • E. Carnitine supplementation has been shown to reverse TPN-related liver damage.
Pathophysiology:
  • Lack of enteral stimulation causes biliary stasis
    • Consequence → Promotes gallbladder sludge (mucin gel matrix + cholesterol/calcium bilirubinate crystals)
Exclusions:
  • Fat base (A) → Dextrose is the primary macronutrient base
  • Lethality (C) → TPN cholestasis is highly dangerous and can progress to cirrhosis in infants
384. Which of the following amino acids has shown potential for increasing the absorptive capability of the intestine after large segment small bowel resection?
  • A. Glutamine
  • B. Serine
  • C. Alanine
  • D. Tyrosine
  • E. Arginine
Pathophysiology:
  • Gut mucosal energy substrate
    • Action → Glutamine stimulates mucosal hyperplasia and adaptation, enhancing small bowel surface area
Exclusions:
  • Serine (B), Alanine (C), Arginine (E) → Do not show same mucosal-trophic properties
385. Which of the following statements regarding starvation are true? (Select the matching combination highlighted in Key)
  • A. Cerebral energy metabolism requirement is 100 g/day of glucose.
  • B. Glycogenolysis comes into play in the first 2–3 days.
  • C. Mobilisation of fat is dependent on a fall in circulating insulin levels.
  • D. Intravenous dextrose has a proteinsparing effect.
  • E. Ketone bodies cannot substitute for glucose for cerebral energy metabolism.
Pathophysiology:
  • Underlying starvation physiology
    • Facts → Brain needs ~100g glucose daily; insulin drop unlocks hormone-sensitive lipase (fat mobilization); minor dextrose prevents muscle catabolism (protein sparing)
Exclusions:
  • Glycogen (B) → Depleted within 24 hours, not 2-3 days
  • Ketone substitution (E) → Ketones readily cross BBB to substitute glucose during prolonged fasting
386. Clinical and biochemical markers suggestive of malnutrition: (Identify the designated markers)
  • A. Albumin
  • B. Urea
  • C. Transthyretin
  • D. Skin fold thickness
  • E. Weight loss
Clinical Assessment:
  • Markers of acute/chronic nutrition decline
    • Indices → Transthyretin (prealbumin) (C) has a short half-life reflecting acute intake changes; weight loss (E) is the classic clinical metric
Exclusions:
  • Albumin (A) → Serum half-life is too long (20 days) for acute acute assessment
333. Which of the following statements regarding free flaps are true?
  • A. Free flap are the best way of reconstructing major composite loss of tissue.
  • B. Surgical expertise and equipment in microsurgery are essential for the use of free flaps.
  • C. Debridement of the area of reconstruction is necessary for the use of free flaps.
  • D. Major donor site morbidity is a possible disadvantage in free-flap surgery.
  • E. It usually takes more time to perform the surgery associated with a microsurgical procedure unless the surgeon is experienced.
True Statements:
  • Composite Reconstruction (A): Free tissue transfer is the gold standard for reconstructing extensive complex defects that involve several tissue types (e.g., combined bone, muscle, and skin loss).
  • Specialized Requirements (B): The success of free-flap surgery relies on precise microvascular anastomoses, making advanced surgical expertise and microsurgical equipment indispensable.
  • Recipient Bed Prep (C): Thorough wound debridement to remove all necrotic, infected, or poorly vascularized tissue is required before a free flap can be successfully transferred.
  • Donor Deficit (D): A primary risk of free-flap harvesting is donor site morbidity, which can result in functional deficits, scarring, or contour deformities at the collection site.
