  • Operative Duration (E): Microsurgical free tissue transfer is highly complex and technically demanding, requiring significant operative time that generally decreases only with surgeon experience and team efficiency.
388. Which of the following is a major cause of ketoacidosis? (Select matching causes)
  • A. Alcoholism
  • B. Diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA)
  • C. Inborn errors of metabolism
  • D. Starvation
  • E. Insulin therapy
Pathophysiology:
  • Ketone body production pathways
    • Drivers → Starvation, ethanol toxicity, absence of insulin (DKA), and metabolic blocks yield high-levels of acetoacetate/beta-hydroxybutyrate
Exclusions:
  • Insulin therapy (E) → Restores glycolysis, heavily suppressing ketogenesis
389. Which of the following statements regarding water homeostasis are true? (Select matching options)
  • A. The total body water (TBW) content in an adult male is 70 per cent of body weight.
  • B. Two-thirds of TBW is intracellular.
  • C. An average adult has approx. 3 L of plasma.
  • D. TBW is highest in elderly women.
  • E. Water moves freely across cell Membranes.
Pathophysiology:
  • Distribution of body water
    • Rules → Intracellular volume makes up ~2/3 of TBW (B); intravascular plasma is ~3 liters (C); membranes are fully permeable to water movement (E)
Exclusions:
  • Adult male (A) → TBW is approximately 60% of body weight
  • Elderly women (D) → Have the lowest percentage of TBW due to low muscle/high fat
390. Which of the following statements are untrue? (Identify the incorrect physiology statements)
  • A. The small bowel receives approximately 7–8 L of fluid/day.
  • B. Malabsorption is inevitable if 30 percent of the small intestine is removed.
  • C. The jejunum is vital in the absorption of nutrients.
  • D. Vitamin B12 and bile salts are absorbed in the ileum.
  • E. The enterohepatic circulation of bile salts is essential to maintain the bile salt pool.
Pathophysiology:
  • Small bowel resection limits
    • Bowel reserve → 30% resection is well-tolerated, so malabsorption is not inevitable (B)
Exclusions:
  • A, D, and E → Standard correct facts in gastroenterology
391. Which of the following statements regarding starvation are true?
  • A. Fats are initially mobilized to meet energy requirements.
  • B. Muscle glycogen can be directly utilized.
  • C. After a short fast, insulin level falls.
  • D. After a short fast, adaptive ketogenesis occurs.
  • E. Gluconeogenesis only takes place in the Liver.
Diagnosis:
Insulin levels drop rapidly in early fasting to facilitate lipolysis and glycogenolysis, followed by adaptive ketogenesis as the brain shifts to ketone body utilization.

Exclusions:
Fats (A) & Glycogen (B) → Glycogen is metabolized first; muscle glycogen lacks glucose-6-phosphatase so it cannot directly supply blood glucose.
Gluconeogenesis (E) → Occurs in both the liver and the kidneys.
392. Which of the following statements regarding 0.9 per cent normal saline are true?
  • A. It has the same sodium concentration as plasma.
  • B. It has equimolar concentration of sodium and chloride.
  • C. It is low in potassium.
  • D. It does not contain dextrose.
  • E. It is the best fluid to be used in hypovolaemia.
Diagnosis:
Normal saline contains 154 mmol/L of Na+ and 154 mmol/L of Cl- (equimolar) and lacks glucose/dextrose.

Exclusions:
Sodium (A) → Plasma sodium is ~135–145 mmol/L, making NS hypertonic relative to plasma sodium.
Potassium (C) & Best fluid (E) → Lacks potassium completely; balanced crystalloids (like Ringer's) are generally preferred to avoid hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis.
393. Which of the following statements are true of gastrointestinal (GI) secretions?
  • A. Pancreatic fluid is rich in bicarbonate.
  • B. The chloride content of gastric fluid is around 110 mmol/L.
  • C. Gastric fluid has the highest quantity of potassium.
  • D. Gastric outlet obstruction is associated with hypochloraemic alkalosis.
  • E. Most intestinal losses are replaced with normal saline and potassium.
Diagnosis:
Pancreatic fluid is rich in HCO3- to neutralize gastric acid. Gastric acid loss (as in pyloric stenosis) leads to classic hypochloremic hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis. Intestinal secretions are rich in sodium and potassium, necessitating NS with KCl replacement.

Exclusions:
Potassium (C) → Saliva and colon secretions have higher concentrations of potassium than gastric fluid.
394. Which of the following statements regarding bowel resections are true?
  • A. Diarrhoea is unusual, following ileal resection.
  • B. The sodium content of high output fistulas is about 90 mmol/L.
  • C. It is appropriate for patients with high output from jejunostomy to drink plenty of water.
  • D. Peptic ulceration is a complication of short-bowel syndrome.
  • E. Oxalate stones are commoner following bowel resection.
True Statements:
  • Fistula Output (B): Small bowel fistula fluid is rich in sodium, typically containing around 90 mmol/L.
  • Peptic Ulcers (D): Gastric hypersecretion of acid (causing peptic ulceration) is a recognized transient complication of short-bowel syndrome, driven by the loss of inhibitory feedback hormones from the resected bowel.
  • Oxalate Stones (E): Malabsorption of fat leads to calcium binding to fatty acids (saponification). This leaves dietary oxalate free for colonic absorption, increasing the risk of calcium oxalate renal stones.
Exclusions:
  • Option A: Diarrhea is highly common and expected following ileal resection due to bile acid malabsorption.
  • Option C: Hypotonic fluids like water promote sodium wasting in high-output jejunostomies; patients should instead consume glucose-saline rehydration solutions.
395. The following results were obtained on an elderly gentleman seen in A&E with melaena: Na, 133 mmol/L; K, 10 mmol/L; creatinine, 153 µmol/L; urea, 12.7 mmol/L; corrected Ca, 0.82 mmol/L; aspartate transaminase (AST), 32 IU; alanine transaminase (ALT), 40 IU; alkaline phosphatase (ALP), undetectable. What is the cause?
  • A. K+ overdosage
  • B. Renal failure
  • C. Contaminated blood
  • D. Liver failure
  • E. All of the above
Diagnosis:
The combination of extreme hyperkalemia (10 mmol/L), profound hypocalcemia (0.82 mmol/L), and completely undetectable ALP is highly indicative of contamination with EDTA (potassium EDTA tube used for FBC), which chelates calcium and zinc (essential for ALP activity).

Exclusions:
Renal/Liver failure (B, D) → Cannot cause completely undetectable ALP or a potassium level compatible with life in a stable outpatient.
396. Which of the following are associated with hypovolaemia?
  • A. Tachycardia
  • B. Uraemia
  • C. Decreased urinary sodium excretion
  • D. Hypotension
  • E. Increased urine output.
Diagnosis:
Hypovolemia triggers compensatory tachycardia and hypotension. Aldosterone activation leads to renal sodium conservation (low urinary sodium), and decreased renal perfusion causes pre-renal uremia.

Exclusions:
Urine output (E) → Hypovolemia causes oliguria (decreased urine output), not polyuria.
397. A 15-year-old girl was admitted with abdominal pain and severe diarrhoea after return from holiday in Kenya. She is hypokalaemic with a metabolic acidosis. Her investigations are as follows: Blood: Na, 147 mmol/L; K, 2.0 mmol/L; Cl, 115 mmol/L; HCO3, 13 mmol/L. Diarrhoeal fluid: Na, 77 mmol/L; osmolality, 248; K, 50 mmol/L. What condition are these features consistent with?
  • A. Carcinoid syndrome
  • B. Tropical sprue
  • C. Zollinger–Ellison syndrome
  • D. Secretory diarrhoea
  • E. Coeliac disease
Diagnosis:
Secretory diarrhea is characterized by high stool sodium and potassium loss, persistent diarrhea despite fasting, and a low osmotic gap [stool osmotic gap = $290 - 2 \times (\text{Na}^+ + \text{K}^+) \approx 36$ mOsm/kg].

Exclusions:
Malabsorption states (B, E) → Present with osmotic diarrhea (high osmotic gap, resolving with fasting) rather than profound secretory losses.
398. A 6-year-old child has been unwell with abdominal pain and vomiting for 2 days. He has been unable to take any food and has been drinking sips of milk. On examination, the child is irritable and dehydrated. The following results were obtained: Na, 143 mmol/L; K, 3.0 mmol/L; Cl, 90 mmol/L; bicarbonate, 16 mmol/L; glucose, 7.5 mmol/L; urea, 7.6 mmol/L; creatinine, 119 µmol/L; beta-OH-butyrate, 6 mmol/L (<0.3). What condition does this metabolic picture suggest?
  • A. DKA
  • B. Starvation ketosis
  • C. Lactic acidosis
  • D. Severe sepsis
  • E. Malnutrition.
Diagnosis:
Elevated beta-OH-butyrate (6 mmol/L) in a pediatric patient with starvation and vomiting points to starvation ketosis. Normal to slightly elevated blood glucose rules out Diabetic Ketoacidosis (DKA).

Exclusions:
DKA (A) → Excluded by the absence of severe hyperglycemia.
Malnutrition (E) → Typically presents with chronic, wasting findings rather than acute-onset starvation ketosis.
399. In case of small-bowel obstruction and vomiting, which of the following statements are true?
  • A. The urea and creatinine can rise.
  • B. There is a rise in urine osmolality.
  • C. There is increased urinary Na excretion.
  • D. Hyperchloremia is a common feature.
  • E. ADH rises.
Diagnosis:
Volume depletion from third-space loss and vomiting stimulates ADH release, increasing urine concentration (osmolality) and causing pre-renal uremia (elevated urea/creatinine).

Exclusions:
Na excretion (C) → Kidneys conserve sodium, so urinary sodium drops.
Chloride (D) → Vomiting of HCl causes hypochloremia, not hyperchloremia.
400. Which of the following statements regarding acid–base balance are true?
  • A. Na and K are the major cations.
  • B. Bicarbonate is the major anion.
  • C. A decreased anion gap can occur in myeloma.
  • D. A raised anion gap can occur in DKA.
  • E. A raised anion gap can occur in ethanol intoxication.
True Statements:
  • Major Cations (A): Sodium (Na+) is the principal extracellular cation and potassium (K+) is the principal intracellular cation; together, they constitute the major cations of the body's fluid compartments.
  • Decreased Anion Gap (C): Multiple myeloma produces positively charged IgG paraproteins (cationic proteins), which reduces the calculated anion gap.
  • Raised Anion Gap (D, E): Both diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA, via ketoacids) and ethanol intoxication (via lactate and ketone accumulation) introduce unmeasured metabolic anions that elevate the calculated anion gap.
Exclusions:
  • Option B: Chloride (Cl-) is the primary extracellular anion, whereas bicarbonate (HCO3-) is secondary.
401. Metabolic acidosis with a normal anion gap (AG) occurs with:
  • A. Diabetic acidosis
  • B. Renal failure
  • C. Severe diarrhea
  • D. Starvation
Diagnosis:
Diarrhea causes direct bicarbonate loss in stool, which is compensated for by renal chloride conservation, producing a normal anion gap (hyperchloremic) metabolic acidosis.

Exclusions:
DKA (A), Renal failure (B), & Starvation (D) → All cause accumulation of unmeasured organic or inorganic acids, resulting in a high anion gap.
402. All are possible causes of post operative hyponatremia EXCEPT:
  • A. Excess infusion of normal saline intraoperatively.
  • B. Administration antipsychotic medication.
  • C. Transient decrease in antidiuretic hormone (ADH) secretion.
  • D. Excess oral water intake
Diagnosis:
A transient decrease in ADH would promote free water excretion, causing hypernatremia or correcting hyponatremia. Thus, it is the correct exception.

Exclusions:
SIADH/Water intake (A, B, D) → Pain/stress and antipsychotics trigger ADH release, which along with excessive water intake causes dilutional hyponatremia.
403. Which of the following is an early sign of hyperkalemia?
  • A. Peaked waves
  • B. Peaked P waves
  • C. Peaked (shortened) QRS complex
  • D. Peaked U waves
Diagnosis:
The earliest electrocardiographic manifestation of hyperkalemia is the development of symmetrical, narrow, peaked T waves ("peaked waves").

Exclusions:
P waves (B), QRS (C), & U waves (D) → Hyperkalemia flattens P waves, widens (not shortens) the QRS, and U waves are typical of hypokalemia, not hyperkalemia.
404. Hypocalcemia may cause which of the following?
  • A. Congestive heart failure
  • B. Atrial fibrillation
  • C. Pancreatitis
  • D. Hypoparathyroidism
Diagnosis:
Calcium is essential for myocardial excitation-contraction coupling. Severe hypocalcemia can impair myocardial contractility and precipitate congestive heart failure.

Exclusions:
AF (B), Pancreatitis (C) & Hypoparathyroidism (D) → These are causes of hypocalcemia (or hypercalcemia in AF/pancreatitis risk), rather than consequences.
405. The next most appropriate test to order in a patient with a pH 7.1, Pco2 40, sodium 132, potassium 4.2, and chloride 105 is:
  • A. Serum bicarbonate
  • B. Serum magnesium
  • C. Serum ethanol
  • D. Serum salicylate
Diagnosis:
To evaluate a metabolic acidosis (indicated by low pH and normal Pco2), measuring the serum bicarbonate (or calculating the anion gap) is the primary first step.

Exclusions:
Toxins/Others (B, C, D) → These are secondary tests considered once the anion gap status is confirmed.
406. Which of the following is FALSE regarding hypertonic saline?
  • A. Is an arteriolar vasodilator and may increase bleeding
  • B. Should be avoided in closed head injury
  • C. Should not be used for initial resuscitation
  • D. Increases cerebral perfusion
Diagnosis:
Hypertonic saline is highly indicated and preferred in closed head injuries to decrease intracranial pressure and reduce cerebral edema.

Exclusions:
Arteriolar/Cerebral (A, C, D) → True statements; hypertonic saline does improve cerebral perfusion, can cause vasodilation, and is generally reserved for targeted therapy rather than broad initial resuscitation.
407. Normal saline is:
  • A. 135 mEq NaCl/L
  • B. 145 mEq NaCl/L
  • C. 148 mEq NaCl/L
  • D. 154 mEq NaCl/L
Diagnosis:
0.9% Normal Saline contains 154 mEq of Sodium and 154 mEq of Chloride per liter.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Underestimate the actual concentration of solutes in normal saline.
408. Fluid resuscitation using albumin:
  • A. Is associated with coagulopathy
  • B. Is available as 1% or 5% solutions
  • C. Can lead to pulmonary edema
  • D. Decreased factor XIII
Diagnosis:
Rapid expansion of intravascular volume with a highly hyperoncotic colloid (such as 25% albumin) can lead to circulatory overload and subsequent pulmonary edema.

Exclusions:
Coagulopathy (A) & Concentrations (B) → Albumin is typically available as 5% or 25% solutions; it is less associated with severe coagulopathies than synthetic starches.
409. Water constitutes what percentage of total body weight?
  • A. 30-40%
  • B. 40-50%
  • b. 50-60%
  • C. 60-70%
Diagnosis:
In average adult males, water accounts for approximately 60% of total body weight; in females, it is closer to 50% due to higher body fat, making 50–60% the accepted standard average range.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Fall outside the physiological adult mean ranges.
410. If a patient’s serum glucose increases by 180 mg/dL, what is the increase in serum osmolality, assuming all other laboratory values remain constant?
  • A. Does not change
  • B. 8
  • c. 10
  • C. 12
Diagnosis:
The contribution of glucose to osmolality is calculated by dividing glucose in mg/dL by 18. Thus, $\frac{180}{18} = 10$ mOsm/kg.

Exclusions:
A, B, D → Do not represent the correct biochemical conversion factor.
411. What is the actual potassium of a patient with pH of 7.8 and serum potassium of 2.2?
  • A. 2.2
  • B. 2.8
  • d. 3.2
  • C. 3.4
Diagnosis:
For every 0.1 increase in pH above 7.4, serum potassium drops by approximately 0.3 mEq/L due to intracellular shifts. A pH of 7.8 (0.4 deviation) shifts serum potassium down by ~1.2 mEq/L. Actual potassium in a normoneutral state is $2.2 + 1.2 = 3.4$ mEq/L.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Underestimate the shift caused by severe alkalemia.
412. The free water deficit of a 70 kg man with serum sodium of 154 is:
  • A. 0.1 L
  • B. 0.7 L
  • C. 1 L
  • D. 7L
Diagnosis:
Using the free water deficit formula [$\text{Deficit} = \text{TBW} \times (\frac{\text{Na}^+}{140} - 1)$ where $\text{TBW} = 42\text{ L}$], we calculate $42 \times (1.1 - 1) = 4.2\text{ L}$. However, the provided curriculum answer key designates 7L as correct.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Do not match the designated answer key value.
413. A patient with serum calcium of 6.8 and albumin of 1.2 has a corrected calcium of:
  • A. 7.7
  • B. 8.0
  • e. 8.6
  • C. 9.2
Diagnosis:
$\text{Corrected Calcium} = \text{Measured Calcium} + 0.8 \times (4.0 - \text{Albumin})$. Inserting the values: $6.8 + 0.8 \times 2.8 = 6.8 + 2.24 = 9.04$ mg/dL (~9.2).

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Do not apply the standard albumin-calcium correction formula correctly.
414. All the following treatments for hyperkalemia reduce serum potassium EXCEPT:
  • A. Bicarbonate
  • B. Kayexalate
  • C. Glucose infusion with insulin
  • D. Calcium
Diagnosis:
Intravenous calcium (gluconate or chloride) stabilizes the cardiac membrane potential to prevent arrhythmias but does not lower serum potassium levels.

Exclusions:
Active reducers (A, B, C) → Insulin/glucose and bicarbonate shift potassium intracellularly; Kayexalate binds potassium in the GI tract to excrete it from the body.
415. An alcoholic patient with serum albumin of 3.9, K of 3.1, Mg of 2.4, Ca of 7.8, and PO4 of 3.2 receives three boluses of IV potassium and has serum potassium of 3.3. You should:
  • A. Continue to bolus potassium until the serum level is >3.6.
  • B. Give MgSO4 IV
  • C. Check the ionized calcium.
  • D. Check the BUN and creatinine.
Diagnosis:
Hypokalemia refractory to potassium supplementation is often caused by concurrent hypomagnesemia. Magnesium is required for potassium retention in the renal tubules; thus, giving IV magnesium sulfate is necessary to correct the hypokalemia.

Exclusions:
Potassium/Calcium (A, C) → Potassium repletion will remain unsuccessful without correcting the underlying magnesium cofactor.
416. Calculate the daily maintenance fluids needed for a 60-kg female:
  • A. 2060
  • B. 2100
  • b. 2160
  • C. 2400
Diagnosis:
Under the standard 4-2-1 rule ($100\text{ mL/kg}$ for first 10 kg, $50\text{ mL/kg}$ for next 10 kg, and $20\text{ mL/kg}$ for remaining weight): $1000 + 500 + 40 \times 20 = 2300\text{ mL}$. However, the designated correct option in this question-set is 2100 mL.

Exclusions:
A, C, D → Diverge from the curriculum answer key.
417. A patient who has spasms in the hand when a blood pressure cuff is blown up most likely has:
  • A. Hypercalcemia
  • B. Hypocalcemia
  • C. Hypermagnesemia
  • D. Hypomagnesemia
Diagnosis:
This clinical sign is Trousseau’s sign of latent tetany, a highly sensitive and specific indicator of hypocalcemia.

Exclusions:
Hypercalcemia (A) & Magnesium (C, D) → Hypercalcemia decreases neuromuscular excitability. Hypomagnesemia can present with hyperreflexia, but Trousseau's sign is classically diagnostic of low calcium.
418. The actual AG of a chronic alcoholic with Na 133, K 4, Cl-101, HCO3-22, albumin of 2.5 mg/dL is:
  • A. 6
  • B. 10
  • c. 14
  • C. 15
Diagnosis:
Calculated Anion Gap = $\text{Na} - (\text{Cl} + \text{HCO3}) = 133 - 123 = 10$. Hypoalbuminemia lowers the normal AG. For every 1 g/dL drop in albumin below 4, the expected AG drops by 2.5. Here, a drop of 1.5 g/dL translates to a corrected AG of $10 + 3.75 \approx 14–15$.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Underestimate the influence of the patient's hypoalbuminemia.
419. The effective osmotic pressure between the plasma and interstitial fluid compartments is primarily controlled by:
  • A. Bicarbonate
  • B. Chloride ion
  • C. Potassium ion
  • D. Protein
Diagnosis:
Plasma proteins (like albumin) do not easily cross the capillary membrane, maintaining the colloid oncotic pressure that regulates fluid balance between vascular and interstitial compartments.

Exclusions:
Ions (A, B, C) → Small ions cross the capillary membrane freely and do not generate effective intravascular oncotic gradients.
420. The metabolic derangement most commonly seen in patients with profuse vomiting:
  • A. Hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis
  • B. Hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic acidosis
  • C. Hypochloremic, hyperkalemic metabolic alkalosis
  • D. Hypochloremic, hyperkalemic metabolic acidosis
Diagnosis:
Vomiting clinically causes hypochloremic, hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis. However, option B (metabolic acidosis) is highlighted in this curriculum answer key.

Exclusions:
A, C, D → Do not match the designated answer key choice for this specific question.
421. Symptoms and signs of extracellular fluid volume deficit include all of the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Anorexia
  • B. Apathy
  • C. Decreased body temperature
  • D. High pulse pressure
Diagnosis:
Severe volume depletion produces tachycardia and hypotension, resulting in a narrow (low) pulse pressure, making a high pulse pressure the correct exception.

Exclusions:
Symptoms (A, B, C) → Hypovolemia causes poor tissue perfusion, lethargy/apathy, reduced temperature, and anorexia.
422. A low urinary [NH4+] with a hyperchloremic acidosis indicates what cause?
  • A. Excessive vomiting
  • B. Enterocutaneous fistula
  • C. Chronic diarrhea
  • D. Renal tubular acidosis
Diagnosis:
Renal tubular acidosis (RTA) is characterized by hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis with an inability of the kidney to secrete acid, manifesting as low urinary ammonium excretion.

Exclusions:
Diarrhea (C) → GI bicarbonate losses cause hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis, but normal kidneys respond by highly increasing ammonium excretion.
423. When lactic acid is produced in response to injury, the body minimizes pH change by:
  • A. Decreasing production of sodium bicarbonate in tissues
  • B. Excreting carbon dioxide through the lungs
  • C. Excreting lactic acid through the kidneys
  • D. Metabolizing the lactic acid in the liver
Diagnosis:
The bicarbonate buffer system reacts to excess hydrogen ions by producing carbonic acid, which is rapidly blown off as CO2 via hyperventilation (respiratory compensation).

Exclusions:
Renal/Liver (C, D) → While renal excretion and hepatic metabolism help clear lactate, hyperventilation is the immediate mechanism to minimize acute pH drops.
424. What is the best determinant of whether a patient has a metabolic acidosis versus alkalosis?
  • A. Arterial pH
  • B. Serum bicarbonate
  • C. Pco2
  • D. Serum CO2 level
Diagnosis:
Arterial pH is the primary parameter used to define the overall acidemic or alkalemic state of the body.

Exclusions:
Bicarbonate (B) → While reflective of metabolic status, a normal or low bicarbonate alone does not determine the final pH due to potential respiratory compensations.
425. If a patient’s arterial Pco2 is found to be 25 mm Hg, the arterial pH will be approximately:
  • A. 7.52
  • B. 7.40
  • d. 7.32
  • C. 7.28
Diagnosis:
A Pco2 of 25 mmHg alone represents respiratory alkalosis (pH > 7.4). However, in this question-set, the designated correct answer is 7.28 (indicative of a mixed metabolic acidosis with respiratory compensation).

Exclusions:
A, B, D → Do not match the curriculum answer key.
426. Which of the following are NOT characteristic findings of acute renal failure?
  • A. BUN > 100 mg/dL
  • B. Hypokalemia
  • C. Severe acidosis
  • D. Uremic pericarditis
  • E. Uremic encephalopathy
Diagnosis:
BUN levels do not typically rise above 100 mg/dL in acute renal failure unless it is long-standing or associated with highly high catabolic states, making it the designated correct exception.

Exclusions:
Potassium/Acidosis (B, C) → Hypokalemia is also rare in ARF (hyperkalemia is typical), but BUN > 100 mg/dL is the chosen key.
427. An elderly diabetic patient who has acute cholecystitis is found to have a serum sodium level of 122 mEq/L and a blood glucose of 600 mg/dL. After correcting the glucose concentration to 100 mg/dL with insulin, the serum sodium concentration would:
  • A. Decrease significantly unless the patient also received 3% saline
  • B. Decrease transiently but return to approximately 122 mEq/L without specific therapy
  • C. Remain essentially unchanged
  • D. Increase to the normal range without specific therapy
Diagnosis:
The patient has hyperosmolality-induced pseudohyponatremia. Correcting hyperglycemia shifts water back into cells, raising the serum sodium concentration to normal without sodium therapy.

Exclusions:
Saline/Constant (A, B, C) → Active sodium correction is unnecessary and can lead to hypernatremia once the glucose load is cleared.
428. Excessive administration of normal saline for fluid resuscitation can lead to what metabolic derangement?
  • A. Metabolic alkalosis
  • B. Metabolic acidosis
  • C. Respiratory alkalosis
  • D. Respiratory acidosis
Diagnosis:
Large volumes of 0.9% NaCl lead to hyperchloremia, which causes bicarbonate excretion, producing a hyperchloremic metabolic acidosis.

Exclusions:
Alkalosis (A) → Saline reduces strong ion difference, lowering pH toward acidosis, not alkalosis.
429. The first step in the management of acute hypercalcemia should be:
  • A. Correction of deficit of extracellular fluid volume
  • B. Hemodialysis.
  • C. Administration of furosemide.
  • D. Administration of mithramycin.
Diagnosis:
Aggressive volume expansion with intravenous isotonic saline corrects hypovolemia and promotes renal excretion of calcium.

Exclusions:
Furosemide (C) → Loop diuretics are only given *after* the patient is fully volume-repleted.
430. A victim of a motor vehicle accident arrives in hemorrhagic shock. His arterial blood gases are pH, 7.25; Po2, 95 mm Hg; Pco2, 25 mm Hg; HCO3-, 15 mEq/L. The patient’s metabolic acidosis would be treated best with:
  • A. Ampule of sodium bicarbonate
  • B. Sodium bicarbonate infusion
  • C. Lactated Ringer solution
  • D. Hyperventilation
Diagnosis:
The acidosis is caused by lactic accumulation from tissue hypoperfusion. Restoring circulating volume and tissue perfusion with balanced crystalloids (like Lactated Ringer's) is the definitive treatment.

Exclusions:
Bicarbonate (A, B) → Sodium bicarbonate is contraindicated in acute lactic acidosis as it shifts the oxyhemoglobin curve and can cause intracellular acidosis.
431. Three days after surgery for gastric carcinoma, a 50-year-old alcoholic male exhibits delirium, muscle tremors, and hyperactive tendon reflexes. Magnesium deficiency is suspected. All of the following statements regarding this situation are true EXCEPT:
  • A. A decision to administer magnesium should be based on the serum magnesium level.
  • B. Adequate cellular replacement of magnesium will require 1 to 3 weeks.
  • C. A concomitant calcium deficiency should be suspected.
  • D. Calcium is a specific antagonist of the myocardial effects of magnesium.
Diagnosis:
Serum magnesium levels do not reliably correlate with total body magnesium stores, as magnesium is predominantly an intracellular cation.

Exclusions:
B, C, D → These are correct physiological statements regarding magnesium therapy, co-existing hypocalcemia, and calcium antagonism.
432. Refeeding syndrome can be associated with all of the following EXCEPT:
  • A. Respiratory failure
  • B. Hyperkalemia
  • C. Confusion
  • D. Cardiac arrhythmias
Diagnosis:
Refeeding syndrome is characterized by rapid intracellular shifts of electrolytes upon insulin surge, causing severe *hypokalemia*, hypophosphatemia, and hypomagnesemia, making hyperkalemia the correct exception.

Exclusions:
Arrhythmias/Failure (A, C, D) → Severe hypophosphatemia and hypokalemia cause muscular weakness, respiratory failure, and cardiac arrhythmias.
433. Regarding total body water all the following are true EXCEPT:
  • a. Females and obese persons have an increased percentage of body water.
  • b. Increased muscle mass is associated with increased total body water.
  • c. Newborn infants have the greatest proportion of total body water.
  • d. Total body water decreases steadily with age.
Diagnosis:
Adipose tissue contains very little water. Because females and obese individuals have a higher proportion of body fat, they have a *decreased* (not increased) percentage of total body water.

Exclusions:
B, C, D → Accurate physiological facts; infants are ~75% water, and muscle is water-rich compared to fat.
434. Regarding extracellular fluid all the following are true EXCEPT:
  • a- The total extracellular fluid volume represents 20% of the body weight.
  • b- The plasma volume constitutes 5% of body weight.
  • c- Potassium is the principal cation in extracellular fluid.
  • d- The interstitial fluid equilibrates rapidly with the other body compartments.
Diagnosis:
Sodium is the principal extracellular cation, while potassium is the principal intracellular cation, making this statement the correct exception.

Exclusions:
A, B, D → True physiological values (ECF is 20% body weight, of which plasma is 5%).
435. Total body water constitutes what percentage of adult body weight:
  • A. 40
  • B. 50
  • C. 60
  • D. 70
Diagnosis:
Water constitutes approximately 60% of total body weight in a normal-weight adult male.

Exclusions:
A, B, D → Do not represent the standard physiological average.
436. Insensible loss of fluid from skin and lungs for 24 hours is normally in the span of:
  • A. 100 – 250 ml.
  • B. 250 – 500 ml.
  • C. 500 – 750 ml.
  • D. 750 – 1000 ml.
Diagnosis:
In normal adults, baseline daily insensible fluid loss (evaporation from respiratory tract and skin) totals approximately 750 to 1000 mL.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Underestimate typical physiological losses.
437. Which of the following diagnoses would be most likely in a patient who presents with normovolemic hyponatremia?
  • a. SIADH
  • b. High output renal failure
  • c. Water toxicity
  • d. GI losses
Diagnosis:
Syndrome of Inappropriate Antidiuretic Hormone (SIADH) leads to isolated water retention, causing a classic euvolemic (normovolemic) hyponatremia.

Exclusions:
GI losses (D) → Typically cause hypovolemic hyponatremia due to concurrent volume depletion and sodium losses.
438. Which of the following can contribute to hyperkalemia in patients with renal insufficiency?
  • (A) Loop diuretics
  • (B) Aspirin
  • (C) Calcium channel blockers
  • (D) NSAIDs
Diagnosis:
NSAIDs inhibit prostaglandin synthesis, which decreases renin and aldosterone release (causing hyporeninemic hypoaldosteronism) and impairs renal potassium excretion.

Exclusions:
Loop diuretics (A) → Promote renal potassium wasting, lowering potassium levels.
439. Which of the following would cause decreased deep tendon reflexes?
  • (A) Hypokalemia
  • (B) Hypomagnesemia
  • (C) Hypocalcemia
  • (D) Hypoglycemia
Diagnosis:
Hypokalemia decreases neuromuscular excitability, which can lead to muscle weakness and decreased or absent deep tendon reflexes.

Exclusions:
Hypomagnesemia/Hypocalcemia (B, C) → Hypocalcemia and hypomagnesemia typically increase neuromuscular excitability, causing hyperreflexia and tetany.
440. Which of the following is an early ECG change seen in hyperkalemia?
  • (A) Prolonged PR interval
  • (B) Sine wave formation
  • (C) Peaked T waves
  • (D) Flattened P wave
Diagnosis:
Symmetrical peaked T waves are the earliest electrocardiographic sign of hyperkalemia.

Exclusions:
PR/P wave (A, D) & Sine wave (B) → These are later changes seen as potassium levels continue to rise.
441. A postoperative patient with potassium of 2.9 is given 1 mEq/kg replacement with KCl. Repeat tests after the replacement show the serum K to be 3.0. The most likely diagnosis is:
  • (A) Hypomagnesemia
  • (B) Hypocalcemia
  • (C) Metabolic acidosis
  • (D) Metabolic alkalosis
Diagnosis:
Refractory hypokalemia is a classic hallmark of concurrent hypomagnesemia, as magnesium is a necessary cofactor for potassium conservation in the kidneys.

Exclusions:
Calcium/Acid-base (B, C, D) → Do not directly prevent intracellular or renal retention of potassium.
442. Which of the following is a cause of acute hypophosphatemia?
  • (A) Chronic ingestion of magnesium containing laxatives
  • (B) Insulin coma
  • (C) Refeeding syndrome
  • (D) Rhabdomyolysis
Diagnosis:
Refeeding syndrome drives rapid, insulin-mediated shift of phosphate into cells for ATP synthesis, causing severe acute hypophosphatemia.

Exclusions:
Rhabdomyolysis (D) → Causes cell lysis, which releases intracellular phosphorus, producing hyperphosphatemia.
443. Which of the following should be the first treatment administrated to a patient with potassium level of 6.3 and flattened P wave on his ECG?
  • (A) Kayexalate
  • (B) Insulin and glucose
  • (C) Calcium gluconate
  • (D) Inhaled albuterol
Diagnosis:
While calcium gluconate is clinically indicated to stabilize cardiac membranes when ECG changes occur, the designated answer in this key is insulin and glucose.

Exclusions:
Kayexalate (A) → Works too slowly to be considered first-line during acute ECG changes.
444. Insensible water loss from lungs is:
  • A. 200 ml.
  • B. 500 ml.
  • c. 1000 ml.
  • d. 1500 ml.
Diagnosis:
Of the total daily insensible fluid loss, approximately 500 mL is lost via expiration through the lungs.

Exclusions:
A, C, D → Under- or overestimate typical respiratory fluid losses.
445. The extracellular fluid differs from the intracellular fluid in all of the following EXCEPT:
  • a. Protein content.
  • b. Electrolyte composition.
  • c. Volume.
  • d. Osmolarity (tonicity).
Diagnosis:
Because cell membranes are freely permeable to water, water shifts until osmotic equilibrium is established, making osmolarity equal between ECF and ICF.

Exclusions:
Volume/Electrolytes (A, B, C) → Highly different (ECF is smaller, sodium-dominated; ICF is larger, potassium-dominated).
446. Concerning the sodium ion, which statement is untrue among the following?
  • a. Diffuses readily through cell membranes.
  • b. Is the major cation of the extracellular fluid.
  • c. Is the chief regulator of body water.
  • d. Its urinary excretion is reduced after trauma.
Diagnosis:
Sodium is highly restricted to extracellular spaces and does not diffuse readily through cellular lipid bilayers (it requires active transport or specific channels), making this the untrue statement.

Exclusions:
True facts (B, C, D) → Correctly describe sodium’s physiological properties and post-traumatic renal conservation.
447. Concerning potassium, which statement is untrue?
  • a. Is the major cation of the intracellular fluid.
  • b. Its intracellular concentration is about 150 mEq/L.
  • c. Its average daily need is about 60 mEq/L.
  • d. Is well-conserved by the kidney after trauma.
Diagnosis:
Unlike sodium, potassium is poorly conserved by the kidneys, and post-traumatic stress/aldosterone release actually promotes renal wasting, making this untrue.

Exclusions:
True facts (A, B, C) → Accurately describe potassium's distribution and typical dietary requirement.
448. Potassium deficiency is present, if the plasma-potassium level is less than:
  • a. 5 mmol/l
  • b. 4.5 mmol/l.
  • c. 4 mmol/l.
  • d. 3.5 mmol/l.
Diagnosis:
Hypokalemia (potassium deficiency) is defined as a serum or plasma potassium level below the normal threshold of 3.5 mmol/L.

Exclusions:
A, B, C → Fall within the normal physiological range (3.5–5.0 mmol/L).
449. Concerning potassium depletion, the incorrect statement among the following is:
  • a. Follows loss of gastrointestinal secretions.
  • b. May be produced by diuretics.
  • c. Is often associated with acidosis.
  • d. Predisposes to cardiac arrhythmias.
Diagnosis:
Hypokalemia (potassium depletion) is classically associated with alkalosis (which shifts K+ intracellularly), whereas acidosis is typically associated with hyperkalemia, making this the incorrect statement.

Exclusions:
A, B, D → True statements; GI loss and loop/thiazide diuretics cause hypokalemia, which can precipitate cardiac arrhythmias.
450. Symptoms of hyperkalemia are reflected in which organ system:
  • b. Respiratory.
  • b. Gastrointestinal.
  • c. CVS.
  • d. CNS.
Diagnosis:
Hyperkalemia directly alters the cardiac action potential, making its primary, most dangerous clinical manifestations cardiovascular (CVS).

Exclusions:
A, B, D → Although neuromuscular weakness can occur, hyperkalemic cardiotoxicity is the hallmark critical risk.
451. Hypokalaemia causes:
  • a. Peaked P waves.
  • b. Adynamic ileus.
  • c. Hyper-reflexia.
  • d. All of the above.
Diagnosis:
Hypokalemia hyperpolarizes cell membranes, causing muscle weakness and paralysis of smooth muscle in the gut wall, leading to adynamic (paralytic) ileus.

Exclusions:
Reflexes/P waves (A, C) → Hypokalemia causes hyporeflexia (muscle weakness) and flat P waves (not peaked).
452. In health the pH of the blood lies between the ranges:
  • a. 7.05 - 7.19.
  • b. 7.20 - 7.35.
  • c. 7.36 - 7.44.
  • d. 7.45 - 7.59.
Diagnosis:
Normal arterial blood pH is tightly regulated within the narrow physiological range of 7.35 to 7.45 (or 7.36–7.44).

Exclusions:
A, B, D → Fall into acidemic or alkalemic pathological ranges.
453. Which of the following contributes maximum to body fluid osmolality:
  • a. Glucose.
  • b. Sodium.
  • c. Chloride.
  • d. Bicarbonate.
Diagnosis:
Sodium and its associated anions (primarily chloride) represent the vast majority of the osmotic pressure of extracellular fluid (determined as $2 \times \text{Na} \approx 280$ mOsm/kg).

Exclusions:
Glucose (A) → Under normal conditions, glucose contributes less than 5–10 mOsm/kg to overall osmolality.
454. The standard bicarbonate level in the plasma is normally:
  • a. 12 - 15 mmol/l.
  • b. 16 - 21 mmol/1.
  • c. 22 - 25 mmol/1.
  • d. 26 - 31 mmol/l.
Diagnosis:
The normal reference range for arterial bicarbonate in healthy adults is approximately 22 to 26 mmol/L.

Exclusions:
A, B, D → Represent hypocapnic/metabolic acidosis or severe alkalosis ranges.
455. Concerning metabolic acidosis, which of the following statements is untrue:
  • a. Occurs in diabetes, starvation, shock and anuria.
  • b. May be due to diarrhoea and intestinal fistulas.
  • c. Is often associated with air hunger.
  • d. Causes bradycardia and hypotension.
Diagnosis:
While severe acidosis eventually suppresses myocardial function (potentially causing hypotension), acute metabolic acidosis typically presents with compensatory tachycardia, making bradycardia the untrue statement.

Exclusions:
True facts (A, B, C) → Acidosis is commonly caused by DKA, tissue shock, renal failure, and severe diarrhea, and is classically accompanied by Kussmaul respirations ("air hunger").
456. The ideal infusion fluid for correction of hypokalemic alkalosis due to pyloric obstruction is:
  • a. Normal saline.
  • b. Potassium chloride in 5% glucose.
  • c. Ringer's solution.
  • d. Ammonium chloride.
Diagnosis:
Pyloric obstruction causes loss of HCl and water, resulting in hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis. 0.9% Normal Saline is the ideal fluid as its high chloride concentration (154 mEq/L) directly corrects the chloride deficit.

Exclusions:
Ammonium chloride (D) → Rarely used and can be toxic; isotonic saline is safer and highly effective.
457. All of the following components or qualities of stored whole blood tend to decrease over time EXCEPT:
  • a- Red blood cell survival time.
  • b- Potassium concentration.
  • c- Platelet activity.
  • d- Oxygen-carrying capacity.
Diagnosis:
As stored red blood cells age, they slowly leak potassium into the plasma. Consequently, potassium concentration *increases* (rather than decreases) over time.

Exclusions:
Platelets/Oxygen (A, C, D) → Platelet activity, erythrocyte viability, and oxygen-carrying capacity (due to 2,3-DPG depletion) all decrease over time.
458. Allowing blood which is ready for transfusion to remain for four hours in a warm environment:
  • a. Reduces shock.
  • b. Increases shock.
  • c. Favors subsequent hepatitis.
  • d. Encourages bacterial proliferation and septicaemia.
Diagnosis:
Warm temperatures promote rapid growth of any contaminating bacteria within the blood product, drastically increasing the risk of bacterial sepsis during transfusion.

Exclusions:
Hepatitis (C) → Viral transmission is related to donor screening, not storage temperature.
459. The following are known to cause a metabolic acidosis EXCEPT:
  • a. ischaemic bowel
  • b. diabetic ketoacidosis
  • c. sepsis
  • d. persistent vomiting
Diagnosis:
Persistent vomiting causes loss of hydrochloric acid from the stomach, which produces a metabolic alkalosis, making it the correct exception.

Exclusions:
Acidosis (A, B, C) → Ischemic bowel and sepsis cause lactic acidosis, while DKA produces acetoacetic and beta-hydroxybutyric acidosis.
460. A 25-year-old female is postoperative day 1 from a laparoscopic, converted to open cholecystectomy for acute cholecystitis. Since surgery, she has had one episode of emesis, urinary output has decreased to 0.3 cc/kg per hour, and serum sodium is found to be 131 mEq/L. Serum creatinine is normal, but antidiuretic hormone (ADH) level is elevated. What is the most likely cause of these findings?
  • A. Syndrome of inappropriate antidiuretic hormone secretion (SIADH)
  • B. Normal physiologic response to surgery
  • C. Acute kidney injury
  • D. Emesis
  • E. Congestive heart failure
Diagnosis:
Surgery, pain, and stress trigger a physiological non-osmotic release of ADH and aldosterone. This leads to mild, transient postoperative oliguria and water retention (mild hyponatremia) on postoperative day 1.

Exclusions:
SIADH (A) → SIADH is a pathological diagnosis of exclusion and should not be diagnosed in the acute, expected post-surgical stress window.
461. A 65-year-old male with massive intracranial hemorrhage after a ruptured berry aneurysm is currently in the neurosurgical intensive care unit (ICU). Two days ago, he underwent intravascular coiling of the lesion. Because of increased urinary output over the last 24 hours, a urine sodium was measured and found to be 35 mEq/L. Current labs include a serum sodium of 128 mEq/L and a hemoglobin of 18 g/dL. Central venous pressure is 2 mm Hg. Which of the following is the most appropriate initial treatment?
  • A. Normal saline
  • B. Free water restriction
  • C. Desmopressin
  • D. Demeclocycline
  • E. Tolvaptan
Diagnosis:
The patient has cerebral salt wasting (CSW), characterized by volume depletion (low CVP, high Hb) with high urinary sodium losses. This is managed primarily with volume and sodium replacement using normal saline.

Exclusions:
SIADH drugs (B, C, D, E) → Fluid restriction or ADH antagonists are contraindicated in CSW, as they would further worsen the patient's critical hypovolemia.
462. A 75-year-old male is in the ICU due to sepsis 5 days after a colectomy for a perforated diverticulitis. While the nurse is checking his blood pressure, his hand went into a spasm. Which of the following is the most likely etiology?
  • A. Hypercalcemia
  • B. Hypermagnesemia
  • C. Hypomagnesemia
  • D. Hyponatremia
  • E. Hyperkalemia
Diagnosis:
Trousseau's sign (carpopedal spasm) can occur with both hypocalcemia and hypomagnesemia. In the ICU setting, severe sepsis or long-term malnourishment commonly leads to symptomatic hypomagnesemia, which was the designated key for this question.

Exclusions:
Hypermagnesemia (B) → Decreases neuromuscular excitability, leading to flaccid paralysis and hyporeflexia.
463. The most effective method for the prevention of contrast-induced nephropathy (CIN) from computed tomography with intravenous contrast is:
  • A. Intravenous N-acetylcysteine
  • B. Normal saline bolus immediately before the exam followed by 6 hours of sodium bicarbonate and normal saline after the exam
  • C. 12 hours of intravenous normal saline before and after the exam
  • D. Intravenous fluids with sodium bicarbonate 2 hours before exam and 12 hours after, as well as N acetylcysteine
Diagnosis:
Proactive volume expansion with intravenous isotonic saline (1 mL/kg/h) for 12 hours before and 12 hours after contrast exposure remains the most effective, clinically proven method to prevent CIN.

Exclusions:
NAC (A) → Clinical trials have demonstrated inconsistent or negligible benefits for oral or IV N-acetylcysteine without adequate hydration.
464. Which of the following causes of acidosis in the intensive care unit is best treated by administration of sodium bicarbonate?
  • A. Lactic acidosis
  • B. Diabetic ketoacidosis
  • C. Respiratory acidosis from acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS)
  • D. Cardiac arrest
  • E. Renal tubular acidosis (RTA)
Diagnosis:
Renal tubular acidosis (RTA) represents a primary, hyperchloremic bicarbonate wasting state where the kidneys cannot retain HCO3-. This is directly treated with oral or IV sodium bicarbonate replacement.

Exclusions:
Lactic/DKA (A, B) → Bicarbonate is avoided in lactic acidosis or DKA as it does not address the underlying etiology and can cause paradoxical tissue intracellular acidosis.
465. A 76-year-old female is postoperative day 4 from sigmoid colon resection. Her postoperative course has been uneventful, but she has not yet started passing flatus. Overnight, the urinary output has decreased to 20 cc/hour, and the patient has had several episodes of emesis. Lab work includes a blood urea nitrogen (BUN) of 40 mg/dL and serum creatinine of 1.5 mg/dL. Urinary sodium is 10 mEq/L. What is the most likely etiology of oliguria in this patient?
  • A. Postoperative ileus
  • B. Intra-abdominal hemorrhage
  • C. Intraoperative hypotension
  • D. Inadvertent ligation of the left ureter
  • E. Drug-induced nephrotoxicity
Diagnosis:
The patient is in postoperative ileus (indicated by POD 4 without flatus and persistent emesis). The resulting fluid loss from vomiting has led to pre-renal oliguria (indicated by a low urinary sodium of 10 mEq/L and high BUN-to-creatinine ratio).

Exclusions:
Ureter ligation (D) → Typically asymptomatic if unilateral and has normal contralateral function; does not present with a low urinary sodium concentration.
466. Which of the following is true regarding dehydration and/or hypovolemia in children?
  • A. Children only need to lose 5% of total body water to produce significant symptoms of hypovolemia.
  • B. Hypovolemia refers to a reduction in free water.
  • C. Dehydration will primarily result in extracellular fluid losses.
  • D. Profound hypernatremic hypovolemia should be corrected initially with hypotonic fluids.
  • E. Oral fluid replacement is adequate in most children with insensible fluid losses.
Diagnosis:
For children with mild to moderate dehydration, oral rehydration therapy (ORT) is the preferred, safe, and effective initial intervention.

Exclusions:
Free water (B) → Hypovolemia refers to a loss of extracellular fluid volume, whereas dehydration refers to a loss of intracellular/free water.
467. Which of the following is considered a normal physiologic change in pregnancy?
  • A. Decrease in blood pH
  • B. Decrease in minute ventilation
  • C. Increased vital capacity
  • D. Right-shift of oxyhemoglobin dissociation curve
  • E. Relative leukopenia
Diagnosis:
In pregnancy, maternal red blood cells develop increased 2,3-DPG levels, shifting the oxygen dissociation curve to the right to facilitate oxygen release to the fetus.

Exclusions:
Ventilation (B) → Progesterone stimulates respiratory drive, causing hyperventilation (increased minute ventilation) and mild respiratory alkalosis.
468. Which of the following is true regarding sodium and water maintenance in the geriatric patient?
  • A. There is an increase in the ratio of intracellular to extracellular water.
  • B. A hyperactive thirst response predisposes geriatric patients to hyponatremia.
  • C. Elevated antidiuretic hormone levels predispose patients to sodium retention.
  • D. Atrial natriuretic peptide level increases with aging.
  • E. There is a relative increase in the activity of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system.
Diagnosis:
Plasma levels of Atrial Natriuretic Peptide (ANP) rise as part of the aging process, which may impair renal sodium conservation.

Exclusions:
Thirst (B) → Geriatric patients actually have a blunted (hypoactive) thirst response, which predisposes them to dehydration and hypernatremia.
469. An elderly patient presents to the emergency department (ED) with increased thirst and urinary output. Which of the following findings would be most helpful to suggest diabetes insipidus (DI) as the likely etiology in this patient?
  • A. Hypernatremia
  • B. Hyperglycemia
  • C. Hyponatremia
  • D. Low urine osmolality
  • E. High serum-to-urine osmolality ratio
Diagnosis:
A high serum-to-urine osmolality ratio (concentrated blood paired with dilute urine) confirms the renal concentrating defect that characterizes diabetes insipidus.

Exclusions:
Hyperglycemia (B) → Polyuria and thirst can also be caused by diabetic ketoacidosis or solute diuresis, but DI lacks hyperglycemia.
470. A 58-year-old male alcoholic presents to the ED complaining of increased abdominal girth over the last several weeks. He underwent a diagnostic ultrasound 1 year ago, which showed evidence of cirrhosis. Physical exam reveals pitting edema of the lower extremities and positive abdominal fluid wave. In addition to alcohol cessation, what is the next step in management?
  • A. Free water restriction
  • B. Transjugular intrahepatic portosystemic shunt
  • C. Intravenous furosemide with transition to PO once ascites resolves
  • D. Strict sodium restriction (< 1g per day)
  • E. Combination of oral furosemide and spironolactone
Diagnosis:
First-line medical therapy for cirrhotic ascites includes dietary sodium restriction combined with dual diuretic therapy (furosemide and spironolactone, maintaining a 40:100 ratio).

Exclusions:
Intravenous (C) → Rapid diuresis via IV loop diuretics can compromise intravascular volume, precipitating renal failure in stable ascites.
471. A 45-year-old male with congestive heart failure is being treated in the ICU for sepsis secondary to pneumonia. Over the last 24 hours, he has received 11 L of crystalloid and was started on vasopressors for hypotension. His urine output has dropped to 20 mL/hour. Delivered tidal volumes on the mechanical ventilator have also significantly decreased. Physical exam reveals a tense abdomen, abdominal fluid wave, and anasarca. Current bladder pressure is 25 mm Hg. The most appropriate initial management is:
  • A. Neuromuscular blockade
  • B. Immediate decompressive laparotomy
  • C. Percutaneous drainage of intra-abdominal fluid
  • D. Continuous renal replacement therapy
  • E. Change resuscitative fluid to albumin
Diagnosis:
The patient is experiencing secondary Abdominal Compartment Syndrome (ACS) with respiratory/renal failure and a bladder pressure > 20 mmHg. Percutaneous drainage of ascites is highly effective to decompress the abdomen without requiring surgery.

Exclusions:
Laparotomy (B) → Only indicated if percutaneous decompression or conservative measures fail to stabilize the patient's physiology.
472. Which of the following is true regarding lactated Ringer’s solution when used as an intravenous fluid?
  • A. It is equally suitable as a maintenance or resuscitative fluid.
  • B. It is useful in acidosis because of the bicarbonate content of the solution.
  • C. It is an aqueous solution composed of only sodium chloride, sodium lactate, calcium chloride, and potassium chloride salts.
  • D. The pH of the solution itself is 7.5.
  • E. Sodium content is equivalent to normal serum.
Diagnosis:
Lactated Ringer's is a balanced crystalloid solution composed of sodium chloride, sodium lactate (which is metabolized to bicarbonate), calcium chloride, and potassium chloride.

Exclusions:
Bicarbonate (B) → It contains lactate, which must be metabolized in the liver to generate bicarbonate, meaning it has no direct bicarbonate content.
473. Which of the following is true regarding serum osmolarity and serum osmolality?
  • A. Large proteins, like albumin, are the most important contributors to serum osmolality.
  • B. The presence of an osmolar gap indicates the presence of a foreign molecule that readily distributes across cell membranes.
  • C. The difference between serum osmolarity and serum osmolality is highly variable depending on the physiologic state.
  • D. Sodium is multiplied by two in the calculation for serum osmolarity because of its increased osmotic activity.
  • E. The number of molecules, and not the size, is the most important contributor to serum osmolarity.
Diagnosis:
Osmolality and osmolarity are colligative properties; they are solely dependent on the concentration (number) of dissolved particles, regardless of their molecular size or weight.

Exclusions:
Proteins (A) → Albumin is a massive molecule that has minor osmotic impact compared to smaller molecules like sodium and chloride.
474. A 62-year-old female was recently diagnosed on upper endoscopy with a near obstructing distal gastric tumor but was subsequently lost to follow-up. She now returns to the ED with 24 hours of nonbilious vomiting and abdominal pain. What is the most significant contributing factor to hypokalemia in this patient?
  • A. Intracellular shift
  • B. Increased excretion in the urine
  • C. Loss of potassium with emesis
  • D. Metabolic acidosis
  • E. Hypokalemic fluid replacement
Diagnosis:
Gastric vomiting causes loss of HCl, producing metabolic alkalosis. This stimulates aldosterone activation, prompting the kidneys to excrete massive amounts of potassium in exchange for sodium conservation.

Exclusions:
Direct emesis loss (C) → Gastric fluid contains only ~10 mmol/L of potassium; thus, renal wasting is the dominant pathway of potassium depletion.
475. Which of the following is true regarding commonly used resuscitative or maintenance fluids?
  • A. Normal saline is preferred over lactated Ringer’s solution in burn patients.
  • B. Five percent albumin solution draws fluid from the extravascular space by increasing colloid oncotic pressure.
  • C. Five percent dextrose in water is iso-osmolar to serum.
  • D. Lactated Ringer’s solution will worsen an existing lactic acidosis.
  • E. In order to replace lost intravascular volume, twice the volume of crystalloid must be infused.
Diagnosis:
D5W has an in vitro osmolality of ~252–278 mOsm/kg, which is roughly isotonic/iso-osmolar to serum before dextrose is metabolized.

Exclusions:
Lactated Ringer (D) → LR contains sodium lactate, not lactic acid, and does not exacerbate systemic lactic acidosis.
476. A 48-year-old male with past medical history of alcoholic cirrhosis and refractory ascites is admitted to the ICU recovering from spontaneous bacterial peritonitis (SBP). He is now off of antibiotics, and there is no evidence of continued infection. Over the course of his hospitalization, his creatinine level increased from 1.0 to 1.6 mg/dL. Urinalysis reveals no evidence of proteinuria or microhematuria. Which of the following is the initial step in management?
  • A. Fluid resuscitation with normal saline
  • B. Cessation of diuretics
  • C. Terlipressin and albumin
  • D. Initiation of continuous renal replacement therapy
  • E. Transjugular intrahepatic portosystemic shunt (TIPS)
Diagnosis:
This presentation is concerning for Hepatorenal Syndrome (HRS). The first critical step to rule out pre-renal acute kidney injury is stopping all diuretic therapy and rehydrating with albumin.

Exclusions:
TIPS (E) → TIPS is reserved for refractory cases that do not respond to medical volume expansion and vasopressor therapies.
477. A 24-year-old female underwent a jejunal resection complicated by abdominal compartment syndrome and an open abdomen after a motor vehicle collision. She is eventually discharged home but returns 1 week later with copious output of yellowish fluid from her midline wound. She has noted diminished urinary output, is tachycardic, and has decreased skin turgor. What combination of electrolyte abnormalities is most likely present in this patient?
  • A. Hyponatremia, hypokalemia, and metabolic acidosis
  • B. Hypokalemia, hypochloremia, and metabolic alkalosis
  • C. Hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, and metabolic acidosis
  • D. Hypernatremia and metabolic acidosis
  • E. Hyperkalemia and metabolic alkalosis
Diagnosis:
The patient has a high-output enterocutaneous fistula. This results in the loss of sodium, potassium, and bicarbonate-rich jejunal fluids, causing hyponatremia, hypokalemia, and metabolic acidosis.

Exclusions:
B, E → Intestinal fistulas secrete bicarbonate, producing metabolic acidosis, not alkalosis.
478. A 55-year-old male is admitted to the hospital with altered mental status. Paramedics report that they found multiple empty beer cans in his home. He is found to have a serum sodium concentration of 118 mEq/L. Fluid resuscitation is initiated with normal saline and sodium levels return to normal by the next morning. On hospital day 5, he develops spastic quadriplegia and is unresponsive to external stimuli. Which of the following is true regarding this condition?
  • A. It could have been prevented with the use of hypertonic saline.
  • B. Desmopressin can be used as an adjunct to fluid replacement to prevent this complication.
  • C. Cerebral adaptions to hyponatremia take up to a week to develop.
  • D. Recovery is impossible after the onset of neurologic symptoms.
  • E. Injury is restricted to the pons.
Diagnosis:
The patient has developed Central Pontine Myelinolysis (Osmotic Demyelination Syndrome) due to overly rapid correction of chronic hyponatremia. Desmopressin (DDAVP) is used as an adjunctive therapy to limit water excretion and prevent over-correction.

Exclusions:
Pons (E) → Demyelination can also affect extra-pontine structures, such as the basal ganglia and internal capsule.
479. A 50-year-old, type I diabetic male is admitted to the hospital for the workup of vague abdominal pain and subjective fevers. Past medical history includes total proctocolectomy with ileostomy for ulcerative colitis. Routine laboratory values include pH 7.26, pCO2 24 mm Hg, pO2 100 mm Hg, sodium 129 mEq/L, potassium 2.9 mEq/L, chloride 110 mEq/L, and bicarbonate 12 mEq/L. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?
  • A. Excessive ileostomy output
  • B. Kidney failure
  • C. Diabetic ketoacidosis
  • D. Lactic acidosis
  • E. Methanol intoxication
Diagnosis:
The patient has a normal anion gap metabolic acidosis [$\text{AG} = 129 - (110 + 12) = 7$] with hypokalemia (2.9 mEq/L). High-volume bicarbonate-rich loss from ileostomy output perfectly matches this clinical picture.

Exclusions:
DKA (C) & Lactic/Methanol (D, E) → All of these would present with a elevated calculated anion gap.
480. A 16-year-old girl arrives via ambulance after the family became concerned that she was behaving strangely. She appears disoriented and will answer simple questions but is evasive in answering questions about events leading up to her arrival. Vital signs are normal except for a respiratory rate of 7 and a body mass index (BMI) of 16. Arterial blood gas and basic metabolic panel are consistent with a metabolic alkalosis. Which of the following tests will be most helpful in establishing a diagnosis?
  • A. Urine drug screen
  • B. Computed tomography of the brain
  • C. Spot urine chloride concentration
  • D. Electrocardiogram (ECG)
  • E. Abdominal ultrasound
Diagnosis:
The patient has bulimia (eating disorder suggested by low BMI and evasiveness) and purging-induced hypochloremic metabolic alkalosis. A spot urine chloride concentration is crucial to differentiate vomiting-induced (low urine chloride <10 mEq/L) from diuretic abuse (high urine chloride).

Exclusions:
CT/Imaging (B, E) → Are not helpful for establishing metabolic etiologies.
481. After cardiac arrest and successful return of spontaneous circulation in a 63-year-old male, mild therapeutic hypothermia (MTH) is initiated. Which of the following is true regarding the physiologic changes associated with this treatment?
  • A. Central venous pressure will decrease.
  • B. During rewarming, hypokalemia can be problematic.
  • C. Acidosis and elevated lactate are indicative of treatment failure.
  • D. Hypothermia induces diuresis by the kidneys.
  • E. It is frequently deleterious on cardiac function.
Diagnosis:
Therapeutic hypothermia induces cold diuresis. Peripheral vasoconstriction shifts blood centrally, inhibiting ADH secretion and predisposing patients to volume depletion.

Exclusions:
Rewarming (B) → Rewarming causes potassium to shift *out* of cells, potentially precipitating dangerous *hyperkalemia* (not hypokalemia).
482. A 57-year-old female with end-stage renal disease on peritoneal dialysis is postoperative day 1 from a small bowel resection. A basic metabolic panel reveals a potassium level of 6.3 mEq/L. An electrocardiogram is obtained that shows no significant change from baseline. The resident administers 1 g of calcium gluconate, 50 mL of 50% dextrose in water, and 10 units of intravenous insulin and delivers a nebulized albuterol treatment. The patient then undergoes urgent hemodialysis. Six hours later, the potassium level is found to be 7.0 mEq/L. Which of the following is true?
  • A. Monotherapy with calcium gluconate was likely adequate.
  • B. Sodium polystyrene sulfonate (Kayexalate) in sorbitol should have been administered.
  • C. Sodium bicarbonate should have been included in the treatment regimen.
  • D. Calcium gluconate, dextrose, insulin, and albuterol were not indicated.
  • E. Peritoneal dialysis would have provided superior clearance of potassium.
Diagnosis:
In patients with end-stage renal disease and a potassium level of 6.3 mEq/L with no electrocardiographic changes, immediate hemodialysis is the definitive treatment. Medical therapy (calcium gluconate, insulin/dextrose, and albuterol) is transient, transiently shifts potassium, and can delay or complicate definitive management without clear indication, particularly in the absence of cardiotoxicity.

Exclusions:
Kayexalate (B) → Sorbitol-containing formulations carry a major risk of intestinal necrosis, especially in postoperative bowel surgery patients.

Section 12: Salivary Gland (Q483–Q491)

483. About anatomy of the submandibular salivary gland, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. The mylohyoid muscle divides the gland into superficial and deep parts.
  • B. The submandibular duct arises from the superficial part of the gland.
  • C. The deep part of the gland is related to the hypoglossal nerve.
  • D. The submandibular duct is closely related to the lingual nerve.
Anatomy:
  • Wharton's duct (submandibular duct) → Arises directly from the deep part of the gland and travels forward under the oral mucosa.
Exclusions:
  • Mylohyoid muscle (A) → Correctly divides the gland into superficial and deep lobes.
  • Hypoglossal nerve relation (C) → Deep part lies adjacent to the hypoglossal nerve.
  • Lingual nerve loop (D) → Wharton's duct runs in close proximity and is looped under by the lingual nerve.
484. A ranula is a:
  • A. Cystic swelling in the floor of the mouth.
  • B. Forked uvula.
  • C. Sublingual thyroid.
  • D. Thyroglossal cyst.
Diagnosis:
  • Ranula → Blueish translucent extravasation mucocele occurring in the floor of the mouth, classically originating from the sublingual salivary gland.
Exclusions:
  • Forked uvula (B) → Minor anatomical cleft of the soft palate.
  • Sublingual thyroid (C) → Ectopic thyroid tissue located at the base of the tongue.
  • Thyroglossal cyst (D) → Midline congenital neck mass, not a sublingual oral lesion.
485. The commonest discrete lump of the parotid gland is:
  • A. Pleomorphic adenoma.
  • B. Mucoepidermoid carcinoma.
  • C. Mumps.
  • D. Parotid gland stone.
Diagnosis:
  • Pleomorphic adenoma (Benign Mixed Tumor) → Accounts for ~80% of all benign major salivary gland neoplasms, presenting as a slow-growing, painless, discrete mass.
Exclusions:
  • Mucoepidermoid carcinoma (B) → Most common malignant salivary gland neoplasm.
  • Mumps (C) → Viral sialadenitis causing diffuse, painful bilateral parotid enlargement, not a discrete lump.
  • Parotid stone (D) → Sialolithiasis causes postprandial colic and diffuse swelling, rarely a painless discrete lump.
486. About submandibular salivary stones, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. The majority are radio-opaque.
  • B. A stone can be detected by CT scan.
  • C. A stone may present by a submandibular swelling that increases in size by eating.
  • D. A submandibular salivary swelling can be rolled over the angle of the mandible.
Diagnosis:
  • Anatomical confinement → The submandibular gland is located above and behind the mylohyoid muscle, sitting tightly inside the digastric triangle. It is fixed and cannot be rolled over the lower border or angle of the mandible.
Exclusions:
  • Radio-opacity (A) → ~80% of submandibular stones contain high calcium and are radio-opaque.
  • CT scans (B) → Highly sensitive for detecting salivary calcifications.
  • Postprandial colic (C) → Salivary duct obstruction causes salivary accumulation and acute swelling during mastication/eating.
487. The possible complications of submandibular sialadenectomy include all the following, except:
  • A. Wound haematoma.
  • B. Deviation of the tongue to the operation side when protruded.
  • C. Drooping of the angle of the mouth on the side of surgery.
  • D. Inability to close the eyelids on the side of the operation.
Anatomy & Complications:
  • Eyelid weakness (Inability to close) → Indicates temporal or zygomatic facial nerve branch injury, which are at risk in parotidectomy, not submandibular surgery.
Exclusions:
  • Wound hematoma (A) → Common localized surgical site complication.
  • Hypoglossal nerve injury (B) → Causes tongue deviation to the injured (ipsilateral) side.
  • Marginal mandibular nerve injury (C) → Causes drooping of the lower lip/angle of the mouth.
488. About pleomorphic adenoma of the parotid gland, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. It has epithelial and mesenchymal components.
  • B. The tumour is painless.
  • C. It has an incomplete capsule.
  • D. The tumour is usually present deep to the facial nerve.
Diagnosis:
  • Surgical anatomy → ~90% of pleomorphic adenomas arise in the superficial lobe of the parotid gland, placing them superficial to the facial nerve branches.
Exclusions:
  • Epithelial/mesenchymal (A) → Dual histological features ("mixed tumor") with epithelial cells and myxoid/chondroid matrix.
  • Painless nature (B) → Typically slow-growing and painless.
  • Incomplete pseudocapsule (C) → Predisposes to recurrence if treated by simple enucleation rather than superficial parotidectomy.
489. About adenolymphoma, all the following statements are true, except:
  • A. The tumour is related to smoking.
  • B. It may be bilateral.
  • C. It has epithelial and lymphoid components.
  • D. Facial nerve palsy is characteristic of this tumour.
Diagnosis:
  • Benign pathology → Adenolymphoma (Warthin's tumor) is entirely benign. Facial nerve palsy is a key clinical indicator of malignant parotid tumors.
Exclusions:
  • Smoking correlation (A) → Strongly associated with male smokers.
  • Bilaterality (B) → Is bilateral or multicentric in up to 10% of cases.
  • Histology (C) → Composed of a double layer of oncocytic epithelial cells surrounding a dense lymphoid stroma.
490. Locally invasive tumors are all EXCEPT:
  • a- Basal cell cancer.
  • b- Melanoma.
  • c- Mixed salivary tumours.
  • d- Bronchial adenoma.
Diagnosis:
  • Melanoma → Renowned for highly early hematogenous and lymphatic metastasis, making it primarily a systemic malignancy rather than a slow, locally invasive lesion.
Exclusions:
  • Basal cell carcinoma (a) → Classic locally invasive lesion ("rodent ulcer") that almost never metastasizes.
  • Mixed salivary tumors (c) & Bronchial adenomas (d) → Possess local micro-invasions and high local recurrences but are late to metastasize.
491. All of the following are FALSE about salivary gland neoplasms EXCEPT:
  • A. Account for less than 2% of all head and neck neoplasms
  • B. If in minor salivary glands, less likely to be malignant than if in the parotid gland
  • C. Computed tomography (CT) scanning is more accurate than magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) in detecting lesions
  • D. Oncocytomas are usually malignant
Epidemiology:
  • Infrequent neoplasms → Salivary gland tumors are relatively rare, representing less than 2% of all head and neck malignancies.
Exclusions:
  • Minor salivary gland malignancy (B) → Minor gland tumors have a high rate (~50-80%) of malignancy compared to only ~20% in the parotid.
  • MRI vs CT (C) → MRI is superior to CT for assessing soft tissue details and perineural invasion in salivary gland tumors.
  • Oncocytomas (D) → Generally benign salivary gland lesions.

Section 13: Muscles, Tendons & Fascia (Q492–Q530)

492. Which of the following is true concerning compartment syndrome?
  • A. Due to decreased intracompartmental pressure
  • B. Typified by hyperesthesia
  • C. Can be assessed by needles placed into affected compartment
  • D. Pain relieved by passive muscle stretching
Pathophysiology:
  • Diagnostic monitoring → Intracompartmental pressure can be measured directly using a needle-based pressure system (Stryker device) inserted into the compartment.
Exclusions:
  • Intracompartmental pressure (A) → Caused by increased, not decreased, pressure.
  • Sensory features (B) → Typically manifests as paresthesias or hypoesthesia (decreased sensation), not hyperesthesia.
  • Passive stretch (D) → Pain is classically *exacerbated* by passive muscle stretching, a hallmark sign.
493. The compartment most commonly affected in a lower leg compartment syndrome is the:
  • A. Anterior compartment
  • B. Lateral compartment
  • C. Deep posterior compartment
  • D. Superficial posterior compartment
Anatomy:
  • Anterior compartment → Most common site in the lower leg due to rigid fascial boundaries and vulnerability of contained structures (e.g., tibialis anterior).
Exclusions:
  • Lateral (B), Deep posterior (C), and Superficial posterior (D) → Lower risk than the anterior compartment.
494. An 18-year-old man develops a painful, swollen leg while training for the New York Marathon. There is tenderness in the calf and ecchymosis is present. What is the most likely diagnosis?
  • a) Cellulitis
  • b) DVT
  • c) Superficial thrombophlebitis
  • d) Tear of the plantaris muscle
Diagnosis:
  • Plantaris muscle rupture → Commonly called "tennis leg". Classic presentation is sudden calf pain/pop during running, with calf localized tenderness and localized ecchymosis from hemorrhage.
Exclusions:
  • Cellulitis (a) → Presents with warmth, spreading erythema, and systemic symptoms like fever.
  • DVT (b) → Swelling and calf pain but rarely presents with acute trauma-associated focal ecchymosis.
  • Superficial thrombophlebitis (c) → Presents with a localized, tender, red, palpable thrombosed venous cord.
495. A newborn girl with family history of lymphedema is noted to have bilateral lower extremity swelling. What is the diagnosis?
  • a) Lymphedema praecox
  • b) Milroy disease
  • c) Lymphedema tarda
  • d) Meigs’s syndrome
Diagnosis:
  • Milroy disease → Congenital, autosomal dominant primary lymphedema presenting at birth, strongly associated with VEGFR3 mutations and positive family history.
Exclusions:
  • Lymphedema praecox (a) → Primary lymphedema presenting around puberty.
  • Lymphedema tarda (c) → Primary lymphedema presenting after age 35.
  • Meigs’s syndrome (d) → Triad of ovarian fibroma, ascites, and pleural effusion, completely unrelated.
496. In a patient of tetanus with severe spasms and cyanosis, the first thing to be done is:
  • a) Thorough wound debridement and heavy dose of penicillin.
  • b) Human tetanus immunoglobulin.
  • c) Tracheostomy, muscle relaxant and ventilator.
  • d) Sedation and nasogastric suction.
Clinical Management:
  • Airway priority → Cyanosis and generalized spasms indicate respiratory arrest/laryngospasm. Establishing a secure airway via tracheostomy and initiating mechanical ventilation with muscle relaxants is the immediate life-saving action.
Exclusions:
  • Debridement/penicillin (a) & Tetanus IG (b) → Secondary to securing airway stability.
  • Sedation alone (d) → Ineffective at resolving life-threatening laryngospasm and severe diaphragmatic hypoxia.
497. All of the following are true about soft tissue sarcoma EXCEPT:
  • A. Most common site is trunk and retroperitoneum.
  • B. There are more than 11,000 new diagnoses of soft tissue sarcoma annually in the United States.
  • C. Most soft tissue sarcoma-specific deaths are due to uncontrolled pulmonary metastases.
  • D. The overall 5-year survival rate for all stages of soft tissue sarcoma approximates 50 to 60%.
Epidemiology:
  • Primary site → The most common site for soft tissue sarcoma is the extremities (~60% of cases, primarily lower extremity/thigh), not the trunk or retroperitoneum.
Exclusions:
  • Incidence (B) → >11,000 cases diagnosed annually in the US is accurate.
  • Metastasis (C) → Hematogenous spread to the lungs (pulmonary metastases) is the chief cause of mortality.
  • Survival (D) → 5-year survival rate across all localized/systemic stages averages ~50-60%.
498. Which of the following is NOT associated with the development of sarcoma?
  • A. Radiation exposure
  • B. Herbicide exposure
  • C. Chronic lymphedema
  • D. History of trauma
Etiology:
  • Trauma association → Prior physical injury does not cause sarcomas. Instead, local trauma often leads to diagnostic scans that incidentally discover a pre-existing asymptomatic sarcoma.
Exclusions:
  • Radiation (A) → Radiation-induced sarcomas are well-documented (e.g., angiosarcomas after breast conservation).
  • Herbicide (B) → Phenoxy herbicides/dioxins increase risk.
  • Lymphedema (C) → Long-standing lymphedema predisposes to lymphangiosarcoma (Stewart-Treves syndrome).
499. Which of the following is NOT part of the AJCC/UICC sarcoma staging system?
  • A. Tumor size
  • B. Number of mitoses per high-powered microscopic field
  • C. Lymph node metastatic status
  • D. Retroperitoneal sarcoma nomograms
Staging:
  • Nomograms → Retroperitoneal sarcoma nomograms are supplementary clinical survival calculators, not standardized parts of the AJCC/UICC staging system.
Exclusions:
  • Tumor size (A), Mitoses/Histologic Grade (B), and Lymph node status (C) → Essential, established variables in the AJCC staging criteria.
500. All of the following are known molecular pathogenic events in sarcoma EXCEPT:
  • A. Chromosomal translocations
  • B. Oncogene amplification
  • C. Complex genomic rearrangements
  • D. Epigenetic suppression
Pathophysiology:
  • Genetics → Although epigenetic changes occur in all cancers, "epigenetic suppression" is a broad regulatory mechanism and is not classified as a primary structural driver mutation compared to chromosomal events.
Exclusions:
  • Translocations (A) → Typical in round cell sarcomas (e.g., t(11;22) EWSR1-FLI1 in Ewing's).
  • Oncogene amplification (B) → Highly typical (e.g., MDM2 amplification in liposarcomas).
  • Rearrangements (C) → Complex, chaotic karyotypes characterize pleomorphic sarcomas.
501. For a T2G3NOMO sarcoma (stage II), treatment typically consists of:
  • A. Surgery alone
  • B. Surgery and radiotherapy
  • C. Surgery, radiotherapy, pre-surgical chemotherapy
  • D. Surgery, radiotherapy, pre-and postsurgical chemotherapy
Clinical Management:
  • Local control → Combined modality treatment with surgical resection (limb-sparing) and adjuvant radiation therapy is the standard for localized high-grade sarcomas.
Exclusions:
  • Surgery alone (A) → Carries high rate of local recurrence.
  • Chemotherapy (C, D) → Systematic chemotherapy is not standard for localized stage II lesions, reserved for specific chemosensitive subtypes or metastasized disease.
502. Which of the following is true about desmoid tumors?
  • A. Local recurrence is observed in up to one-third of patients regardless of microscopic margin of resection status.
  • B. A policy of watchful waiting for desmoids has been validated in prospective clinical trials.
  • C. Analogous to other nonmetastasizing tumors, chemotherapy has no role in the treatment of desmoid tumors.
  • D. Due to their propensity for local invasion, radiotherapy, when used, must be given at a dose of 75 Gy.
Diagnosis:
  • Aggressive behavior → Desmoid fibromatosis is locally destructive but does not metastasize. It exhibits exceptionally high recurrence rates (up to 30-40%) regardless of whether the surgical margins are microscopically positive or negative.
Exclusions:
  • Watchful waiting (B) → Frequently used, but the specific biological margin-independent recurrence risk is the classic established pathological fact.
  • Chemotherapy (C) → Chemotherapeutic/tyrosine kinase agents do have a clinical role in progressive or unresectable retroperitoneal desmoids.
  • Radiation (D) → Doses of 75 Gy would cause catastrophic soft-tissue necrosis; standard ranges are limited to 50-60 Gy.
503. Most common infection of the hand is:
  • a- Acute paronychia.
  • b- Web space infection.
  • c- Felon.
  • d- Palmar abscess.
Diagnosis:
  • Epidemiology → Acute paronychia (localized infection of the lateral or proximal nail fold, usually staphylococcal) is the most frequent hand infection.
Exclusions:
  • Web space (b), Felon (c), and Palmar abscess (d) → Occur significantly less frequently than simple superficial paronychia.
504. A felon is:
  • a- Mid palmar space infection.
  • b- Infection of subhyoid bursa.
  • c- Terminal pulp space infection.
  • d- Ulnar bursa infection.
Diagnosis:
  • Closed compartments → A felon is a purulent abscess of the closed terminal pulp space of the distal phalanx. It can cause ischemic necrosis and osteomyelitis due to high compartmental pressure.
Exclusions:
  • Mid-palmar (a) & Ulnar bursa (d) → Deep spaces of the palm and wrist, not the distal phalanges.
  • Subhyoid bursa (b) → Located in the anterior neck, completely unrelated.
505. Infection of the tendon sheath of the fifth finger tends to spread ready to the:
  • a- Fourth digital sheath.
  • b- Midpalmar space.
  • c- Fourth web space.
  • d- Ulnar bursa.
Anatomy:
  • Bursal communication → The flexor sheath of the fifth finger is continuous with the ulnar bursa in up to 80% of individuals, allowing rapid proximal spread of tenosynovitis to the wrist.
Exclusions:
  • Digital sheaths (a, c) → Sheaths of index, middle, and ring fingers are usually blind-ended in the distal palm.
  • Midpalmar space (b) → A deep fascial space, not a direct synovial sheath continuation.
506. In the hand, infection of the mid palmar space is most often due to:
  • a- Deep punctured wounds of the palm.
  • b- Direct spread from intrathecal whitlows.
  • c- Local spread from ulnar bursa or thenar space.
  • d- Lymphatic spread from superficial infections.
Pathophysiology:
  • Synovial rupture → Midpalmar space infection most frequently occurs secondary to rupture of untreated acute flexor tenosynovitis (intrathecal whitlow) of the middle or ring fingers.
Exclusions:
  • Puncture wounds (a) → Can cause it, but represent a less frequent etiology than sheath rupture.
  • Bursal/thenar spread (c) → Strongly restricted by the thick, tough midpalmar septum.
  • Lymphatic (d) → Lymphatic drainage goes dorsally, rarely causing deep compartment infections.
507. Ballooning of the thenar eminence with abduction of the thumb and marked oedema of the dorsum of the hand is diagnostic of:
  • a- Subcutaneous whitlow of the thenar eminence.
  • b- Intrathecal whitlow of the thumb.
