  • c- Radial bursitis.
  • d- Thenar space infection.
Diagnosis:
  • Thenar space infection → Classically presents as dramatic swelling ("ballooning") over the thenar muscle group, forcing the thumb into abduction and causing extensive dorsal sympathetic edema.
Exclusions:
  • Subcutaneous whitlow (a) → Localized superficial skin pustule.
  • Intrathecal whitlow of thumb (b) & Radial bursitis (c) → Tenderness is focused strictly along the FPL tendon sheath pathway.
508. Kanavel’s sign is:
  • a. Swelling above the flexor retinaculum.
  • b. Flexion of the thumb when the radial bursa is infected.
  • c. Flexion of the fingers in a compound palmar ganglion.
  • d. Tenderness over an infected ulnar bursa between the transverse palmar creases.
Diagnosis:
  • Ulnar bursa sign → In ulnar bursitis, exquisite focal tenderness is elicited over the path of the bursa in the palm, particularly located where the proximal and distal transverse palmar creases intersect.
Exclusions:
  • Swelling (a) → A non-specific, late feature of advanced forearm extension.
  • Thumb flexion (b) → Non-diagnostic for ulnar bursa.
  • Finger flexion (c) → Typically associated with chronic tuberculous or rheumatoid tenosynovitis.
509. The space of Parona is:
  • a. In the wrist between the deep flexor tendons and the pronator quadrates.
  • b. Above the patella between the quadriceps muscle and the femur.
  • c. Beneath the tendon of the iliopsoas.
  • d. Between the Achilles tendon and the posterior aspect of the tibia.
Anatomy:
  • Potential space → The space of Parona is a deep potential fascial space in the distal forearm, situated anteriorly to the pronator quadratus and deep to the flexor digitorum profundus tendons.
Exclusions:
  • Choices B, C, D → Describe distinct anatomical areas located within the lower extremities.
510. The ‹frog hand› is seen in:
  • a. Acute fulminating tenosynovitis.
  • b. Deep palmar abscess.
  • c. Infection of proximal volar space.
  • d. None of the above.
Diagnosis:
  • Frog Hand deformity → Severe deep palmar abscesses lead to massive swelling of both palmar and dorsal aspects of the hand, forcing fingers into a semi-flexed, widely abducted stance resembling a frog's foot.
Exclusions:
  • Tenosynovitis (a) → Results in localized symmetrical swelling of a single digit.
  • Proximal volar space (c) → Produces forearm swelling, not the characteristic semi-flexed frog-like digits.
511. For vascular injuries to the hand requiring tourniquet, the maximum time the tourniquet should be applied to prevent tissue necrosis is:
  • A. 1 hour
  • B. 2 hours
  • C. 3 hours
  • D. 4 hours
Clinical Guidelines:
  • Ischemic limit → Safe pneumatic tourniquet inflation time for upper extremities is capped at 2 hours to prevent irreversible skeletal muscle necrosis and tourniquet-induced palsy.
Exclusions:
  • 1 hour (A) → Safe but well below the physiological tolerance threshold.
  • 3 hours (C) & 4 hours (D) → Significantly increase risks of ischemic necrosis, tissue acidosis, and compartment syndrome.
512. Anesthetic agents with epinephrine should NOT be used in:
  • A. The fingertip
  • B. The hand
  • C. The wrist
  • D. The forearm
Pharmacology:
  • Ischemic necrosis risk → Epinephrine causes severe localized vasoconstriction. It is contraindicated in end-arterial structures like the fingertips to prevent digital ischemia and gangrene.
Exclusions:
  • Hand (B), Wrist (C), and Forearm (D) → Possess abundant collateral circulation, minimizing ischemia risk.
513. Most nondisplaced fractures do NOT require surgical treatment EXCEPT:
  • A. Those of the lunate bone of the wrist.
  • B. Those of the capitate bone of the wrist.
  • C. Those of the scaphoid bone of the wrist.
  • D. All nondisplaced fractures require surgical treatment.
Orthopedic Surgery:
  • Scaphoid nonunion risk → Nondisplaced scaphoid fractures are prone to avascular necrosis and nonunion due to retrograde blood supply, often necessitating early percutaneous screw fixation or highly prolonged casting.
Exclusions:
  • Lunate (A) & Capitate (B) → Typically treated conservatively with short-term splinting.
514. Phalen›s and reversed phalen›s test are diagnostic in:
  • A. Carpal tunnel syndrome.
  • B. Neuroma.
  • C. Malunion of the scaphoid bone.
  • D. Trigger finger.
Diagnosis:
  • Median nerve compression → Phalen's (wrist flexion) and reversed Phalen's (wrist extension) tests elevate carpal tunnel pressures, reproducing median nerve paresthesias.
Exclusions:
  • Neuroma (B) → Elicits sharp pain via direct percussion (Tinel's sign).
  • Scaphoid malunion (C) & Trigger finger (D) → Structural/mechanical hand conditions, not neuro-compressive.
515. A patient shown to have wasting at the interdigital web spaces, experiences numbness of the ring finger and exhibits Wartenberg sign on physical examination most likely is suffering from:
  • A. Cubital tunnel syndrome
  • B. Carpal tunnel syndrome
  • C. Pronator syndrome
  • D. Anterior interosseous nerve syndrome
Diagnosis:
  • Ulnar nerve compression → Wasting of interosseous muscles (web spaces), small finger abduction (Wartenberg's sign), and ring/small finger numbness indicate ulnar nerve entrapment at the elbow (Cubital Tunnel Syndrome).
Exclusions:
  • Carpal tunnel (B) & Pronator (C) → Affect median nerve distribution (radial 3.5 digits).
  • AIN syndrome (D) → Purely motor nerve dysfunction; does not present with numbness.
516. The most common primary malignant tumor of the hand is:
  • A. Melanoma
  • B. Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC)
  • C. Basal cell carcinoma
  • D. Epithelioid sarcoma
Oncology:
  • Epidemiology → Squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) is the most common primary malignant tumor of the hand, usually arising on the sun-exposed dorsal hand surface in elderly patients.
Exclusions:
  • Melanoma (A) & Basal cell (C) → Less common on hand skin compared to SCC.
  • Epithelioid sarcoma (D) → Most common hand sarcoma in young adults, but far less common overall than SCC.
517. Necrotizing infections:
  • A. Often present with pain out of proportion to findings.
  • B. Often have discharge present.
  • C. Debridement should begin following confirmation by way of radiograph findings.
  • D. Oral antibiotics should begin immediately.
Diagnosis:
  • Clinical hallmark → A vital early sign of necrotizing fasciitis is extreme, severe pain that is completely out of proportion to minimal superficial cutaneous changes.
Exclusions:
  • Discharge (B) → Typically absent in early stages.
  • Radiograph delay (C) → Surgical debridement is a surgical emergency; waiting for imaging is contraindicated.
  • Oral drugs (D) → Demands immediate high-dose intravenous antibiotics and surgical intervention.
518. The majority of acute cases of infections flexor tenosynovitis (FTS) are due to:
  • A. Systemic lupus erythematosus
  • B. Chronic inflammation as a result of diabetes
  • C. Rheumatoid arthritis (RA)
  • D. Purulent infection
Pathophysiology:
  • Infectious etiology → The vast majority of acute presentations are purulent (infectious) in nature, typically resulting from penetrating trauma introducing bacteria like S. aureus into the sheath.
Exclusions:
  • Lupus (A), Diabetes (B), and RA (C) → Cause non-infectious, chronic inflammatory tenosynovitis, which rarely presents as acute surgical emergencies.
519. The most common soft tissue tumor of the wrist is:
  • A. Mucous cyst
  • B. Ganglion cyst
  • C. Lipoma
  • D. Schwannoma
Diagnosis:
  • Wrist masses → Ganglion cysts are benign mucin-filled lesions that represent ~70% of all soft tissue masses of the wrist/hand.
Exclusions:
  • Mucous cyst (A) → Specific type of ganglion located strictly over the DIP joint.
  • Lipoma (C) & Schwannoma (D) → Benign solid neoplasms, significantly less common than cysts.
520. Ganglion cyst is most commonly located at:
  • A. Anterior aspect of the wrist.
  • B. Posterior aspect of the wrist.
  • C. Dorsum of the hand.
  • D. Palmar surface of the hand.
Diagnosis:
  • Dorsal frequency → ~60-70% of wrist ganglions are dorsal (posterior), typically originating from the scapholunate ligament joint capsule.
Exclusions:
  • Anterior wrist (A) → Volar ganglions represent only ~30% of cases.
  • Dorsum of hand (C) & Palmar hand (D) → Much rarer compared to direct wrist joint origins.
521. All hand infections EXCEPT the following require surgical management:
  • A. Paronychia
  • B. Cellulitis
  • C. Felon
  • D. Osteomyelitis
Clinical Management:
  • Medical therapy → Cellulitis is a spreading superficial infection without loculated abscess or tissue necrosis. It is treated medically with systemic antibiotics.
Exclusions:
  • Paronychia (A), Felon (C), and Osteomyelitis (D) → Standardly require surgical drainage or debridement to prevent ischemic digit damage.
522. Which of the following patient groups has a 1000-fold increased risk of developing squamous cell carcinoma?
  • A. Transplant patients on immunosuppression
  • B. Patients with xeroderma pigmentosa
  • C. Patients with actinic keratosis
  • D. Patients exposed to inorganic arsenic
Genetics:
  • UV damage repair defect → Xeroderma pigmentosa is an autosomal recessive deficiency in nucleotide excision repair. This leads to an extreme susceptibility to skin cancers (SCC, BCC) following UV exposure.
Exclusions:
  • Transplant (A) → Carries a ~65-100x elevated risk.
  • Actinic keratosis (C) & Arsenic (D) → Do not reach the genetic 1000-fold scale of risk.
523. Which of the following about enchondromas is true?
  • A. Have never been reported in the trapezoid.
  • B. Discovery is often prompted by patients presenting with hand pain.
  • C. Are the most common malignant primary bone tumors.
  • D. The most common location is the middle phalanges.
Diagnosis:
  • Carpal rarity → Enchondromas are highly common in the phalanges and metacarpals, but carpal bone enchondromas are extremely rare. No cases have been reported in the trapezoid bone.
Exclusions:
  • Pain (B) → Typically asymptomatic, discovered incidentally on radiographs for unrelated trauma.
  • Malignancy (C) → Entirely benign cartilaginous bone lesions.
  • Location (D) → Proximal phalanx is the most common site in the hand.
524. Proper handling of a traumatically amputated digit includes which of the following?
  • A. Place dry in a waterproof bag.
  • B. Immerse in an antiseptic solution.
  • C. Prep and wrapped in moistened gauze.
  • D. Place on dry ice.
Clinical Guidelines:
  • Tissue preservation → To optimize replantation, the amputated digit should be wrapped in saline-moistened gauze, sealed in a dry plastic bag, and then placed in an ice-water slurry.
Exclusions:
  • Dry (A) → Direct un-moistened bagging causes tissue desiccation.
  • Antiseptic (B) → Chemical solutions cause severe cellular toxicity.
  • Dry ice (D) → Causes freezing injury (frostbite), rendering the tissue unviable for replant.
525. Dupuytren’s contracture is best treated by:
  • A. Surgery.
  • B. Steroids.
  • C. Splinting.
  • D. Physiotherapy.
Clinical Management:
  • Definitive correction → Significant contracture (MCP flexion >30° or any PIP contracture) is managed primarily via surgical fasciectomy to excise diseased palmar fascia cords.
Exclusions:
  • Steroids (B) → Do not regress organized collagen cords.
  • Splinting (C) & Physio (D) → Ineffective at correcting structural palmar myofibroblast shortening.
526. What is the most common hand anomaly?
  • A. Syndactyly.
  • B. Club hand.
  • C. Polydactyly.
  • D. Constriction band.
Pediatric Orthopedics:
  • Congenital syndactyly → Webbing or fusion of adjacent fingers is widely recognized as the most common congenital anomaly of the hand (~1 in 2000 births).
Exclusions:
  • Club hand (B), Polydactyly (C), and Constriction band (D) → Significantly lower global incidence rates than syndactyly.
527. Drainage of distal pulp space infection is done through:
  • A. Bilateral incision of each side of the finger.
  • B. Incise the nail fold.
  • C. Transverse incision on the fingertip.
  • D. Short longitudinal skin incision on area of maximum fluctuance not crossing the interphalangeal joint crease.
Surgical Technique:
  • Felon drainage → Best performed with a brief longitudinal incision directly over the area of greatest fluctuance, ensuring the IP joint crease is not crossed to avoid contracture or scar pain.
Exclusions:
  • Bilateral fishmouth (A) → Obsolete, destroys critical septa and digital nerves, causing tip necrosis.
  • Nail fold incision (B) → Indicated for paronychia, not pulp infections.
  • Transverse incision (C) → Cuts across digital neurovascular structures.
528. Stenosing tenosynovitis (trigger finger) is best treated initially by:
  • A. Early surgery.
  • B. Physiotherapy.
  • C. Corticosteroid injection.
  • D. None of the above.
Clinical Management:
  • Initial treatment → Local corticosteroid injection into the flexor tendon sheath at the A1 pulley offers a highly successful, non-operative first-line response.
Exclusions:
  • Surgery (A) → Reserved only for cases that fail corticosteroid injections.
  • Physiotherapy (B) → Minimal therapeutic value in reversing mechanical stenotic changes.
529. De Quervain Tenosynovitis presents with pain at the:
  • A. Radial aspect of the wrist.
  • B. Dorsal aspect of the wrist.
  • C. Volar aspect of the wrist.
  • D. Ulnar aspect of the wrist.
Diagnosis:
  • First dorsal compartment → Affects the APL and EPB tendons over the radial styloid process, causing pain focused entirely on the radial aspect of the wrist.
Exclusions:
  • Dorsal (B), Volar (C), and Ulnar (D) → Pain points away from the first dorsal compartment pathology.
530. What is the most common tumour of the hand?
  • A. Fibroma.
  • B. Enchondroma.
  • C. Angioma.
  • D. Osteoclastoma.
Oncology:
  • Primary bone neoplasm → Enchondromas are the most common primary bone tumors of the hand (~90% of hand bone lesions).
Exclusions:
  • Fibroma (A), Angioma (C), and Osteoclastoma (D) → Occur with significantly lower clinical frequencies in the hand.
